Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Live-stock Slaughter Levy Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 82 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-stock Slaughter Levy Act 1964.
Loan ACt(No. 2) 1982 (Act No. 107 of 1982)An Act to authorize the borrowing and expending of moneys for defence purposes.
National Crimes Commission Act 1982 (Act No. 138 of 1982)An Act to establish a National Crimes Commission to investigate criminal activities, in particular organized criminal activities, with a view to the prosecution of offenders.
Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.
Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 128 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Pay-roll Tax (Territories) Assessment Act 1971.
Public Service Acts Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. III of 1982)- An Act relating to the Australian Public Service.
Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 840f 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No. I) 1930.
Sales Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 85 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Sales Tax Act (No.2) 1930.

Wednesday, 13 October 1982
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS
Mr SPEAKER-I indicate to the House that I have been forewarned that the Leader of the Opposition wishes to raise a matter of privilege which I would normal1y cal1 first. But, in the special circumstances, I will cal1 the Acting Prime Minister first to announce ministerial arrangements.
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Acting Prime Minister)-I inform the House that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) left Australia this morning to attend the Commonwealth Heads of Government Regional Meeting in Fiji. I will act as Prime Minister until the Prime Minister returns on Monday.
PRIVILEGE
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-Mr Speaker, I wish to raise with you a matter of privilege. It relates to an article which appeared in the Australian newspaper of today's date under the heading 'ALP attack on tax avoidance suffers setback'. The article is written by Brian Hill, Pam Bornhorst and Wio Joustra. The article states:
Government sources said the public servant's name had been deleted from a sheaf of documents tabled yesterday in the House of Representatives by the Leader of the Opposition, Mr Hayden.
That statement is untrue. I would suggest that it is not only deliberately untrue but also maliciously untrue. The facts of this matter were, in fact, given to Mr Brian Hill of the Australian, who was apparently one of the joint authors of this article, when he came to my office.
Yesterday I tabled a number of documents from company records which showed certainly Mr Moore, a solicitor-the person referred to in this article-as a director of those companies at the time of the transaction which led to a process of their being dumped in the bottom of the harbour. Those documents were presented in exactly the way in which they had been photocopied from the official records. In each case there was a covering sheet of some few pages which listed, for the sake of condensation, directors and secretaries at the time of sale to dealer. Those sheets were typed in my office as a matter of convenience for people who wanted to refer to these tabled documents. Those typed sheets did not include the name of Mr Moore. They did not include the name of Mr Moore for this very good reason:

There were other documents which I did not table or refer to yesterday because I did not have sufficient time to attend to many other matters I would have wished to attend to in the course of my address to this House, including this matter; so I did not tender those documents. But other company documents show for the record that Mr Moore, for several of the companies-at least al1 those for which we could obtain relevant documents-had retired as a director of each of those companies on 31 May 1977, that is, before the transaction was put in place. For the benefit of the House, I seek leave to have those relevant copies tabled at this point.
Leave granted.
Mr HAYDEN-The documents subsequently filed at the Companies Office were in error in maintaining the description of Mr Moore as a director at the time of the transactions of 28 June 1977. Those documents, copied exactly, were presented with that error included. The typed covering documents which I referred to, outlining the directors and secretaries at the time of sale to dealer, did not include Mr Moore for the very proper reason that relevant documents showed he had resigned as a director on 31 May and therefore his person was not relevant to the matter before the House. It has been declared by Mr Moore and referred to in a number of newspaper comments this morning that in fact he had retired. He declared that himself.
Mr Brian Hill came to my office after the debate had taken place yesterday, inquiring about this matter, mentioning that it had been drawn to his attention-I presume by sources close to the Government if not of the Government -and the facts were explained as I have just explained them. One must conclude that Mr Hill and/or his col1eagues decided, nonetheless, to embark upon a misleading and, in the circumstances, wilfully misleading statement which has the clear implication that I sought to doctor documents in a quite unethical, dishonest way and therefore to mislead the Parliament. Mr Speaker, in those circumstances, I ask that you take the matter into consideration and determine whether it should be referred to the appropriate committee as a matter of privilege.
Mr SPEAKER--I ask the honourable gentleman to make the papers available to the Clerk. I will have the transcript from Hansard as soon as possible after I leave the chair. I will then consider the matter and see whether there is a prima facie case of breach of privilege.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
TAXATION: IMPORTED AIRCRAFT
Mr CUNNINGHAM-In addressing my question to the Treasurer I refer him to a matter I raised in this Parliament on 19 August in regard to the importation of aircraft for taxation purposes. I referred at that time to a company which was headed by a former Minister. Is the Treasurer aware that that former Minister, Mr Staley, has asked the Victoria Police to investigate serious allegations in regard to aircraft and possible fraud against Commonwealth revenue? Is it a fact that the aircraft involved is a 1124 Westwind aircraft imported from Israel? Has the Treasurer, or the Prime Minister, called for a full report on these allegations from the Fraud Squad? Is it a fact that the Government's interest in this matter has been activated by the implication of a prominent businessman who sits on government bodies? Is it also a fact that as a result of information given to police in respect of this tax racket a prominent Liberal, Sir Andrew Grimwade, has resigned from all government appointments?
Mr HOWARD-The honourable gentleman's question makes a number of assumptions and allegations upon which I will not offer any comment.

I can inform the honourable gentleman that I am aware that there has been some contact between the former Minister whom he named and sections of the Victorian police force. I am not aware of the total substance of that contact. If there is a responsibility at a police level in the matter it is a responsibility of the Victorian police force and not a responsibility, as I understand the matter, of the Commonwealth police force. So far as the direct taxation affairs of that company are concerned, I can only confirm what I have told the honourable gentleman earlier, namely, that section 16 would preclude the Commissioner of Taxation from disclosing to me, or indeed anybody else, without the authority of the company, the taxation affairs of that company. Finally, I can inform the honourable gentleman that I have sought from the Australian Taxation Office advice as to whether any areas of the taxation law that might be touched by the circumstances to which the honourable gentleman's earlier question referred might be in need of amendment. In the event of receiving advice from the Taxation Commissioner that it should be amended and if I think it is appropriate to do so-and only if I think it is appropriate to do so-I will recommend to the Government accordingly.
URANIUM ENRICHMENT
Mr GILES-I address my question to the Minister for Trade and Resources. Is it correct that the Government has announced the acceptance of certain recommendations for the enrichment of uranium in Australia? What economic benefits could be expected to flow to the nation from the establishment of an enrichment industry, preferably in South Australia? Are there any significant health or environmental risks associated with such an industry?
Mr ANTHONY-The Government has received a report from the Uranium Enrichment Group of Australia which consists of four Australian companies which have been doing an evaluation on this question. That report made certain recommendations which the Government has accepted. The recommendations are that the Government now accept the Urenco-Centec centrifuge system of enrichment as the basis of further feasibility studies and that the two likely sites for an enrichment plant in Australia, near Brisbane or Adelaide, be further evaluated. The proposal would involve an expenditure of something like $500m in the initial stages, giving employment to about a couple of thousand people during the construction stage and probably permanent employment to about 1,000 people.

1920 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Questions without Notice

Mr Uren-What about your statement about 500,000 jobs?
Mr ANTHONY-I note an interjection about 500,000 people being employed. I think that the House ought to put it on record once again quite clearly that it was the Dunstan Government in South Australia in 1976 which initiated a study of uranium enrichment. In that initial document it was stated that an enrichment industry could provide jobs for about 8,000 people but that if we had a totally integrated uranium industry in Australia it could provide both direct and indirect benefits to something like half a million people. A Labor Premier of South Australia said that. It shows us how contrary the Labor Party is when it will not accept the advice of even one of its principal father figures. The environmental hazards related to an enrichment plant are no greater than the normal environmental problems for light industry. Indeed, an enrichment plant would be no more hazardous than a chemical plant being constructed somewhere in Australia. Of course, there will have to be the necessary safeguard arrangements to ensure that there is protection. But from our knowledge of overseas enrichment plants the problems are small and very few hazards are involved.
Mr Scott-You obviously do not know anything about it.
Mr ANTHONY-We know the attitude of members of the Australian Labor Party. They have this mania about uranium. They are in total conflict with one another as to how to proceed. But the facts are that Australia now has a uranium industry operating successfully, exporting and earning income. Now we are looking at the beneficiation of that uranium so that it can bring to Australia added benefits and a lot more job opportunities. At a time when there are job difficulties, members of the Labor Party who go on in the cantankerous way in which they do deserve to be condemned.
SALES TAX EVASION
Mr WILLlS-I refer the Treasurer to recent publicity given to widespread sales tax evasion through so-called straw company schemes which are similar in operation to the notorious bottomof-the-harbour scheme. Is it a fact that the Commissioner of Taxation repeatedly warned the Government, particularly the Treasurer, of the need to take action against such schemes between 1978 and 1980? Is it also a fact that when the Crimes (Taxation Offences) Act was eventually enacted in 1980 against bottom-of-the-harbour schemes it applied equally to schemes to evade sales tax because of the inadequacy of previous

tax legislation to prevent such abuses? Why then has the Government not included in the recoupment taxation legislation currently before the House measures to recoup evaded sales tax to ensure that persons participating in such schemes do not simply walk away with their ill-gotten gains?
Mr HOWARD-I can inform the honourable gentleman that the legislation in respect of sales tax matters that was passed in 1978, together with legislation subsequently passed, is still effective in relation to these matters in the view of the Commissioner of Taxation. I can also inform the honourable gentleman that, as I think I indicated in a statement a couple of weeks ago, certain people have been arrested and charged in respect of sales tax evasion arrangements. I can also say that the advice given to me by the Commissioner of Taxation to enact remedial legislation in respect of sales tax evasion schemes during the time that I have been Treasurer has been acted upon by the Government. It is accepted as being good advice which should be acted upon, and it has been promptly put into legislative form by the Government.
I can only repeat that the honourable gentleman should refer again to the statements that I made after the ill-fated allegations made in the New South Wales Parliament by a Labor member to the effect that the Commissioner of Taxation was of the view that existing law was adequate to deal with the sales tax evasion schemes named by that member and presumably the allegations to which the honourable gentleman refers. The Commissioner of Taxation does not accept that the revenue in question has been lost. The Commissioner of Taxation remains confident in the view that the existing law is adequate to deal with the problem. In that view he is substantially supported by the independent opinions of two Melbourne counsel, including a Queen's Counsel.
SHEEP SLAUGHTER BOUNTY
Mr BOURCHIER-Does the Minister for Primary Industry agree that there is an urgent need to introduce a sheep slaughter bounty in Victoria? Is the Minister aware that the State Government of Victoria will not provide this bounty? In view of this decision, will the Minister now take steps to introduce this bounty as a direct action by the Federal Government? Does the Minister agree that the Federal Government could provide this bounty through local government sources? If the Federal Government provides the bounty direct, will the Minister indicate whether the amount that should be provided by the Victorian Government as its share will be deducted from Federal

Questions without Notice

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1921

Government funds supplied through tax sharing arrangements?
Mr NIXON-Because of the seriousness of the drought throughout Australia, the Commonwealth Government has introduced a number of important measures, including a fodder subsidy, a freight subsidy for the transfer of sheep or cattle from place to place and a later announced slaughter subsidy, to try to alleviate the situation. The fodder subsidy is being directly funded by the Commonwealth. The freight subsidy is one of what we call the core measures that are being shared with the States. What I did was to announce that the Commonwealth was prepared to share with the States a slaughter subsidy of $3 a head as part of the core measures, expecting the States to pick that up as they had done with the other core measures. The whole package is a fairly complete one to try to overcome some very difficult situations that farmers and graziers throughout the nation are facing in terms of looking after their livestock.
Insofar as the slaughter subsidy is concerned, I regret to say that Victoria has not picked it up. It has chosen in some way to assist local government authorities, with the use of bulldozers, to dig pits for the burying of dead sheep. I think it has been prepared to finance one or two aspects of that need in some small way. That, of course, does not help the farmer or the grazier with his losses. I think it is regrettable that the Victorian Government has not picked up the slaughter subsidy. I will try to encourage it to do so. I think it is adopting a bit of a mean attitude on this aspect. There is no help for the farmer or grazier in that respect in the line that it has taken.
The honourable member for Bendigo asked whether the Commonwealth would pick up the subsidy and provide it as a separate measure as we have done with the fodder subsidy and the interest rate subsidy. Because I am dealing with six States in these matters, I would prefer to leave the subsidy as part of the core measures. Other States are prepared to pick these schemes up and I would not, therefore, like to remove this subsidy from the core measures. We would have administrative difficulties and we would have to set up some form of bureaucracy to deal with it, whereas the States have a ready-made system for dealing with it in all its details. I, therefore, would not be attracted to the idea of doing it separately. Rather, I hope that the honourable member will encourage his State colleagues to make representations in Melbourne to try to encourage the Victorian Government to see some sense on the issue.

PECUNIARY INTERESTS OF MINISTERS
Mr DAWKINS-I refer the Acting Prime Minister to my question of 10 September 1980 to the then Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, who was at that time just plain Mr Garland, in answer to which the then Minister stated that he had disclosed his business interests to the Prime Minister as he said was required by stated Government policy. I ask the Acting Prime Minister whether he will ascertain when the Prime Minister was informed that the then Minister was a shareholder in Automotive Holdings Ltd, a company which disposed of at least 13 of its subsidiaries to the Maher organisation in March 1976, which subsidiaries finished their lives at the bottom of the harbour with, as their company secretary, a woman named in the Costigan report as a brothel keeper.
Mr ANTHONY-Questions of that nature hardly deserve answering in this House. I would have thought some of the name dropping of the honourable member would have been of sufficient embarrassment to him to encourage him not to keep on this trend. As far as Sir Victor Garland is concerned, no doubt he has revealed his interests to the Prime Minister and the Prime Minister will be in a position to answer the question if he feels he needs to answer it. If people want casually to drop names, as members of the Australian Labor Party are doing, to smear people, certainly the Government will not react every time they drop a name in this Parliament. Honourable members can rest assured of this. If there is some prima facie evidence that action should be taken, the Government will do so, but I am sure that, up to this point, Sir Victor Garland has revealed to the Prime Minister all of his interests.
INTEREST RATES
Dr EDWARDS-Will the Treasurer provide information to the House about recent movements in key interest rates both in Australia and overseas?
Mr HOWARD-I am sure that all members of the House will have noted with some satisfaction movements which have occurred in key official and private sector interest rates around the world over the past few months. To name but some of them, between May 1981 and October 1982 the prime interest rate charged to large borrowers by United States of America banks fell from 20.25 per cent to around 12 per cent or 13 per cent--
Mr Mountford-What about Australia?
Mr HOWARD-I am coming to Australia. I have some good news on that front, too. This morning three of the largest banks in the United

1922 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Questions without Notice

States announced a further fall of one per cent in their prime rate. I think it ought to be remarked that one of the major reasons why these falls in interest rates in the United States have occurred-certainly not the only reason but one of the major reasons-is that there has been a quite spectacular fall in the rate of inflation in the United States. This rather endorses and underlines the importance that ought to continue to be attached to the level of inflation so far as an improvement in economic indicators is concerned. Since August 1982 there has been a fall of about 2t per cent in bond rates in Australia. There have already been the beginnings of some falls in prime overdr:.ft rates charged by Australian banks to large borrowers. They have declined from 17t per cent to 17 per cent between April 1982 and October 1982.
I think I have said before in the House that I believe that the new system of selling Commonwealth Government bonds has made a major contribution to the bond selling task and could in part have played a role in the decline which has occurred in interest rates. Looking around the industrialised world, although the falls in interest rates are not as marked as they have been in the United States, except perhaps in respect of the United Kingdom, where the prime rate went down from 16.5 per cent to 11 per cent between October 1981 and October 1982, there is still a pattern of some declines in interest rates in the case of West Germany, Italy, the Netherlands, Switzerland and Belgium. It is perhaps not surprising that one of the exceptions to this rule is that, so far as I understand it, there have not been any significant falls in interest rates in France during the same period. Perhaps there is some connection there between budgetary policies and interest rate developments.
Whilst we should not read too much into the developments that have occurred in interest rates and whilst we should recognise that as well as the falls in inflation there has been a significant contribution from the decline in demand for funds through lower corporate sector activity, the development in the United States is, nonetheless, very healthy. As this Government has repeatedly said in the past, the fortunes economically of this country are linked inevitably with the fortunes economically of the rest of the world. As a consequence, it is to be hoped that, as has already occurred, the declines in interest rates in the United States will have some beneficial effects in Australia. They have already been felt. Those that have been experienced ought to be a cause of satisfaction to all members of the House.

TAX EVASION LEGISLATION
Dr JENKINS-I refer the Treasurer to the proposed legislation against bottom-of-theharbour tax evasion schemes. Does this legislation seek to recoup only company and undistributed profits taxes owed by companies which were involved in these schemes? Is it a fact that persons participating in these schemes were able to avoid paying personal income tax in respect of payments for their shares which, had they been distributed as dividends, would have been taxable at the shareholders' marginal tax rate? Is the total revenue lost from this component of bottom-of the-harbour schemes similar in magnitude to that tax which the Government is attempting to recoup? Why has the Government limited the scope of this legislation so that as much as half the total tax loss will remain uncollected?
Mr HOWARD-In answer to the honourable gentleman's question I make three comments: Firstly, the legislation is concerned with the collection of tax which was assessed and remains unpaid, although it is the intention of the Government under the legislation, if it is approved by the Parliament, to collect that tax which remains unpaid from the vendor shareholders of the companies concerned. The second point I make is that the alleged non-payment of personal tax to which the honourable gentleman referred is in respect of a tax that was never actually assessed. In those circumstances, there is a significant difference between the tax which is sought to be collected under this legislation and that which in fact might have been collected having regard to what might have happened to the handling of the companies in question.
Thirdly, the honourable gentleman's question, if I may say so, totally ignores the implications of the judgment of the High Court of Australia in Slutzkin's case, which held that section 260 of the Income Tax Assessment Act would not operate in those circumstances to impose a taxation liability on the vendor shareholders. I take the opportunity of informing the honourable gentleman that the Government does not propose to widen the reach of the recoupment legislation. There are good and proper reasons why the reach of that recoupment legislation should not be widened and I will have very great pleasure during debate on this legislation to point out in great detail to the honourable gentleman and his colleagues why the reach of that legislation should not be widened.
QANTAS AIRWAYS LTD: FLIGHTS TO ZIMBABWE
Mr TAMBLING-Can the Minister for Aviation provide details of new arrangements

Questions without Notice

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1923

with regard to international flights to Zimbabwe by Qantas Airways Ltd? What commercial negotiations have been finalised between Qantas and South African Airlines to guarantee through travel to South Africa? Is it a fact that as a consequence South African Airlines will upgrade its services between Johannesburg and Harare from using DC9 aircraft to using Airbus aircraft in order to accommodate the additional travellers and will alter timetables and fare costs considerably?
Mr FIFE-For foreign policy reasons which are well understood in this country and particularly in this House, the Commonwealth Government will not grant approval to Qantas to fly direct to South Africa. However, in recent times Qantas has made application to the Government for approval to fly direct to Zimbabwe and it has indicated publicly that it has been in consultation with the Zimbabwean Government with a view to establishing a service from Sydney via Perth to the Zimbabwean capital, commencing on 7 November. Of course, this service requires the approval of both governments. The Australian Government has given approval for that service and Qantas is still awaiting the approval of the Zimbabwean Government. I do not want to anticipate the Zimbabwean Government's attitude but I believe that it probably would favour this air link and probably would provide the necessary approval. But, as I have indicated, this is a matter for both governments. The Australian Government has provided approval and it is now a matter for the Zimbabwean Government to indicate its position.
I am not aware of the details of any commercial negotiations that have been entered into between Qantas and South African Airlines, but I point out that any air service between Zimbabwe and any other country, be it in Africa or elsewhere, is a matter for the Zimbabwean Government. I reiterate that the Australian Government has given approval for Qantas to fly to Zimbabwe. I understand that, subject to the approval of the Zimbabwean Government, the service will commence on 7 November.
MEAT INDUSTRY
Mr KERIN-I refer the Minister for Primary Industry to the 409 pages of notes by Mr Landos, secretary of the Kelly Committee of Inquiry to Examine Commonwealth and State Meat Inspection Systems, which were tabled on 23 September and which noted many examples of corruption and malpractice in the meat industry. When did the Minister first learn of the Landos notes? Was

Mr Landos a member of the Department of Primary Industry when secretary of the Kelly Committee? Has Mr Landos been a member of the Department of Primary Industry since the completion of the Kelly Committee's report? If so, in what capacity and in what area of the Department is Mr Landos employed? If the Minister was not aware of the allegations contained in the .Landos notes at the time they were compiled, how was it that crucial information could be available to the Department but not to the Minister in charge of that Department? Did the Bureau of Animal Health take any action on the Landos notes? If not, why not?
Mr NIXON-The honourable member for Werriwa has asked a series of questions. I will try to deal with them one after the other. I first became aware of the Landos notes on 13 July 1982 when I was in attendance at the hearings of the Royal Commission into the Australian Meat Industry as a witness. I think the second question related to what role Mr Landos has in the Department. He was appointed as an officer of the Department as secretary of the Kelly Committee. He returned to the Department in the role that he held previously relating to the export inspection service. I have written down here 'yes' to the third question. I do not remember what the question was, quite frankly.
Mr Kerin-What is he doing now?
Mr NIXON-He is engaged in the same role as he was previously, prior to his appointment as secretary of the Kelly Committee. The Landos notes were not made available to me. I made that clear previously in the House in the debate on the matter. It is a tragedy that they were not made available. Mr Kelly had a copy of the Landos notes. He did not bother to refer them to me as Minister on the day of our meeting in Melbourne, nor did he see that I obtained a copy of the notes afterwards.
Mr Mountford-What is wrong with your departmental head?
Mr NIXON-Mr Speaker, I will do my best to answer this question if honourable members would just be patient for a moment. The relationship between Mr Landos and the Kelly Committee was as it is with all parliamentary committees-I think most members of parliament would recognise this-that is, when an officer is seconded to a committee the work of that committee is done in confidence. The officer does not necessarily report back to the department the activities of the committee. This is a fairly normal procedure, as I understand it. However, in this case Mr Landos did, I understand, because of the seriousness of the allegations made in the notes

1924 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Questions without Notice

that he had compiled during the course of the hearings, seek permission from Mr Kelly to refer those notes to the Director of the Bureau of Animal Health. Mr Kelly approved that, apparently. I understand that those notes were referred to Mr Gee, who is the Director of the Bureau of Animal Health. I understand further that Mr Gee referred those notes further down to Mr Hart, who is in charge of this activity in the Bureau of Animal Health. Regrettably, that is where the matter seems to have rested. I can give no explanation at this point as to why no further action was taken on those notes. Having regard to the number of questions asked by the honourable member, I will look at the detail of the questions to see whether I have provided all the information sought.
TELEGRAM SERVICE
Mr DEAN-Is the Minister for Communications aware of the concern of the Union of Postal Clerks and Telegraphists that the telegraphic service in Australia is being allowed to run down; that is, the service operated both by Telecom Australia and Australia Post? Is that so? Is the Government doing anything to ensure that a telegraphic service does continue and, where possible, will be promoted?
Mr N. A. BROWN-I am aware of that matter of concern mainly because the union keeps sending telegrams to me about it. This is about the first boost of which I am aware in the use of the public telegram service that there has been in recent years. I would like to encourage the union members in that activity-not sending the telegrams to me, of course, but to other people. The situation is that the public telegram service will undoubtedly continue in this country. I can be certain about that. The disturbing area, however, is that in recent years there has been a steady decline in the use of the telegram service. That has been matched by a very substantial increase in the costs of running the service, so much so that this year and in recent years it has been running at a loss of about $20m a year, which is a matter for very great concern. I think the other factor that should be taken into account with respect to this matter is that there are other and similar-in some cases much better-methods of fast communication. Of course, I refer to the telex and developing areas such as electronic mail.
I can say this much to the honourable member and to those who have an interest in this subject: The telegram service will continue. However, we are concerned about the costs and we cannot go on running the system at a loss of those dimensions without either economising or trying to find some other way of reducing the ever-increasing

cost of the service. I should also add that one of the great hopes in this area and with respect to other forms of communication is the report of the Davidson Inquiry into Telecommunications Services in Australia, which I hope I will receive in the course of the next few weeks. I hope that will provide for us a charter for the future on the basis of which we can plan for fast economical communications which will serve the very real needs of the country.
MEAT INDUSTRY
Mrs CHILD-My question, which is addressed to the Minister for Primary Industry, also refers to the Landos notes. Do the Landos notes point out examples of blackmail, bribery, incompetence, non-compliance with inspection standards, the substitution of horse meat for meat for domestic consumption and the substitution of domestic meat for export meat? Mr NIXON-I made it perfectly plain before that the Landos notes were not public notes. So the last part of the honourable member's question is correct. The Landos notes became public towards the end of the hearings of the Royal Commission when, under questioning from the Royal Commissioner, it was disclosed that these notes were in existence. As was pointed out by the honourable member for Werriwa, several hundred pages of notes were compiled containing information relating to the travellings of the Kelly Committee and the information it gathered during the course of its investigations. There is a series of allegations-some specific and some non-specific-in the 400-odd pages. If I recall correctly, I tabled the Landos notes in the Parliament at the time of the earlier debate.
My understanding is that when the Landos allegations were made available to the Department they were referred to the officer in control of that section of the Department which deals with these matters. They were not referred to me, regrettably; but that is the way it was. As I understand it, certain administrative actions were taken to overcome administrative weaknesses and difficulties contained in the administration of the

Questions without Notice

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1925

Bureau of Animal Health. I can offer no more information about the matter at this point other than to say that since then, of course, a lot of steps have been taken to ensure that the sorts of misdemeanours and malpractices that have occurred cannot occur again. EXPORTS
Mr LLOYD-My question is directed to the Acting Prime Minister. What details can he provide on the threatened United States Government action in retaliation against European Economic Community export policies by the introduction of direct subsidies for United States export commodities? What effect will such action have on Australia's exports, particularly agricultural exports? What effect will this United States policy have on the forthcoming General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade ministerial meeting and the Australian Government's initiative to prevent further increases in worldwide levels of trade protection?
Mr ANTHONY-I have learnt in the last 24 hours that the United States Congress has moved that a special appropriation be provided to allow United States farm exports to be promoted or subsidised in order to meet, obviously, competition that the United States is facing from subsidised European production. This emerged from Congress; it was not as a result of a request from the Administration. It reflects the growing concern within the United States of the practices of the European Economic Community and its common agricultural policy. We in Australia have been saying for some considerable time that the policies of Europe are leading to surpluses. The subventions that are being paid on exports are totally distorting world trade, are suppressing prices and are making it extremely difficult for efficient producers such as Australia to be able to compete. Australia certainly does not condone this measure by the United States. I think it is unfortunate and regrettable that it has had to do this, but obviously it is due to the utter frustration that it is finding with the EEC and its policies.
If it becomes a practice of the United States Administration to pursue export subsidies, I wonder what the world trading system will come to when two of the largest economies in the world start to fight each other to gain third markets around the world. I only hope that the United States might hold its hand until we have the GATT ministerial meeting in November when the initiatives that Australia has put forward have

gained quite a momentum and there is a recognition of why it is so important now to try to have a moratorium on countries adding to their protection, by way of tariffs, voluntary restraints, nontariff measures or by export subsidies and so forth. Growth in the use of such measures has to come to a standstill, otherwise international trade will collapse. Therefore, whilst I understand the United States measures, I certainly hope that it does not go ahead with them until there is at least a chance of getting some sense through the GATT.
JAPAN: RAW MATERIAL REQUIREMENTS
Mr KEATING-Has the Minister for Trade and Resources approached the Japanese Government and Japanese business about what one can perceive to be an almost permanent downward shift in Australia's share of raw materials being imported into Japan? Has he indicated to Japan the seriousness with which Australia views this shift and has he sought assurances that our traditional market shares will be maintained? Additionally, did he indicate to Japan that, if it is to be that Australia is to be treated so unfairly in a recession, when indeed our industries have been built in service of the Japanese raw material requirements, in a time when Australia and the world are back in a period of buoyancy, the most favoured nation status in respect of energy investment will not obtain in respect of Japan unless the treatment is reciprocated?
Mr ANTHONY-I have taken a lively interest in this aspect of trade between Australia and Japan. Indeed it was one of the main features of discussion at the joint ministerial meeting held here in Canberra in July. Apart from that, I have had a number of discussions with the Japanese Ambassador to emphasise our concern at any shift in the proportions of trade that we have with Japan. I think it is fair enough to say that Australia has to be realistic and recognise that there is a downturn in the world's steel industry. Japan is feeling the consequences as are other countries. Indeed, for the first time since 1972, Japan is forecasting a production ofless than 100 million tonnes of steel. That has always been considered the magic figure. We have always hoped that it would never go below 100 million tonnes but at the moment it appears that, because of a lack of demand for steel within Japan and, in particular, because its export trade does not have a market, Japan cannot purchase the same quantities of coking coal and iron ore.
We are very concerned to ensure that if there is to be a cutback it will be a proportional cutback

1926 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Questions without Notice

and Australia will maintain its traditional export proportions, which are approximately 48 per cent for iron ore and an equitable share is around 50 per cent for coking coal. On the most recent figures available to me there seems to have been a lift in iron ore exports to Japan. The figure did slip back in the earlier part of this year to about 43 per cent, but it seems to be moving nearer the fi~ure of 48 per cent. I certainly hope that is achieved and I will be most appreciative of the Japanese if they maintain the figure at that level. I share the honourable member's concern in this area. The main thing is that Japan recognises us as a traditional long term supplier of great value. The growth of the Japanese steel industry has paralleled the growth of Australia's development of the great resources involved in it. I think it is intrinsic that there be a sound relationship between the two industries. That can best be maintained if Japan keeps up its traditional practices.

UNEMPLOYMENT
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:

The tragedy of high and rising unemployment and the refusal of the Government to concede any responsibility for this threat to the economic and social welfare of Australia.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number of members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr HAWKE (Wills) (2.54)-Official unemployment has burst through the half million mark in this country. Last month the official figures were 504,800. The tragedy is that this Government does not give a damn about these figures. Worse than that, this Government has consistently lied to the Australian community about both the dimensions--
Mr SPEAKER-I interrupt the honourable gentleman. It is not unparliamentary to talk about government lying, but it is not good practice of parliamentary words. I ask him to cease using such words.
Mr HAWKE-Well, the Government has consistently misrepresented to the Australian community both the dimensions and the causes of unemployment in this country. For this growing legion of unemployed the immediate personal devastation of their predicament must be bitter enough. I refer to the loss of income, the loss of capacity to satisfy their aspirations and the needs of those dependent upon them, the loss of status in the eyes of family, neighbours and friends and, worst of all, in many cases the erosion of their own self-esteem. It is difficult-indeed, I suggest that it is impossible-for any honourable member to comprehend fully what this experience is now doing to hundreds of thousands of our fellow Australians but there is one thing that we all ought to be able to understand-whatever the feelings of hurt, humiliation, anger and depression amongst the unemployed, these emotions will be intensified by the perceptions of the attitude of this Government to their plight.
Before talking about the despicable attitude of this Government towards the plight of the unemployed, let me first of all outline the factual dimensions of this unemployment tragedy in this country. Whilst acknowledging straightforwardly and immediately that the work force at the time was only about one-third of the size of that of the present day, we may start to get some idea of the enormity of the current crisis in absolute terms if we go back to the 1933 census which was taken in the midst of the Great Depression. That showed that total unemployment at 30 June 1933 was 481,000, a figure which was surpassed last month. Even if at this stage we have not already passed

Unemployment

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1929

the peak absolute unemployment levels achieved during the Great Depression we will undoubtedly do so within the next few months.
In 1975 the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) promised jobs for all wanting work. I will go into that in more detail in a moment. Seven years later he and his Government have produced the same absolute unemployment levels that existed in 1933. The fact is that not only has unemployment reached record post-war proportions but also the deterioration in the employment situation is accelerating. Over the past 12 months unemployment in Australia rose by a massive 113,700 people. In other words, there was a 29 per cent increase in unemployment in this country in the last 12 months. There is an awesome risk of another unemployment rise of this magnitude or more by early next year. The Government has obviously lost control of the situation. The impact of unemployment in this country is totally inequitable. It is magnifying enormously the inequities already present in Australian society. The unemployment rate last month was 7.4 per cent-as I have said, a post-war record-but that rate was inequitably distributed. Amongst women it was 8.4 per cent and amongst youth it was 19 per cent. in other words, about one in five of our young people are unemployed. Of course, the rate was very much higher still amongst Aboriginals.
The Government has created a lost generation-thousands and thousands of young Australians reach their mid-twenties with little or no work experience.
Over the past 12 months the unemployment crisis has taken another disturbing twist. Most of last year's unemployment rise of 100,000 plus was concentrated among adult breadwinners. Adult male unemployment alone rose by 60,500 people. The rise for adult women was smaller only because they are dropping out of the labour market on a massive scale into the hidden unemployed. So unemployment is now striking at the heart of the nuclear family for which this Government professes to have such a high regard. The chief victims of hidden unemployment are women. If all these hidden unemployment figures were properly accounted for the statistics would show that the true unemployment rate in Australia was at the very least 10 per cent to 11 per cent.
I refer not only to the numbers of unemployed but also to the fact that the average periods of unemployment are rising disturbingly. In August, 157,700 people wanting full time employment had been unemployed for six months or more. Of the total unemployed 178,200 had been unemployed for six months or more. Indeed, in August

the average length of unemployment for teenagers was 24 weeks, or just under six months, and for the total unemployed the average was 32.8 weeks, an incredible eight months. For older workersthe forgotten unemployed, I suggest-the figure was even higher. For those in the age bracket 55 to 59, the average unemployment period was 46.9 weeks. For those in the latter span of employment possibilities, those in the age group 60 to 64, the average duration of unemployment was 57.5 weeks or more than one year. Necessarily briefly, those are the facts, the cold statistics, that mask the human tragedy-a tragedy which, as I have said, is being made more bitter by the disgraceful attitudes and excuses of this Government.
What have been those attitudes and excuses which this Government has adopted in regard to unemployment in this country? All honourable members will recall that, in 1975 when we had seen the beginning of serious unemployment in this country, the Prime Minister, who was then the Leader of the Opposition, unequivocally ridiculed any suggestion that external economic events could be used in mitigation of responsibility by the Australian Government. I have had cause to refer to this matter before, but I remind the House again of what the Prime Minister, the then Leader of the Opposition, had to say on 22 January 1975 because these words need to be met by the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) who will answer in this debate. This is what his leader, the then Leader of the Opposition, had to say on 22 January about external factors:
When political leaders say the present situation cannot be helped, it is part of a world situation, they are expressing the futility of their own leadership.
That is what the present Prime Minister said in 1975. When this Government says that, in respect of unemployment, it is part of a world situation, it is expressing the futility of its own leadership.
The unequivocal promise was made in this context by the then Leader of the Opposition before he became Prime Minister: If his Government came to power at the end of 1975, it would restore full employment. In making that unequivocal promise there was not a mention or a word that that promise would in any way be impeded by external considerations. I ask the Minister to note and to recall that when his leader made the promise in 1975 that he would restore full employment not a word or a mention was made that the wages policy of the Australian trade union movement would prevent the achievement of that promise. There was not a word in 1975 that external forces or wage movements in this country would prevent this Government restoring full employment. The

1930 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Unemployment

promise was unequivocal and unqualified: Elect a Fraser Government and unemployment will be banished. There will be jobs for all who want to work.
As that promise was miserably and inevitably broken and as unemployment increased under the contractionary economic policies of this Government, what has it had to say? Of course, at first the line was that there really was not a problem. Members of this Government used and encouraged in others the dole bludger syndrome. Their argument was that there was plenty of work for those who really wanted to work. That was never accurate and it was always obscenely objectionable. As the obscenity became more and more apparent as the ranks of the unemployed continued to grow, the Government has changed its tack. Having blamed the Whitlam Government and the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission, the Government's line now is to blame the two factors that it did not mention in 1975 that is, the international economic scene and excessive wage movements. The Government has now blamed everyone, including the unemployed. It has blamed every factor but it has not blamed itself and its own economic policies. Not one word has ever been uttered by the Prime Minister or by any of the Ministers in this Government that they and their economic policies have one skerrick of responsibility for unemployment in this country. It is always someone or something else. Now it has blamed the two factors that it did not mention in 1975 as constituting any possible bar to bringing back full employment in this country.
I intend to look at those two factors which were used by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) in his Budget Speech as the reasons for the level of unemployment. I challenge the Minister in his reply to address himself to these issues. He should not simply get up and say that it is due to external factors or wage movements; he should analyse what is said about it. Before going to those factors I want to give some idea of what credence honourable members can give to the Government's budgetary explanations and analysis of the problem of unemployment by just looking at the blatant and deliberate misrepresentation that was undertaken in the Budget Papers about the cause of unemployment during the financial year. I remind honourable members of what was stated at page 61 of Budget Paper No.1:
Consistent with this assessment, and the assumed effect of Government manpower policies, the Budget estimates allow for the average number of unemployment benefits recipients to rise from an average of 332 000 in 1981-82 to an average of 395 000 in 1982-83.

That was simply a blatant and deliberate lie. No other use of language is appropriate.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order!
Mr HAWKE-Well, it is a blatant misrepresentation, Mr Deputy Speaker. When that figure of 395,000 was used in the Budget Papers the figure for July was already 405,000 and the figure for the succeeding month of August was 422,000. Honourable members will recall that the Minister ran away from those figures as soon as I faced him with them.
In the light of the Government's deliberate misrepresentation of the dimension of unemployment, what weight can one give to its analysis of the causes? First of all, the Government says now that the problem has international causes. We have pointed out what the Prime Minister said about this in 1975. Let us simply have on the table the truth about this factor. It is true now, as it was in 1975, that Australia cannot escape the impact of what is happening overseas. It was true to say in 1975 that we were affected and it is true to say that we in Australia are affected now by what happens overseas; but that is no excuse for what is happening in Australia. If there is no engine of growth or there are no employment opportunities within this country as a result of what is happening overseas, it is all the more important that we should adopt stimulatory policies to try to countervail the impact of what is happening overseas.
In respect of the second factor-that is, that excessive wages growth in this country is causing unemployment-let me simply remind the House that this Government is responsible for what has happened to wages in this country in the last 12 months or more. It was this Government which helped to destroy indexation in 1981. It was this Government which said to the unions: 'Use the same power as business has got to get what you will'. The unions inevitably, as I have pointed out before, responded to this attitude of the Government. One cannot blame wages in that sense. We on this side of the House accept that wages are a significant factor, but there must be a positive policy, such as the policy of the Australian Labor Party; that is, one of mild stimulatory expansion under the cover of a prices and incomes policy with job generation programs, which my colleague will speak about, which will match this tragedy that exists in this country. The Government can no longer bleat about the cost of job generation when it is faced with the costs of massive social dimensions in this country; those of crime, hard drugs, homelessness, desperation and suicide. The Government has to face up to those

Unemployment

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1931

costs and undertake now programs which will match the tragedy of unemployment in this country.
Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations) (3.9)-The honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) has quoted in a selective way from statements made by the then Leader of the Opposition in a particular speech. The honourable member would do well to look at the Hansard record of the time when this Government was in opposition. Honourable members, including me, continually analysed the state of the economy at that time with reference to external factors. Indeed, I recall saying ad nauseam, as did others, that there were external factors and it was a great fortune for Australia that we had a ready supply of low cost Bass Strait oil which largely insulated us from the worst aspects of the economic downturn overseas. So we acknowledged that there were external factors. We also acknowledged the wages explosion as, to his credit, did the honourable member for Wills when he was leader of the trade union movement-or he did so shortly afterwards when the wages explosion clearly got out of hand.
I do not see the point in spending much time on the past, except to the extent that we might learn something from it. But we are confronted now with a similar situation to that which prevailed in 1974-75 in that we have just recovered from a comparable wages explosion. We are experiencing much the same international economic pressures which existed at that time. We certainly have provided, as we are bound to provide, information through the Australian Bureau of Statistics and elsewhere on the State of the labour market. Those are the figures cited in this debate. I do not disagree with the analysis of the labour market as it now stands but I do resent very strongly a statement by the honourable member to the effect that this Government does not give a damn about the unemployed and has misrepresented the position to the Australian people. We have done nothing of the kind. We have said over and over again that we are concerned about the state of unemployed persons. We have a range of welfare and other policies as well as the array of manpower and training programs designed to assist those people. But in dealing with the twin problems of inflation and unemployment we must be careful to act within the bounds of responsible economic management which must, therefore, have some regard to the deficit in order not to worsen the position of persons on fixed incomes or those whose jobs are marginally held. That would be the situation if we had a greater Budget deficit than we have at the moment. It would be the situation if we put

further pressure on interest rates and on inflation. This is a matter of judgment for the government oftheday.
Looking at the programs put forward by the Australian Labor Party one sees fragmentary programs-one by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) in his so-called shadow Budget and one by the honourable member for Wills, a much more conservative and, in my view, a much more responsible approach in terms of so-called job creation. In terms of the kind of unemployment problem drawn it is not a great advance on the number of people catered for and, in my view, in terms of longer term job creation, more permanent job creation, it is not an advance at all. It is one thing to talk of 150,000 people or 70,000 people placed in a job as a result of some job subsidy program which blows out the Budget and another thing to look at the consequences of displacement of other persons who currently are in employment as a result of that kind of initiative. I believe that the Labor Party has not itself got its house in order. If we are going to be talking in some of these extravagant terms let me refer the Labor Party to the fact that it, in dealing with its policies for the forthcoming election, met in July and overlooked dealing with the question of UrIemployment. Mr COHEN-The most damning indictment of the Government is its total refusal to take any action to alleviate unemployment. All we have heard since 1975 is that the way to provide employment is to strengthen the private sector and to wait for an upturn in the world economy. For seven years the Australian people have waited and waited, and the result is still more and more unemployment. Even the most conservative estimates suggest that unemployment will rise to over 600,000 when the schoolleavers go on to the employment market early next year. Some estimates suggest 700,000. One wonders how a government with such an appalling record of economic mismanagement and which promised to restore full employment has the gall to remain in office. When one adds its economic record to the scandals that have wracked it-tax evasion, Asia Dairy Industries (Hong Kong) Ltd, the Australian Dairy Corporation, and the meat industry-one would think that if it had one ounce of decency left it would have resigned by now. If not, the least it could do would be to recant, admit its failures and to show some feeling for the unemployed by providing some job creation schemes in an attempt to lift the economy out of the deep hole into which it is presently sliding.
The Government and the Prime Minister have a deep philosophical commitment to the monetarist theories of Milton Friedman. Mr Friedman has amongst his admirers President Reagan, Mrs Thatcher and the Prime Minister. The economies of the countries of two of those leaders are almost basket cases, and the Australian economy is going the same way. What a great record the advice of Milton Friedman has! I noticed that the Minister, when quoting economies that were not picking up, did not mention countries which tried a different approach such as Japan and Austria. He chose West Germany and France. This one man, Milton Friedman, plus those who follow the simplistic nonsense that public sector expenditure is bad and private sector expenditure is good, have done more damage to the world economy than any others in recent times; yet people still cling to his ravings in the belief that one day-some day; any day-they will fall into place. It is the sadomasochistic school of economics; if one suffers so much pain it really must eventually do some good.
The opposition to public sector expenditure is the main reason why the Government cannot bring itself to admit that it was wrong and provide

Unemployment

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1935

funds for job creation schemes. It is so hooked on its past decade of rhetoric attacking public sector expenditure that it would rather go down the gurgler and take the rest of Australia with it than admit that it was wrong and reverse its policies. When most people talk of job creation schemes they think of the Labor Government's Regional Employment Development Scheme introduced by Clyde Cameron. Few recall the fact that the McMahon Government, facing a minimal increase in unemployment, provided $27m in 1971-72 and $103.8m in 1972-73 to create employment in rural areas with its Rural Unemployment Relief Scheme. At its peak there were some 15,000 people employed throughout the nation under the RUR scheme at a cost of some $6m per month. The problem with the RUR scheme was that 75 per cent pf the funds had to be spent on labour. The result was that only labour-intensified projects could be undertaken-painting, chipping weeds and mowing lawns. When the $130m had been spent there was nothing to show for the expenditure except nice clean, tidy communities.
When the Labor Government came to office Clyde Cameron determined that if ever it was necessary to provide funds for unemployment relief, the money expended would leave the community with something of value. This was found to be necessary in August 1974, and the Regional Employment Development scheme-the RED scheme-was introduced, and the amount of funds required to be spent on labour was reduced to 50 per cent. This meant that slightly fewer jobs would be provided but that projects of a much more substantial nature-a socially useful nature-could be provided and that the communities which benefited from RED funds would be left with something of value when the scheme was over. In 1974-75 some $160.4m was expended on the RED scheme. A total of $184.3m was expended during the course of the RED scheme. At the peak of the scheme in July 1975 some 32,000 people were employed directly on RED projects. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table indicating RED scheme employment.

Leave granted. The table read as follows-

Mr COHEN-That table does not take into account the number of jobs created by indirect expenditure. Let me make it clear that no one in the Australian Labor Party believes that any job creation scheme is a total panacea for full employment. Obviously the strength of the economy is the major factor. We are firmly of the view that a job creation scheme is still the best measure to alleviate short term unemployment. It is the only way of providing depressed areas with some relief. It is the only way of providing work experience for some young people who have never known what it is to have a job. It is the only way of giving dignity to the unemployed; even if they get only three months' work a year it is a far better way of utilising taxation revenue than paying people unemployment benefits. A proper job creation scheme will leave, as the RED scheme did, hundreds of useful public works projects which will benefit present and future generations of Australians.
