Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BOURCHIER (Bendigo) (3.33)-Before directly addressing this matter of public importance I shall respond to some of the comments made by the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) and the honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen). The honourable member for Wills mentioned that the Government is apparently not concerned about unemployment, which will be seen, of course, to be totally untrue when he and all other Australians take into account the measures introduced in the recent Budget. The honourable member has, of course, taken the usual stance that all the blame should fall on the Government. Coming from a person who was the leader of the trade union movement in this country, that comment would have to be treated as a joke if this were not such a serious matter. The unions must share the blame as must governments, employers and everybody else. In fact I and, I think, most people in this country have come to believe that the unions have to shoulder more than their fair share of the blame because of their actions in constantly pressing for wage demands at the risk of jobs. The honourable member for Wills knows quite well, even though he raises his eyebrows as usual, that the demand by unions to press up wages is constantly causing Australian production to be priced out of the market. There is no way that Australian manufacturers have been able to produce goods to compete. As a result, the only way they can compete is to reduce their costs. Material costs are very difficult to prune but labour costs are not. Employers have to look at that factor. Unfortunately and regrettably, in many cases they have to retrench people in order to reduce their overhead costs so that they can remain in business.
One has only to look at the Tasmanian experience not long ago. A Tasmanian firm decided, in discussion with its employees, that it had two choices to make. Firstly, it could close down and everybody would be retrenched or, secondly, employees could take a reduction in salary, not the standard salary but the overaward payment which would bring salaries to a standard level. Of course, the bulk of employees accepted the second proposal and were prepared to work for a reduced salary but they had no choice in the matter. The trade union movement stepped in and said: 'It is a matter of principle. We do not care if every member loses his job as long as we uphold the principle.' What a great principle! The trade union movement wants to demand high wages

and put everybody out of work. That is exactly what it did in that case.
There is another factor for which the unions must take the blame. Possibly there are arguments in some areas why this should have happened. I refer to the increase in salaries that has been demanded for juniors. The wage of juniors in proportion to the adult wage has markedly increased over the years. As a result, many employers have been reluctant to take on young inexperienced people in preference to experienced adults. They know full well that, even though they will pay slightly more, they will have a productivity gain for the simple reason that a young person has to be trained. The first 12 months at least of his employment is a total loss of production. These factors have led to the serious problem we have today. It is fair to say that employers would rather put on young people if they had the incentive to do so.
The honourable member for Wills mentioned figures for 1933. Apart from the increase in population since that time, I suggest that he ought also to take into consideration that two of the factors that have affected employment today are the employment of married women and equal pay for women. I am not objecting to those factors at all. I do not think that anybody is opposed to the principle. I am merely pointing out that they are factors that have increased the job opportunities for married women. I say that in fairness if one is looking at figures-that is what the honourable member for Wills was doing when he referred to 1933-one ought to consider that there are far more married women in the work force today than there were in 1933. I am merely pointing out that factor. People should not say in this House that this matter is of concern and it is the Government's fault. We ought to be looking at what we can all do to try to overcome the problem.
Mr Hawke-But you will not accept any responsibility.
Mr BOURCHIER-The member for Wills says that the Government will not accept responsibility. It has accepted responsibility.
Mr Hawke-Have you made any mistakes?
Mr BOURCHIER-Of course governments make mistakes but the honourable member for Wills made many mistakes when he was leader of the trade union movement. Instead of trying to be as smart as the honourable member for Wills in blaming all and sundry and not accepting some responsibility let me suggest to him that it might not be a bad idea if we looked at the matter as it should be looked at. It is an Australian problem, not a government problem.

Unemployment

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1937

Mr Hawke-Did you say that in 1975?
Mr BOURCHIER-The honourable member should be patient. The same problem has occurred since 1974-75. I have said before and I say it again that it is not a matter for government. Governments will not fix the situation by just pouring in money. If money is the answer, we should look at the increase in funds that the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) has supplied for manpower and training programs. That will not create permanent jobs although it will be far better than the Regional Employment Development Scheme. It will at least provide job training for permanent jobs. It will create the opportunity for people to learn trades and be involved whereas the RED scheme was proven to be a failure by the Australian Labor Party which introduced it. The honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) made his only comment that was any different from what the honourable member for Wills said when he mentioned the RED scheme. The favourite platform of the Labor Party socialists is that if they spend money all the problems will go away. It is not true. They found that the cost of trying to prop up temporary jobs made the RED scheme a total failure. As a result, they withdrew the scheme. I wonder whether it is possible to find out how many of those people who obtained temporary jobs are in fact still employed in those jobs today. I suggest that very few are.
There is no doubt, as the honourable member for Robertson correctly said, that the scheme probably helped the morale of unemployed people. That is a good thing on a temporary basis but surely it is better for us, as a community, a government and a people, to look at ways of providing long term effective employment? In this category I suggest that the unions, for example, could call for a removal of penalty rates and create jobs in the service industries such as restaurants, theatres and places where people are employed after hours, at night time or weekends. Many of these places are forced to close because they have to pay double time on Sundays, time and a half on Saturdays and so on. If there were no penalty rates they would stay open seven days a week to provide services to the public and jobs to the people who need them. If the unions were to do away with the 17t per cent loading on holiday pay and the same amount of money were spent on labor, how many extra jobs would that create in factories such as General MotorsHolden's Ltd in Melbourne? Productivity would increase and the cost of cars would come down. Jobs would be created.

I call on the Federal Government, as a starter, to ask the Public Service-then, of course, it would flow on to other employers--to introduce a job sharing program on a voluntary basis, not as a compulsory measure. Many people in the work force today would be glad to work for, say, two or three days a week and create jobs. Those jobs could be taken up by young people who could be trained so that at the appropriate time they could take over the jobs full time when the other people retired or resigned. There are opportunities for us as a community to be involved and to do something. These are the measures we should all be undertaking. We should not just sit on the other side as the Opposition loves to do and blame the Government without putting forward any positive measures. I am putting forward a couple of positive measures. I suggest to the honourable member for Wills who used to so keenly support the trade union movement-I assume he still does, if it will have him-that he might like to support what I am saying. Let him for once stand up and be counted and say that members of the Labor Party, the socialists of this country, want to see jobs, that they are prepared to do away with overtime rates and penalty clauses. I see the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding), the rough red from Richmond, shaking his head as usual but of course I know his stance. Let us get down to the facts. He can also stand up and say these things. He can also stand up and support the people of this country. He should ask members of the work force whether they would like the unions to do that. Of course they would. How many people would be prepared to take on part time and shared work provided they would not be penalised? To do that State governments would need to become involved with workers compensation adjustments. Of course they could do it.
Mr Holding-You are now trying to blame State governments for unemployment. You unmitigated hypocrite!
Mr BOURCHIER-There is an interjection from the honourable member for Melbourne Ports. He has said nothing to this House about this matter since he came here. He is not a bit interested in what is happening to young people in this country.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member for Melbourne Ports will withdraw the reflection.
Mr Holding-I withdraw.
Mr BOURCHIER-The Victorian Government tried to introduce another type of RED

1938 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Petitions

scheme which paid $70 a week to local government to employ people. If any local government takes on a person, it has to pay $170 to $200 a week to supplement his wage. How many extra jobs do honourable members honestly believe that any local government can afford, irrespective of how much they want to employ people? How much could they afford to spend of the ratepayers' money to create extra jobs?
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired. The discussion is concluded.
PETITIONS
The Acting Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
South West Tasmania: Wilderness Area
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That your petitioners are gravely concerned that the taxes of all Australians will be used to finance a dam that would flood the Lower Gordon and Franklin Rivers in South-West Tasmania.
Your petitioners declare that the intrinsic values of this area, and the provision of funds from the taxes of all Australians , for the destruction of this area of the National Estate and of world heritage value, does make this a national issue.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government take all possible steps, including financial and legal steps and fulfil its responsibilities under the Australian Heritage Act to prevent the destruction of this area of the National Estate.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr N. A. Brown and Mr Cunningham.
Petitions received.
Australian Postal Commission
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The Petition of certain citizens of Australia shows:
I. that Australia as a sparsely populated continent requires the maintenance of a high quality postal service as a vital means of communication.
2. that the Australian postal service meets the needs of all Australians.
3. that any reduction in Australia Post's monopoly power to carry letters for reward will destroy the concept of the national postal service and result in:
increased rates for mail carried outside the capital cities;
a reduction in mail delivery and collection frequencies in non-profitable areas;
the closure of post offices.
Your petitioners therefore pray: that the powers of the Postal Commission to carry letters for reward should be strengthened to ensure that the national postal service is retained in the interests of all Australians.
by Mrs Child.

Petition received.
Australian Postal Commission
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of certain citizens undersigned respectfully showeth:
I. That Australia as a sparsely populated continent requires the maintenance of a high quality postal service as a vital means of communication.
2. That the Australian Postal Service truly meets the needs of all Australians.
3. That any proposal that Australia Post's monopoly power to carry letters for reward be diminished will destroy the concept of the national postal service.
Your petitioners therefore pray: That the powers of the Postal Commission to carry letters for reward should be strengthened to ensure that the national postal service is retained in the interests of all Australians.
by Mr Tuckey.
Petition received.
National Estate Areas
To the honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled: The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned at the lack of effective Federal environmental protection legislation and urge that suitable legislation be enacted immediately.
Your petitioners are concerned that National Estate areas such as South West Tasmania, the Great Barrier Reef and the Western Australian Jarrah Forests cannot be fully protected until adequate legislation exists and is enforced. Your humble petitioners therefore call upon the House of Representatives to ensure that all provisions of the Heritage Act and the Environment Impact of Proposals Act are fully complied with in respect of National Estate Areas. In particular your petitioners call upon you to adopt the recommendations of the report of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on the Environment on South West Tasmania. Your petitioners believe that this is a national issue and must be resolved by the action of the Federal Parliament.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr MacKellar and Mr Mountford.

Retirement Benefits: Private Enterprise
To the honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of certain citizens of Australia respectfully showeth our concern that:
I. The Income Tax Assessment Act (the Act) and its administration is inadequate in providing retirement benefits for employees in private enterprise;

Petitions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1939

2. The Commissioner of Taxation (Commissioner) denies deductions necessary to index retirement pensions;
3. Deductions for the self-employed and employed are inadequate;
4. The Commissioner has exercised his discretion improperly by negating a choice the Act allows on investment in Government Securities;
5. The Commissioner's constant alterations to guidelines and lack of communication has created great uncertainty.
6. The Commissioner is acting arbitrarily, and without proper notice in restricting trustee's investments with employers;
7. The Commissioner seeks to apply new actuarial guidelines -retrospectively to July 1981.
Your Petitioners humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, take action to remedy these inadequacies and in particular we pray that action be taken:
(i) To ensure that the Commissioner exercises his discretion only with proper regard to legal principles;
(ii) To ensure that the Commissioner does not delegate to auditors part of his function of administering the Act;
(iii) To enact legislation allowing proper levels of retirement benefits to be provided;
(iv) To ensure that retirement benefits are regulated by a Department with a primarily social welfare function.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr N. A. Brown.
Petition received.
Broadcasting Licences
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That Family Radio Ltd represents the largest single interest group in the community-i.e., christians-who do not have a voice in public broadcasting commensurate with their representation. That prime time access to existing broadcasts by christian groups is virtually non-existent, and access at other times is fast disappearing.
That, by comparison with other capitals, Brisbane is entitled to at least one more category 'S' FM broadcasting licence.
That there appears to be no prospect of the release of further category 'S' FM licences in the near future for Brisbane.
That satisfactory fulfilment of the aims of Family Radio and its expansion can only be achieved by broadcasting in its own right under licence.
That Family Radio has a responsibility to the large support groups which have contributed substantially to its development, to achieve the aims in broadcasting and community work for which it was established.
That there is an urgent need for the kind of service and support that Family Radio can provide to other community groups through the particular type of integrated programming proposed by the company under a full-time licence.
And that pruned Government resources are obviously insufficient to advance the development of public broadcasting as originally intended.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will suport Family Radio's application for a broadcasting licence and will enable more category 'S' FM licences to be made available in Brisbane.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Department of Social Security
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectively showeth:
That citizens of Queensland view with great alarm the removal of the Director of the Office of Child Care and fear for the future of that office and the children's services programme which it administers.
We believe the Office of Child Care to be inappropriately placed within the Department of Socia! Security, where children's services are in competition with welfare services for the aged, disabled, and other such groups.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, should take every step as necessary to obtain immediate withdrawal of the Office of Child Care from the Department of Social Security and the establishment of a Children's Commission.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Vietnam Veterans
To the Right Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
(I) that US., Australian and allied forces used huge amounts of toxic chemicals for military purposes, such as Agents Orange, Blue and White, in Vietnam during the war there;
(2) many of these chemicals were, and are, known to be highly dangerous to human health;
(3) Australia's Vietnam veterans and their families are suffering many severe problems which may be due to their exposure to these chemicals; and
(4) the Vietnam Veterans Association of Australia has been seeking a Royal Commission for well over two years to ensure an independent investigation of these matters.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly pray that the Parliament of Australia will (a) express its support for the Royal Commission; and (b) urge the Federal Government to establish the Royal Commission immediately.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Holding.
Petition received.

Petition received.
Rights of Disabled
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respecfully showeth:
I. The failure of the Federal Government to seek information from the census re disabled people-i.e. partial or total disability-to enable for adequate planning of, and for, types of services needed by disabled people to be undertaken.
2. The failure of the Federal Government to lead the way in employment of disabled people by employing a greater number of such people within the Commonwealth Public Service-note the small number currently employed within the Service.
3. The wastage of the Federal Government funds perpetuating large, bureaucratic institutions and organisations where there are often few disabled people on their Boards and little emphasis is placed on consumer participation in decision making.
4. The failure of the Federal Government to encourage independence of disabled people, or to develop 'normalised' support systems--attendant care.

5. The failure of the Federal Government to provide 'noninstitutionalised' specialised accommodation for those people who would not be able to b~nefit from, or cope with attendant care.
6. Failure by the Federal Government to alter, or create a 'disability' allowance means test free for all disabled people-this would assist with necessary equipment and mobility costs.
7. Failure to provide a national compensation scheme thus saving time and enabling people to be automatically covered in the event of a disability.
8. Failure by the Federal Government to abolish tariffs on all equipment available to the disabled-e.g. Optacon.
9. Mismanagement of Federal l.Y.D.P. funds-e.g. nonreceipt of some States of funds through unresolved negotiations.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that immediate action be taken to remedy the above stated and that the Federal Government recognise that disabled people are citizens who should have equal rights.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Mountford.

Petition received.
Telegram Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We the undersigned citizens of the electorate of Macarthur respectfully showeth:
That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:
I. planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
2. closing country telegraph offices,
3. increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service,
4. withdrawing telegram operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
5. generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service through centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30 per cent of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of the Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrBaume.

That the plan by the Australian Telecommunications Commission to withdraw public telegram facilities and downgrade telegram standards by:
I. planning to withdraw the 24 hour telegraph service at the Chief Telegraph Office in each capital city and in Canberra and Darwin,
2. closing country telegraph offices,
3. increasing telegram charges whilst downgrading the service,
4. withdrawing telegram operating facilities from a large number of suburban and country post offices,
5. generating serious delays in telegram delivery by reducing standards of service through centralisation of the network and reduction of operating and delivery staff,
will reduce an available means of communications in commercial enterprise, deny the opportunity for people of Australia to make contact with family and friends in times of emergency, and civil or natural disasters, particularly for the 30 per cent of Australians who are not telephone subscribers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will take measures to ensure the maintenance of an efficient, effective public telegram service to meet the needs of the Australian people socially, publicly, commercially and in times of emergencies and civil or natural disasters.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Sainsbury.
Petition received.

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1947

SOCIAL SECURITY LEGISLATION AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 23 September, on motion by Mr Wilson:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (3.49)-Today we are debating the social welfare largesse extended to the disadvantaged in our community in a Budget which was conceived as an early election Budget. It is as a result of that early election impetus that for once a Budget of this Government contains some advances in the social welfare field and some improvements in benefits. However, as these changes come after seven years of neglect in this field, the provisions can be summed up as too little, too late. The Opposition will not be opposing the Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill because of the improvements that do occur but it will be making one major general criticism of the measure and three specific criticisms. I wish to devote what I am going to say this afternoon to a consideration of those points.
First of all, we deplore the Government's failure to protect the real value of social security pensions, benefits and payments generally. Throughout my speech, I will illustrate the failure of this Government over seven years to maintain the real value of benefits and pensions. We would make three specific criticisms of the Government's social welfare legislation as embodied in this measure. First of all, the Government continues to discriminate against the unemployed as compared with other groups of social welfare beneficiaries despite the increasing catastrophic proportions of unemployed in our community. Secondly, clearly there is a need to improve the situation of the unemployed under 18 years of age. Their needs remain. Thirdly, we want to look particularly at maintaining the real value of payments made through the social welfare fund to children in our community. I will deal with those three specific points: The discrimination issue; the issue of the unemployed under 18 years of age; and the issue of benefits for children. Throughout the debate we will be able to illustrate the more general criticism we are making, which is the undermining in seven years of the real value of the social welfare provision in this country.
I will begin with the discrimination issue. I think we can divide the welfare beneficiaries in this community into various classes of citizens as defined by this Government. I will begin with the pensioners. The six-monthly indexed pension increase in November which is authorised in the Budget will increase the single pension to $77.25 a

week and the family pension to $128.80 a week. However, despite this indexation, the standard pension rate has fallen from 24.5 per cent of average weekly earnings in 1975 to the current 22 per cent of average weekly earnings, and will go on declining in the months ahead unless there is an additional boost to the standard rate of pension. Indeed, at the rate the pattern is moving we will return to the position we had under the McMahon Government where, at the end of that Government, the pension rate as a proportion of average weekly earnings was 18.6 per cent. I think it is important to make this contrast now.
In three years the Whitlam Labor Government, despite the economic difficulties faced by it, was able to raise the rate of pension as a proportion of average weekly earnings from 18.6 per cent to 24.5 per cent. We did not quite get to our goal of 25 per cent. In the seven years of this Government there has been a gradual and slow erosion from the 24.5 per cent figure of 1975 to the present 22 per cent figure. Contrast the achievement of Labor in this field and the failures of this Government in this field. One other increase for the pensioner in this Budget is a $2 a week increase in the supplementary rental assistance. That increase will cost a total of $24m in a full year, which is very much a pittance compared to the $415m given in aid to first home buyers. I think one can easily see with such a comparison the sort of priority the Government really gives to the aged. Age pensioners in the eyes of this Government are very much second class citizens.
If the pensioner is treated as a second class citizen by this Government, there are other citizens far lower in the Government's pecking order. Consider, for instance, a family whose breadwinner is thrown out of a job, being retrenched after 20 years of service to a firm. That is increasingly common in this society. Certainly that man will receive a basic benefit at the same rate as the family age pension. But, unlike the pensioners, the allowance the unemployed person will receive for his spouse and children is subject to tax. This is quite distinct from the situation applying to the pensioner. Even with no outside income, an unemployed person has to pay income tax if he has more than three children. Pensioners do not have to pay taxes on these allowances. This discrimination suggests that this Government wants in some way to punish those half a million Australians who dare to be unemployed and to treat them differently from other welfare beneficiaries. This seems to me a petty and outrageous discrimination. It would be relatively simple for the Government to end it and end it quickly.

1948 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill

The unemployed families do not receive the rental assistance given to pensioners. Apart from the health care card, they do not receive the attached fringe benefits on public transport, rates, car registration, et cetra, which are extended to pensioners by the States. The unemployed have to pay $2 on each prescription for their medicines. That is certainly an improvement on the past situation, but it is trumpeted by this Government as a great social achievement. Yet the unemployed families do not receive pharmaceuticals free of charge, as do other welfare beneficiaries, as do the pensioners. Is this because the family on the unemployment benefit is less needy than pensioners? No. It just seems to me that the Government considers them less worthy than pensioners and provides simply a concessional rather than a free pharmaceutical allowance. So, we may classify this second group, that is, the unemployed with families, as Fraser's third rate citizens.
However, if the unemployed person is single and over 18 without dependants he or she will be paid from November a benefit that is $12.85 a week less than the single rate of pension. When pensioners receive their May indexation increase-the single unemployed without dependants will receive nothing-the single unemployed will fall even further behind because their rates are not indexed. The single unemployed receive a benefit increase only at the whim of the Government. Should this unemployed person decide to exercise some of the enterprise that is much praised by this Government, he or she can earn only $10 a week before the benefit starts to be reduced. So there is little scope for these people to supplement their meagre benefits. Even the pensioners, the second-class citizens of this Government, can earn $30 a week without having the payments reduced. The single unemployed have these additional disadvantages on top of all those that the family unemployed suffer. They are Fraser's fourth-rate citizens.
We in the Labor Party believe that there is a strong case for making the rates and conditions applying to pensions and benefits as similar as possible. Not only are the needs of pensioners and unemployed beneficiaries likely to be very much the same, but also it can often be a matter of chance which benefit a person with marginal work force status receives. Such a lottery ought not to produce the harsh results that now apply. One thinks of the constant problems of the movement between invalid pensioner and unemployed status and the difficulties that that occasions for families. That is why we will be moving a specific amendment to end the discrimination against the

unemployed that now exists. But we have not finished the classes of Fraser's lower level citizens.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The Chair cannot accept the term used by the honourable member.
Dr BLEWETT-Now we come to the fifthclass citizens of the present Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). I suppose that we could call these the untouchables, that is, the unemployed near-adults of 16 or 17 years. This group, after seven years of no change in their unemployment benefit, will be receiving from this generous Government a $4 a week increase. They will now get $40 a week as their benefit. This is the first increase that this group has received in seven years. The value of the dollar has nearly halved in that time under this Government. A 17-year-old is expected to be able to live on $24.40 a week less than the 18-year-old receives. His or her paltry benefit is less than half the poverty line figure.
The social tensions that Fraserism has built up are causing massive dislocations in family life in our society. Given the poverty and unemployment in Australian society today, many families have difficulty in coping with the problems of near-adults. In many cases these young people simply have to leave the family home to escape the conflicts that arise. To cite a situation closer to the Government's heart-I see that at least one member of the National Country Party is here, so let me address the problems to him-we all know the catastrophic state of unemployment amongst young people in country towns and country areas. What about the 16-year-olds and 17-year-olds who are forced to move out of these areas to the city to look for work and to attend interviews? We know that with the best will in the world it is a long grind for these young adults to get any kind of job. In the weeks and months they are looking for work they have to live away from home, paying for food and board or rent on $40 a week, $5 a day. One suggests that perhaps the Ministers should undergo a period trying to survive under those conditions. Even if one wants to argue that there should be some distinction between 16-yearolds and 17-year-olds and the adult groups in their benefit payments, at least I think there is a glaring need for rental assistance for any unemployed young person living away from home. There is indeed an argument that any unemployed person should receive some form of rental assistance where required. But there is a very strong case indeed for the young unemployed living away from home to receive some rental support. Thus, while the total unemployment benefit situation needs an overhaul, the 16-year-olds and 17-yearolds are a special case because they have been the

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1949

most harshly treated under the seven years of this Government's neglect. That is why in our second reading amendment we will be proposing that the Bill be redrafted to provide for greater increases in payments to the under 18 years unemployed.
Before leaving the unemployment benefit, we should notice that the Budget estimates on unemployment for 1982-83 seem to have a distinct rubberiness about them, to use a word made famous by the former and lamented Minister for Industry and Commerce. The farcical level of these estimates becomes more apparent each month as the catastrophic increases in the level of unemployment in this country are announced. The estimated expenditure on the unemployment benefit for 1982-83 is supposedly $1,567m. But this was based on an average number of beneficiaries of 395,000. The latest available figure on the number of beneficiaries is 420,000 in August, and we have all read of the massive leap in unemployment since that date as revealed in the Australian Bureau of Statistics figures in September. It is the Government's own mismanagement of the economy which is plunging Australia deeper and deeper into recession. This will blow out the payment on the unemployment benefit by, I would calculate, approximately $300m. Of course, all this was done because the Government believed it could rush this Budget to the country and have an early election before the shabby nature of its calculations were revealed to the Australian public.
Finally, I want to look at the Government's much-vaunted policies on the importance of the family. In 1976 the Prime Minister called the family allowance changes the single most important reform in the Australian welfare system since Federation. He really will have to stop making these kinds of moralistic pronouncements because so many of them are now coming back to haunt him. Even with the increases that this Bill provides for the first and second children, the real value of family allowances has declined by 15 per cent in the intervening six years. A family with five children today is $33.20 a month worse off than under the 1976 family allowance level. Had the 1976 family allowance level been indexed for inflation, a family with five children would today be $33.20 a week better off. Despite the increases of this year, the real value of family allowances has declined since 1976.
Mr Fisher-There weren't any at all before then.
Dr BLEWETT-Of course, we welcomed the family income supplement. But as the honourable member knows this was simply a Budget change;

this was a shift from one form to another in family support, which we supported at that time as probably more effective. Under the honourable member's Government it has become less effective. We all welcome the other side of the Government's family initiatives, that is, the family income supplement provision, but are worried by the very narrow base. The Government has used that extremely narrow base of the health care card to structure its family income supplement program and, therefore, it is most certainly missing out many very needy families in the community. The Labor Party places real importance on the value of the family and on providing it, in these extremely difficult times, with forms of support in the welfare area. That is why we will move in our amendment to the motion for the second reading that there should be increases in payments for children so that at least family allowances maintain their real value. In conclusion, I move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not declining to give the Bill a second reading, the House-
(I) deplores the Government's failure to protect the value of social security pensions, benefits and payments generally, and
(2) is of the opinion that the Bill should aIsoprovide for- (a) an end to the discrimination against the unemployed;
(b) greater increases in payments to the under-I 8 unemployed, and
(c) increases in payments for children to maintain their real value.'
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Drummond)- Is the amendment seconded?
Mrs Child-I second the amendment
Mr HARRIS (Chisholm) (4.6)-The purpose of the Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill is to implement the many worthwhile announcements made in the Budget in support of the needy and those most important groups in the community to whom we ought to give support. There are many new initiatives which will be of real help to large numbers in the community. The Budget recognises these needs. It also recognises the views widely held by many Government members. In particular I am very pleased to see the help provided in the Budget for families and for pensioners.
This Bill increases by 50 per cent the family allowance for the first two children. This Government is committed to the family. It is an area we must support. It is a vital part of our community which I hope we would all agree must be given every support and encouragement. The family is worth supporting not just as a worthwhile and,

1950 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill

indeed, vital institution but also as the best and often the most effective means of dealing with many of the problems which this community faces. The family allowance is a payment of great significance. It recognises the value of the family. It is said that it assists those who have means as well as those without means. I suppose that that is true but insofar as it is a direct cash payment it unquestionably supports and helps most those
who need it most. It is in fact a transfer from those who do not have children to those who do. It recognises the greater obligation and cost of maintaining children. I have on other occasions spoken in favour of weighting family allowance payments in favour of the first child. Although the amount payable for the first child is still less than for others this substantial increase, 50 per cent, is welcome. I hope that in succeeding years further increases will be weighted in favour of the first child, as that child has in most cases the greatest cost impact on the family.
In addition to the increases in family allowance
payments, from May 1983 an income tested nontaxable family income supplement will be introduced for low income families with children. The maximum rate of allowance will be $10 a week for each eligible child, which is equal to the additional pension or benefit paid in respect of children. The allowance will be income tested on the joint income of both parents. Payment of the supplement will be closely aligned to the disadvantaged persons health scheme for low income earners. Persons with an income below the threshold, currently equal to $184 a week, will be eligible to receive the maximum rate for each child. The threshold is the point at which a married couple without children will cease to be eligible for assistance under the disadvantaged persons health scheme as from November 1982.
In determining the eligibility for the family income supplement the maximum rate of assistance will be reduced by half of any income in excess of the threshold. Thus if the scheme were in operation in November 1982 a family with three children and an income of $200 a week would be eligible for a maximum payment of $30 a week less half the income in excess of $184 a week; that is, $8. So the family income supplement payable to that family would be $22 a week. Payment of the family income supplement will generally continue for a six-month period. As with the disadvantaged persons health scheme, there will be no reduction in the rate of the income supplement throughout that period if income during any four-week period does not exceed by more than 25 per cent the income at the time of determination. Entitlement will be redetermined at the end of a six-month

period. If a person's income falls at any time during the period he can seek an increase in the supplement. The legislation allows a three-month period of grace in which claims for the supplement can be made. Thus if a person is initially unaware of the availability of the new benefit he may get full payment from the date of introduction of the supplement provided he claims by 1 August next.
One cannot discuss the help given to families by
this legislation without taking into account the changes made in taxation arrangements. The spouse rebate has been increased by $200, which is of significant help to single income families. There are other measures. The income tax exemption limit has been raised by $400. The standard rate of tax has been reduced from 32c in the dollar to 30c in the dollar. These measures will have significant benefit for the average family. For example, the
family of a single income earner earning about the average income of $320 a week with two dependent children will be $15 or so a week better off. Such a family earning $200 a week will be $24 a week better off. Such a family earning $184 a week will be $32 a week better off. That represents an 18.5 per cent increase in disposable income for such a family. These figures indicate the value and the benefit of this Budget to families and also the weighting given to those who have family responsibilities and those who have average and lower incomes. Of course, all this is before taking into account the new tax rebate of 30 per cent on payments of home loan interest at rates above 10 per cent on loans up to $60,000. Interest rates, it is true, are too high. Many average families have had to pay significant interest bills and this rebate will be of great benefit to them.
The second area in which real help is provided is that of pensioners, especially age pensioners. I remind the House that decisions announced in the Budget complement the important measures first adopted by the Government in the 1976 Budget to protect and automatically cover against inflation most pensioners and beneficiaries who receive the basic rate of pension by making twice-yearly adjustments to pension rates linked to the consumer price index. It should be noted that this commitment is expensive. The November 1982 and May 1983 increases will cost about $770m in a full year. These regular adjustments are necessary for pensioners to meet inflation but involve large sums of money. It should be remembered, too, that as time goes on the number of people receiving the age pension will increase. However, several worthwhile additional measures, which I believe are of real benefit to pensioners, have been announced in this Budget. The allowable income

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1951

limit has been increased from $20 a week to $30 a we~k for single pensioners, which is a 50 per cent increase, and from $34.50 a week to $50 a week for couples. The fringe benefit income limit has also been increased from $40 a week to $54 a week for a single pensioner and from $68 a week to $90 a week for a couple. There can be little doubt that each of these measures is timely. The effect of inflation has eroded the value of the previous income limits.
It is sometimes said that affluent people are receiving pensions. If this is so it is not the result of the income limits. People who are well off and who receive the pension have achieved this by having their means in a form which does not attract the income test or, to put it another way, to which the income test cannot apply. Regardless of whether there are large numbers of these people, there are people who are entitled to greater assistance to whom these changes in the means test will apply. People who until now have been receiving, say, $40 a week in private income in addition to the pension have had difficulties. There can be no doubt of that. I am very glad that, by these measures, we have done something to improve their lot significantly. These measures will also give recognition to those who have made a contribution to their retirement and represent some incentive to them to continue to do so. They assist those pensioners who have part time work or some small superannuation policy. These initiatives will also encourage pensioners to use their resources to best advantage and to reduce the incidence of pensioners placing their assets in non-income producing forms so as not to lose their entitlement to fringe benefits.
I would like to give some examples of how these measures assist pensioners. A single pensioner under 70 years of age, that is the most numerous group, earning $54 a week private incomeincome additional to the pension-will in November be $11.53 a week better off and will also be entitled to fringe benefits. In November somebody with $40 a week private or additional income will be $12.26 a week better off, again gaining fringe benefits. Somebody receiving or earning $30 a week in addition to the pension will be $12.79 a week better off. That last person, of course, already has the pensioner health benefit card. Anybody can see from these examples that there have b~en significant advances for the aged and pensioners.
I mention the position of the married aged pensioner couples under 70 for they too will benefit significantly from the changes introduced in this

Budget. A couple who receive $90 a week additional income will in November be approximately $13 a week better off. Of course, they will also receive the fringe benefit of a health card. A couple with $50 a week additional income will be $14.36 a week better off and will receive the fringe benefit health card. A couple who are presently receiving $40 a week in additional income will be $15.81 a week better off in November. Of course, they would already be receiving a health card.
There are, of course, other benefits in the Budget for the pensioner. A new tax rebate for pensioners has been introduced. This will be $167 this year and $250 next year. This year the rebate will be reduced by 12.5c for each dollar earned above $5,007 per annum. This step, combined with a $400 increase in the tax free threshold, will ensure that pensioners with a pension and no other income will pay no tax at all. The effect of these measures is that a pensioner can have a total income, including a pension, of $5,429 a yearover $100 a week-without having to pay any tax. As I have said, the rebate reduces at the rate of 12.5c in the dollar but it does not cut out until income of around $7,000 a year is reached. As with the easing of the means test on pensions and on fringe benefits these steps represent a real improvement for pensioners. They also represent both an incentive and a reward for people to provide, to the extent that they are able, for their own retirement.
Further help for the pensioner is also provided in a new, three-tiered arrangement for pharmaceutical benefits. Those pensioners who have the health benefit card will continue to receive medicines on the same basis. Of course, following the relaxation of the income test, more pensioners will be entitled to that card. In addition, other pensioners without the card will no longer pay the $3.20 a prescription; the fee will be reduced to $2 a prescription. This is a real help to the pensioner, particularly the age pensioner, who so frequently calls on medical assistance. The personal care subsidy paid to organisations caring for frail aged people in approved hostels will be increased to $30 a week for each eligible person from 6 July this year. This is an increase of 50 per cent on the present level or 100 per cent since October 1980 when the rate was last increased from $15 to $20. It will help to ensure that a high standard of care continues to be provided. This subsidy helps to meet the costs of employing staff to provide additional services for frail aged residents, such as assistance with bathing or dressing, cleaning, personal laundry, supervision of medicine and the like. At the end of June 1982 a total of 780 hostels was receiving the subsidy which was helping to

1952 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill

meet the costs of additional services for approximately 21,700 frail aged. In 1982-83, $35m will be spent on this subsidy compared with an expenditure of $22.24m in 1981-82-an increase of 57 per cent.
The Commonwealth currently assists the maintenance of Meals on Wheels services by providing a subsidy of 45c for every meal with an approved vitamin C supplement and 40c for every other meal. These rates will be increased to 55c and 50c respectively from October 1982-an increase of 22 per cent and 25 per cent respectively. At the end of June 1982, 738 organisations were receiving the Commonwealth subsidy. In 1981-82 some 9,650,000 meals were delivered compared with 8,905,000 in 1980-81. A final matter to mention with regard to help for the aged is the increase in funds available for aged persons accommodation and elderly citizens centres. These, too, have been significantly increased. All of these measures which I have outlined clearly indicate the concern and the determination of this Government to give assistance to our aged and our pensioners.
Other initiatives in this Bill will be of benefit to the community. A mobility allowance has been introduced. This is payable to a substantially disabled person who is permanently or, in the long term, unable to use public transport by reason of his or her disabilities and who is engaged in gainful employment or undertaking vocational training. The allowance is expected to provide a positive incentive to severely disabled persons seeking to enter, re-enter or maintain themselves in the work force. It is payable at the rate of $10 a week. 'Gainful employment' will be taken to mean employment for 20 hours or more a week and will include sheltered and home-based employment. 'Vocational training' wi1l be taken to mean training for 20 hours or more a week in preparation for finding paid work, carrying on a profession, trade or business. It will include tertiary education and training at an adult training centre or activity therapy centre. Payment will be made to an individual or his or her agent. Such payments will not be regarded as assessable income for income tax or pension purposes. These payments can continue to be paid for up to three months after loss of employment or cessation of vocational training.
I refer now to transitional employment arrangements for invalid pensioners. To reduce disincentives which face those invalid pensioners and recipients of the sheltered employment allowance who contemplate the possibility of returning to the full time work force certain administrative and other initiatives are to be instituted. Those who are manifestly permanently incapacitated for work and lose their pension through employment

will be guaranteed reinstatement of pension without further medical examination if they are forced to give up work within 12 months. Those who are not manifestly incapacitated but lose their pension through their earnings will receive priority in assessment and determination of any claim for invalid pension or sheltered employment allowance if they are forced to cease work within 12 months. Such persons will also qualify for a health care card under the disadvanaged persons health scheme for a period of 12 months free of any income test. It is anticipated that these measures will prove to be of particular value to those recipients of the sheltered employment allowance who find that they are unable to continue in open employment and again seek to contribute to society through their employment in a sheltered situation.
I turn now to unemployment and sickness benefits. The rate of unemployment and sickness benefits for a single person under the age of 18 and the rate of the unemployment benefit for older persons without dependants are not indexed, as has been mentioned by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett). In the case of the person under the age of 18 the maximum rate has been increased by $4-from $36 to $40 a week. The rate of the unemployment benefit for people over 18 years of age without dependants was increased last year, making it equal to half the rate payable to a married couple. To maintain the situation, the rate will be increased from $58.10 a week to $64.40 a week. It is estimated that approximately 248,000 beneficiaries will receive increased payments through those changes.
In November 1980, a partially tapered income test was introduced for the unemployment and sickness benefits. That important initiative has enabled beneficiaries to supplement their incomes by casual employment and has given unemployed youth in particular an opportunity to gain some work experience. To cement the advantages gained by that initiative, the upper tapered limit will be increased to $60 a week for people under or over 18 years of age. In addition, the permissable income limits in the free areas will be raised from the existing $3 a week for a single person aged under 18 years and from $6 a week for the others to a standard $10 a week for all beneficiaries. Thus, a beneficiary can, by earning $60 a week, have an income equal to the maximum rate of benefit plus $35.

Mrs CHILD (Henty) (4.26)-It is with pleasure that I second the amendment moved by my colleague, the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett). I was interested to hear the honourable member for Chisholm (Mr Harris) speaking on this Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill. He devoted two minutes of his 20 minutes to speaking about unemployment and the unemployment benefit. At a time when this country is hit by half a million people unemployed and that number increasing, and probably half a million hidden unemployed, I do not really think that two minutes in a speech is quite enough to devote to such a massive problem facing the Australian people. He talked about young people being able to gain work experience and to earn a bit more to add to their pitiful income. I would like to know where those jobs are because they certainly are not available in my electorate. If they are available in his electorate I would be very surprised. Part time work disappeared a long time ago.
I wish to speak a little about the fact that pensioners do not pay tax on their spouse and children's allowances but people in receipt of the sickness benefit, special benefits and the unemployment benefit are doubly discriminated against by this Government in that they receive lower incomes and they are taxed as well. I find it a tremendous burden to put on to people that if they have more than three children they are taxed, only because they are paid additional amounts for their children. All the talk from the Government about families does not really apply to those living in poverty because we tax them for having kids. Those on the sickness benefit are also called on to pay tax on their supplementary allowance as well as their income.
Those on the unemployment benefit do not receive the supplementary rent assistance. They face the same costs as pensioners in private rent accommodation, but are given no rent relief either by the Department of Social Security or by State housing authorities. With rents as high as they are, if these people are getting this massive $64.40 a week, I will be damned if I know how they are paying their rent. Of course, they are not; they are being evicted all over the country because they cannot pay their rent. It is not because they do not want to, but the income they are receiving will not keep pace with the private rents of $80 a week to $95 a week. One cannot do this, particularly if one is a supporting parent. murder. It is a petty and outrageous discrimination against those on the sickness benefit and the unemployment benefit and pressures should be brought to bear on the Government to remove this discrimination.

The latest available figures indicate over half a million unemployed. Hidden unemployed would account for hundreds of thousands more. Of course, in those hundreds of thousands more women predominate. The average duration of unemployment these days, after seven years of a Liberal-National Country Party Government is 30 weeks, and still the Government will not grant supplementary assistance or fringe benefits to the unemployed. They have only the health care card which entitles them to free medical and hospital care in standard wards. Out of their miserable income they have to pay $2 for each prescription. For those who have chronic illness it becomes a nightmare. It is useless to tell people they can go to the doctor if they cannot afford then to go to the chemist and have their prescriptions made up. Considering the below poverty incomes, I believe it is particularly mean not to give the army of unemployed a pensioner health benefit card on the same basis as they are given to pensioners.

The amount of $40 represents a figure $37.25 a week below the single rate and is less than halfthe amount regarded as the poverty line. There is no recognition that many young people must live away from home for family reasons or, as my colleague pointed out earlier, the country kids come down to town to look for non-existent work. Nothing in the Budget explains how a 16 or 17-year-old can live on $5 a day. I am sure there are many people in the community who would be delighted if the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) could explain to them how to eke out their $5 a day so that they eat as well as have a roof over their heads. Who was it who said: 'We will be generous to those who want to work and who can't get a job'?

help at times of crisis, while the voluntary agencies have fewer resources with which to help them.
