Mr McLEAN (Perth) (4.35)-1 support the Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill, which brings together the social security and welfare initiatives contained in the Budget, which I think has done and will do much more for the average family and for pensioners than any other Budget in my memory. 1 think the Budget is less than perfect in an economic sense, but its social direction is sound inasmuch as it gives much needed support to the family unit, and it has tackled positively the major problems which pensioners have been bringing to my electorate office over a number of years.
1 have advocated in this Parliament for many years the provision of assistance to families in a way which would ensure that families, particularly single income families, do not suffer undue financial hardship as a result of having more children. 1have suggested that this needs to be done in a systematic, co-ordinated and professional manner by the use of sophisticated data-based family impact statements rather than ad hoc measures, as occurs at the present time. Certainly, I prefer ad hoc action to no action at all, but if we genuinely believe the family unit to be the natural and fundamental group unit in society, as I do, we really need to have a continuing assessment of its well-being. At the present time many Commonwealth Government departments have a direct effect financially and legally on family circumstances-the Department of the Treasury

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1955

through its taxation, fiscal and monetary policies and interest rates; the Department of Social Security through its various income support programs; the Department of Education through its responsibility to maintain freedom of choice in education; the Department of Veterans' Affairs; the Attorney-General's Department and others. The activities of these departments, together with the actions of State and local governments, all have an impact, for better or worse, on the capacity of families to manage their affairs.
At the present time government departments and different levels of government act independently of each other when it comes to something as specific as the family unit. I believe that family impact statements of the kind I have referred to previously in this place are necessary to provide a data standard from which decisions are taken in Commonwealth, State and local government budgets. The information is already available; it simply needs to be brought together from each level of government in each State. The data provided need not prove to be an imperative for action at any given time. It should simply be available as a standard against which well-informed and co-ordinated policy action could take place when it is possible for governments to do so. I again urge the Treasurer (Mr Howard) at least to examine the possibility of implementing this proposa\.
Although no such data was available for the preparation of the 1982-83 Budget there is no doubt that the variety of measures contained in it will greatly benefit the average family in Australia. Family allowances for the first and second child in a family rose by 50 per cent following the 50 per cent increase for third and subsequent children in the 1981-82 Budget. Given, as my colleague the honourable member for Chisholm (Mr Harris) has said, that the first child is the one who requires the most substantial capital outlay for a family, I regard this as a most sensible initiative by the Government. This means that over the past two Budgets a family of three children has seen family allowances rise by $31.45 a month and a family of four children has seen family allowances rise by $44.45 a month-very substantial increases and very necessary increases given the economic difficulties confronting most family units, particularly those with one income and a relatively new and large mortgage. The spouse rebate, which is not the subject of this legislation but which is an important companion to the thrust of this legislation, has been increased by $200 to $830 per annum.

The combination of the Budget measures incorporating the family allowance increases, the income tax reductions, the spouse rebate increases and the housing loan interest rate rebate means that the average family, consisting of a taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two dependent children and with a mortgage of $25,000 paying interest at 13t per cent and receiving an income of some $300 a week, is to receive an additional $19.84 a week. Even after the imposition of the indirect taxes announced in the Budget, that family will still benefit by some $16.84 a week-again a very substantial improvement. In my opinion it is a boost to the status of the family in Australia not only in a financial sense but also in terms of the recognition that this Government has accorded the family by giving it top priority for social welfare and taxation intitiatives. This is a priority with which I totally agree, although I again say that the family unit is in need not only of one-off adjustments of the kind which occurred for pensioners before automatic indexation but also of continuing assessment and remedial action of the kind which can be obtained only from authoritative ongoing family impact statements.
While on the subject of the family unit I state again that, although income support is important, the taxation system should give due recognition to the single income family by allowing such units to split their income for income tax purposes. I know that the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs and Minister Assisting the Minister for Social Security (Mr Wilson), who is at the table, has long been an advocate of this policy. These days we hear a lot about the morality of tax avoiders. If we are talking about morality rather than legalities, I must say that I see no morality in a situation where a family is able to split its income if the mother or father is engaged in a certain industry or profession while other families are denied such simple and substantial taxation benefits. No amount of change to the social security system in favour of average single income families will compensate for the bias which presently exists in the taxation system against the average wage and salary earning family. I have raised this issue also in this House over a long period. I will continue to do so until this discrimination against the family is removed from the taxation system.
The other aspect of this legislation which I support relates to the suppor;: given in the Budget to pensioners. Any honourable member of this House who seeks to keep himself or herself informed as to the problems of his or her constituents would agree that this Budget has successfully tackled the major problems confronting a great

1956 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill

number of pensioners. We all know that the income limits which determine eligibility for the maximum pension had not been increased since 1972. This has affected all pensioners. The lack of adjustment has basically proved to be an increasing disincentive for people approaching retiring age to make some provision from their own resources for their retirement. For example, a person with an indexed superannuation benefit would find that a cost of living adjustment to his superannuation would lead to some reduction in his pension. He would therefore suffer overall a real decrease in his combined income.
Clause 36 of the Bill will increase the income levels determining pension entitlement for aged and invalid pensioners by 50 per cent to $30 a week for single pensioners and by 45 per cent to $50 a week for pensioner couples. This measure means that some 430,000 pensioners throughout Australia with private incomes above the existing free areas will receive substantially higher pensions in the near future. I congratulate the Government for this much needed adjustment, which has certainly been long overdue. Certainly, this is not a measure which concentrates welfare expenditure on the neediest pensioners-those with no private income-although all pensions will be increased in November with indexation. Rental assistance will also be increased.
With a limit on available resources inevitably a choice had to be made by the Government between two conflicting principles, both reasonable and both requiring attention-one principle being the principle of concentrating resources on the neediest pensioners and the other being the principle of maintaining an official pension scheme which does not discourage people from making some provision for their own retirement. In my view an anti-poverty approach to welfare in some other areas and some reductions in lower priority Government expenditure could have provided the funds necessary to satisfy both of those principles in this Budget. Nevertheless, a matter of long standing and reasonable complaint by pensioners has been properly tackled by the Government. Although problems still remain in the pensions area few people would realise that a married couple in receipt of an age pension will be able to receive a total gross income of $178.80 a week before their pension is diminished on the basis of SOc for each dollar of non-pension income in excess of $50 a week. Their pension will not be totally withdrawn until their gross income reaches $307.60 a week. For single pensioners the comparable figures are $107.25 and $184.50 a week.

This legislation-in clause 59 of the Bill-also increases the income limits which determine pensioner entitlement to health benefits. These limits will be increased by 35 per cent to $54 a week for single pensioners and by 32 per cent to $90 a week for pensioner couples. Unlike the limits determining entitlement to the pension, these limits were previously adjusted only three years ago. Nevertheless, this action by the Government to increase the limits was necessary because pensioners with indexed private incomes were being placed in a position in which a small cost of living adjustment in their private incomes was causing a very large financial loss by removing them from entitlement to pensioner health benefits. These measures, combined with the Government's decision to lift the tax free threshold, which means that about 300,000 pensioners and low income earners who would otherwise pay tax in 1982-83 will not now do so, have substantially improved the lot of many pensioners throughout Australia.
Before making some general comments on the welfare area I want to refer briefly to the Delivered Meals Subsidy Act which basically keeps the Meals on Wheels operations going. Clause 22 of this Bill will increase the subsidy available from October 1982 from 45c to 55c per meal, where a vitamin C supplement is provided, and from 40c to SOc per meal in other cases. These increases are vitally important. In my electorate of Perth there are a number of organisations including the League of Home Help for Sick and Aged and various senior citizens' centres which provide a highly efficient meals on wheels service. The cost of producing these meals in relation to the price charged for them makes this kind of subsidy adjustment absolutely necessary if these organisations are to remain viable. This voluntary service is without equal anywhere in the world as far as I am aware. If eligible organisations are to retain their voluntary, private spirit we must ensure that they can provide their basic service at a good quality and at a reasonable price.
Without these periodic subsidy adjustments this important and underrated social service would collapse within its present structure and might have to be administered by government authorities which most organisers within these organisations feel would destroy the whole spirit and concept of the meals on wheels service. This program is important in meeting the requirements of so many aged people who prefer to stay in their own homes, in familiar surroundings, for as long as possible before having to move into institutional accommodation. This unique, decentralised, voluntary service involves in excess of 700 organisations throughout the country, providing some

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1957

9,650,000 meals in 1981-82. The magnitude and the nature of the service can be sustained only by governments ensuring the viability of these approved organisations through the kinds of subsidy adjustments contained in this legislation.
One could outline the many other initiatives undertaken by the Government in the area of increased benefits for disabled people and veterans and with respect to the improved funding of accommodation for the aged and the frail aged, but that is well documented. I simply make the point that those who suggest that this Government does not give due consideration to welfare matters would do well to examine the Budget aggregates. For 1982-83 the Government has committed $13.2 billion to social security and welfare. That is just for this coming financial year. That represents an increase of over 15 per cent on the previous finanial year. But more importantly it constitutes more than 28 per cent of total Budget outlays compared with 23 per cent in the last Australian Labor Party Budget. I think those figures provide a proper basis of comparison regarding the priorities the respective parties give to social welfare.
Under this Government Commonwealth Budget expenditure on social security and welfare has risen from $5 billion in the 1975-76 Labor Budget to in excess of $13 billion in the 1982-83 Budget. For a country of 15 million people, with one of the highest standards of living in the world, Australia still has a poverty problem, notwithstanding the scale of its expenditure on social security and welfare. It seems to me that the extent to which poverty exists in Australia is not so much a consequence of the level of funds directed to the social security area in successive Budgets. Rather it is related to the fact that so much of that expenditure is allocated on a universal basis with people receiving benefits according to the occurrence of some specified contingency-for example, having children-rather than relating those contingencies to levels of well-being amongst the prospective recipients. This Budget again reflected that trend, notwithstanding the fact that the universal approach inevitably, although accidentally, helps many classes oflow income people.
This Budget, through the family income supplement, increased rental assistance and increased meal and accommodation subsidies paid under the Homeless Persons Assistance Act did have some anti-poverty initiatives. But, overall, the universal approach to welfare has remained dominant. This has meant a less than optimum use of welfare funds if we make the elimination of poverty our top priority policy. I do not pretend that it is easy to change the emphasis or direction

of welfare spending. I am aware of the realities of this world. But the fact remains that with much of the welfare area automatically indexed and with the aging of the population it will not be possible on economic grounds, nor will it be justifiable on social justice grounds, to continue on the present course of ever-expanding universal welfare payments. A greater proportion of the nation's resources available for welfare expenditure will have to be allocated on a needs basis. If this does not occur the poor will remain relatively poor and all pensioners and welfare recipients will suffer as the nation's capacity to develop its living standards will be impeded. In the absence of a change in the funding emphasis higher expenditures will be accommodated only by ever-increasing taxation or by higher interest rates, if Budget deficits expand, and lower levels of economic growth will result.
On economic grounds and on social justice grounds this change of emphasis in welfare spending will have to occur if we are going to be able to confront successfully the poverty that exists in this nation. If we are to increase the standard rate of pension to, say, 25 per cent of average weekly earnings at a cost of something like $460m per annum; if we are to increase assistance to the poorest families and to the unemployed; if we are to elevate the funding status of the Homeless Persons Assistance Act, which takes only $6.7m this year out of a total welfare commitment of $13,272m; and if we are to do more in these and other anti-poverty areas, obviously in the future some other universal welfare programs will have to be modified on a needs basis. It is only in this way that social justice will be provided in the longer term. In the short term and in the context of the 1982 Budget. I congratulate the Government for this legislation which has certainly helped overcome many of the pressing welfare problems experienced in recent years by Australia's families and pensioners.
Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (4.56)-I rise to speak to the Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 1982. 1 support the amendment moved so ably by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) and seconded by my colleague the honourable member for Henty (Mrs Child). First, 1 shall comment on some of the points made by my colleague, the honourable member for Perth (Mr McLean), who philosophised as to why we have such a great amount of poverty in Australia today and surmised that it does not stem from any lack of welfare payments. Certainly, it is not just the lack of welfare payments that is causing the great extent of poverty. The sad thing is that an increase in social welfare payments has been

1958 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill

necessitated by the fact that bad management by the Government has caused more and more Australians to find it absolutely impossible to meet the increased costs that have come from extra sales tax, income tax, doctors' bills, education bills and all those necessities that should be their right. The situation is particularly difficult for those thrown out of work. The increases are necessary. After all, who wants handouts? No one would wish to live in this manner. People would rather have dignity and be able to afford all these
necessities.
Honourable members on the other side of the House spoke about family support and the top priority which it should receive from governments. I entirely agree with this proposition. But they and the Government they represent have made a glaring error in terms of human suffering. They have apparently neglected to realise that unemployed people are not alien creatures roaming a society in which they have no part and in which they have no rights. The most notable increase in the numbers receiving unemployment benefits is in the 25 to 44 years age group; that is, generally people with young families-the last people who would walk away from jobs. They are in the unemployed group because they are the victims of factory shutdowns and mine closures. These people are in the same situation as any pensioner couple but the Government refuses to allow them any fringe benefits apart from the health card, and most particularly will not give them a penny towards the rents they have to pay. It is part of the Fraser Government's philosophy of punishing the unemployed, no matter how undeserved.
The extent of the desperate plight of the unemployed has been outlined time and again in this chamber by members of the Opposition-it has been stressed today by my colleagues-but the Government has not responded. Its outdated and inadequate policies from a past age are unchanged. We still have no government-sponsored job creation scheme. We still, in the midst of climbing unemployment numbers-the worst since the Great Depression-are sadly stuck with a government which coined the term 'dole bludgers' yet refuses to give Australians seeking work anything other than a benefit which does not cover their needs. So the story of the unemployed must be told again and again, and it will be told until the Government's policies change or, even better, until there is a change of government.
At the height of the Great Depression a famous observer of the human condition wrote a book about the plight of London's thousands of homeless people or tramps, as he described them. The book describes his experiences and feelings as he

wandered the streets and stood in queues, dressed as a tramp dresses and living as tramps then lived. The book provided the average citizen of those times with an opportunity to learn of the fantastically disagreeable life, as the author described it, of an entire subculture of societal rejects. The name of the book is Down and Out in Paris and London and its author is George Orwell, arguably the greatest social reporter of this century.
Whilst the book makes fascinating reading, one would have thought that 50 years later the opportunities for this sort of reportage would have disappeared. I commented on Orwell's book because in a recent feature story in Brisbane's Courier Mail reporter Alan Fewster managed to paint a chilling Orwellian picture of that same subculture as it exists today in the sunshine State of our own lucky country. Entitled 'The Young and the Hopeless', the article outlines Mr Fewster's first hand observations of the lifestyles of the inhabitants of Brisbane's hostels for the homeless as he spent three depressing days moving among them. His impressions provide a stark warning that 1984 could ultimately resemble the picture Orwell painted of his own times rather than the authoritarian, one-dimensional society he prophesied in another famous book, 1984. I shall quote Mr Fewster's article because it is pertinent. Time and again we hear the plight of the unemployed and the plight of people living in poverty brushed aside as though they were part of some emotive picture conjured up by people who do not have the facts in their hands. The picture is indeed factual and very sad. Mr Fewster wrote:
As one who for some years has been used to earning a living wage, I found it impossible to exist without charity. My budget was just over $5 a day. Clearly I could not afford to rent a place or eat on that. The alternatives were the shelters or the streets.
Despite the knowledge that mine was only a sojourn among the unemployed, I had to make a more serious mental adjustment in order to deal with the psychological aspects of life on the dole. Depression was something I confronted. Faced with so much desperation, I became desperate myself. How was I going to get through my time? On occasions, it seemed a little like a jail sentence and on a practical level, what to do through the day?
Unemployment, I found is about loneliness and boredom. On $4 a day, you cannot go to the pictures or the pub. Many youngsters, however, can stretch their dole cheque to include a tube of aeroplane glue.
Unemployment is also about intellectual and emotional poverty. The IS year old who runs away from home and school and ends up in a hostel full of derelict alcoholics is starting life with little going for him. Virtually all his relationships are confined to the limits of his immediate environment.
This then is the picture of homeless youth in Brisbane today as it was confronted by one reporter. As the Senate Standing Committee on Social Welfare's report on homeless youth in Australia

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1959

noted, Brisbane has around 2,000 homeless kids roaming its streets while the number for Australia as a whole is over 12,000. The need for shelter for these people is desperate. Why has the need not been addressed in this Budget?
The Brisbane Telegraph of 17 August reported that the number of homeless young people seeking help in Melbourne has jumped 30 per cent in the past few months, and the situation will get worse when more young people come on to the job market this Christmas. Most of these were under 18 years and unemployed. According to the Courier-Mail of 21 August, records kept by the Salvation Army showed that the number of homeless and unemployed people in Brisbane under the age of 25 has increased 10 per cent since the beginning of the year. Forty two per cent of all admissions at the Salvation Army hostel for men and women are aged 18 or under. Forty two per cent of the people whom the Salvation Army is mopping up are young people-our young people in Australia-coming from decent homes with decent families. And nothing has been done in this Budget to look to their needs.

ing. It is also disgusting. The Senate Standing Committee on Social Welfare described the grow-
ing numbers of homeless youth as Australia's hidden problem. This Government's attitude to
homeless youth could quite obviously be described as one of hiding from the problem. This Government has, however, allocated $1.7m this

homeless young people in the rest of Australia?

One third of those who stay at the Brisbane St Vincent de Paul shelter for homeless men are under 25-it would make me speechless except the words have to be said-and one-sixth are under 18. Ten years ago the average age of those living at the Society's shelter was 48; today is it 32. Yet while the demand for crisis accommodation for homeless persons is soaring the supply is sadly lacking. The Brisbane Telegraph reported on 16 August that up to 40 poverty stricken people a week were being turned away from the Gold Coast's only hostel for the homeless. Most were aged between 16 and 25.
If these stories are any indication, the situation is clearly critical. A simple sense of humanity tells us that homeless young people should be at the top of any government's list of priorities. Yet when we look at this government's list of priorities in the Budget we find that Liberal-National Country Party concern for the plight of homeless unemployed people, whether young or old, is, like the success of the Government's economic policies, negligible.
Total outlays by way of subsidies to voluntary welfare organisations for the construction, purchase or maintenance of night shelters, reception centres and hostels for homeless persons will rise this year by a grand total of $400,000. This is the Federal Government's main form of financial support for homeless unemployed people who are

ment has totally ignored the problem of unem-
ployment in this country. Similarly, the Budget in-
dicates that the accommodation needs of unemployed people have also been ignored. The only sign that it recognises their plight was the de-
cision to increase the under-18 rate of unemploy-

should not be doing such immoral things.' They certainly should not be put into this situation but it is very poor when they are forced, by lack of
money, into situations like that. They are only

1960 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982 Social Security Legis/ation Amendment Bill

kids. The adults who put through the legislation turn their backs on them. Since the Government has still not chosen to index the new rate in line with the married rate of unemployment benefit the same situation will recur in May 1983. This practice is also increasing amongst 18-year olds, something the paltry increase to $40 in the junior rate of benefit will do nothing to prevent.
Perhaps the heaviest burden carried by unemployed people with a permanent roof of sorts over their heads is rent. But as has already been spelt out in this chamber, they will not get rent relief, so the homelessness must go on. Perhaps the masterpiece of confused priorities concerning the unemployed in this Budget is the 21 per cent increase in the allocation for job training programs. As the honourable member for Wills has pointed out in the lifetime of this Government, between 1975-76 and 1982-83, there has been a 38 per cent drop in real terms in expenditure. The irony in the 21 per cent increase this year, however, lies in the fact that with there being no projected growth in employment in 1982-83 this money will be used to train young people to go absolutely nowhere. There are job training schemes but no jobs-the ultimate irony. This Government should do what the Age newspaper did recently and survey experts in the field of employment for their views on the efficacy of these schemes. On 12 August the Age stated:
There is widespread agreement among the authorities contacted by the Age that the schemes run by the Federal Government to ease unemployment are inadequate, out of date and, in some cases, misdirected. There is no programme which creates jobs: schemes are all directed towards training unemployed workers.
Herein lies the tragedy with the schemes and the new guidelines to the Community Youth Support Scheme. I have dwelt on the very real and still growing plight of the homeless unemployed to focus on a relatively unpublicised area in which this Government has literally deserted people in physical need. However, there is another group in society which is also being kicked in the teeth by the Fraser Government. That group comprises the pensioners of this country who receive little or no private income. I acknowledge the extent to which this Budget has a last gone towards relieving certain aspects of the bad conditions. with which the pensioners have been burdened. But the needy pensioner without extra income has been given no relief and, in fact, has been put into a worse situation. When we consider the nature of the Budget decisions affecting pensioners we can see how this is so. Mr Fraser, in his 1975 policy speech said--

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)-The honourable member will refer to the Prime Minister as such.
Mrs DARLING-The Prime Minister, in his 1975 policy speech, said:
The real value of pensions will be preserved.
This has not happened. Under the Labor Government, between 1972 and 1975, pensions rose almost twice as fast as the consumer price index. Under the Liberals they have been indexed in line with the consumer price index but several months later, in spite of the promise the Prime Minister made that there would be no lag, the pension has now declined to less than 22 per cent of average weekly earnings. Unless there is an additional boost to the standard rate pension it will go back to the 18.6 per cent which it was in Mr McMahon's day before the Labor Government gave substantial rises twice-yearly. The drop in the pension compared with community living standards demonstrates that this Government has no commitment to giving pensioners an adequate income. The increase still only covers pensioners for the higher prices they paid between January and June 1982. From the point of view of the average pensioner who has received less private income than was allowable under the old income test, the extensions to these limits are meaningless, particularly if these people are of an age or possess a degree of invalidity which renders them in-
capable of work. In other words, pensioners and beneficiaries who are too frail or incapacitated to work, who are too poor to have substantial private investments or who are fully tied up at home caring for their children will receive absolutely no benefit from the decisions.
It does not stop there. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) also made much in his speech about the benefits for pensioners of the higher tax threshold
and pensioner tax rebate. Yet it should be immediately obvious to most members of this chamber that neither of these measures would be of any assistance at all to pensioners who do not pay tax. As luck would have it, many of the pensioners not yet liable for tax are the same pensioners I spoke of earlier who will not receive any benefits from the easing of the pensioner income tests. The simple fact is that pensioners having to subsist solely, or almost solely, on the basic pension receive an annual income well below the present tax threshold. For the financial year just ended, a single pensioner could have received extra income up to an average of $11.30 a week for the entire year before becoming liable for tax. These people, living in utter poverty, can receive no joy from the news of higher tax thresholds and special tax rebates and most certainly will get

Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill 13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1961

nothing out of the raising of the pension income test limits. This is the giant hoax perpetrated on our pensioners. This is the kick in the teeth which down and outers have been delivered by this Budget and which was also delivered to our homeless unemployed youth. Yet not content with delivering these body blows the Government, in its infinite wisdom, also decided to put up indirect taxes and slap on a bank transactions tax. As these people purchase goods with their few coins and go to cash their cheques at the bank they will also feel the sting of this neat uppercut; and this Government dares to use the word 'compassion' in its political lexicon.
The plain fact is that while total expenditure on social welfare in this Budget is estimated to rise in real terms the major part of the increase will be taken up by the automatic six-monthly rises already in place and by the huge increase in unemployment beneficiaries caused by a government-induced recession. The people who benefit from the belated largesse of this Government are certainly deserving of the relief that they are to receive. I am worried about our neediest citizens who have been trampled on by this Government in its rush to win votes. Their only crime is that they have not been identified in Liberal Party opinion polls as swinging voters.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (5.15)-The Social Security Legislation Amendment Bill has some cross-connection with a number of other Bills such as the Aged or Disabled Persons Homes Act, the Compensation (Commonwealth Government Employees) Act, Delivered Meals Subsidy Act and of course the Social Security Legislation Act itself. This legislation is the vehicle by which the Government gives effect to its budgetary commitments in the social security area. It is a piece of legislation that affects a vast number of people. I suppose it is for that reason that the Government has allowed some latitude in this debate particularly taking into account the fact that it concerns some of the most needy people in the Australian community. Mr Kent-And in a very able way.

We know there is some limit to what can be paid in tax and that there has to be enough money left over to give people an incentive to work. Under the Whitlam Government, the means test was lifted first of all for people over 75 years of age and then in 1975 lifted for the people over 70 years of age. Later, the whole means test arrangements were changed so that we disregarded the process of taking into account the value of a person's assets. In the end we took into account only the value of income. This has resulted in a change in superannuation arrangements so that people are receiving what we call the golden handshake or a lump sum payment instead of periodical payments. This has the effect of increasing the pension bill. I suppose that is a proper thing to consider in the proper context. The honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), who led for the Opposition in this debate, pointed out that, despite the increase in the pension rates which is to take place from I November, there is no effective increase in the living standards of Australia's pensioners. Here we engage in another bit of skulduggery or trickery. We talk about the pension being a proportion of average weekly earnings, but the people receiving average weekly earnings are themselves subject to periodical conning. They are deprived of wage indexation. They are deprived of taxation indexation. Their living standards are shot down because of changes in the health scheme and matters of that kind. After they have all been tricked and humbugged, we then relate pensions to that false level of earnings. Even in that situation it is interesting to note that the standard rate of pension, which was 24.5 per cent of average weekly earnings in 1975, is at present only 21.9 per cent of average weekly earnings and after the November increase will rise to 22 per cent. So no effective progress is being achieved in this respect.
The very fact that the adjustments are made on a twice-yearly basis in itself represents a serious loss to pensioners because the consumer price index is moving up steadfastly. It would be a considerable improvement-the Opposition is considering making this proposal an aspect of its social security policy-to index pensions quarterly rather than half-yearly. The loss to the pensioner community runs to many millions of dollars and to a very significant figure for each individual pensioner. I see that we now call permissible income 'the free .area limit', a new bit of terminology about which the pensioner community is confused. The amount of permissible earnings a

pensioner can have before the pension is reduced has been increased for the first time since 1972. We welcome, as do the pensioners, the increase in permissible income or free area income for single pensioners from $20 to $30 a week and for a married pensioner couple from $34.50 to $50 a week. But this does not go anywhere near redressing the reduction in real terms and there is no proposal to index this free area income or these permissable earnings. The limits may not be increased again for quite some time, so it seems apparent that pensioners will suffer a further decline in the not too distant future because of the inadequacy of the Government's approach to this matter by not providing indexation.
There is a similar problem in respect of fringe benefits. Not everybody knows that two means tests are applied to pensioners under the social security laws-one to determine how much pension one gets and one to determine whether one is eligible for free benefits. There has been an increase also under this free benefits means test. It has gone up in respect of a single pensioner from $40 to $56 a week and for married people from $68 to $90. But once again there is no indexation and pensioners will be granted fringe benefits only to find them cancelled out because of a cost of living increase in their superannuation benefits. One moment they have it and they feel very secure in that they can go to hospital and get medical care, free pharmaceutical benefits and the benefit of all the other things which go with the pensioner medical card, and suddenly simply because of a consumer price index increase in their superannuation or some other increase they lose out. Every honourable member who acts diligently in his or her electorate would experience from time to time the anxiety of those people. I think that matter should be looked at so that in the future it is put on a more secure basis than it has been in the past.

think that is good enough. I am told that, while they are to be given this $2 a week extra, rentals at the bottom of the market are rising annually by more than $10 a week. REPATRIATION LEGISLATION AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 23 September, on motion by Mr Thomson:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (5.36)-The Opposition welcomes most of the features of this Bill. Indeed, it is the most flattering repatriation legislation which has been brought down in a long time in that it mirrors so clearly key aspects of the Australian Labor Party's platform. Indeed, it embodies the policies for which we have argued strongly in this House and in the other place. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the Australian Labor Party's recently determined platform on veterans' affairs policy which was put through at the July conference and in which honourable members will recognise most of the measures introduced in the Government's August Budget.
Leave granted.

The document read as follows-
AUSTRALIAN LABOR PARTY 1982 BIENNIAL CONFERENCE-CANBERRA-5-9 JULY
Veterans' Affairs Policy
A Labor Government will:
extend liberal treatment to all ex-service personnel disabled as a result of war service and to their dependants;
review recent changes to the repatriation system which reduce long-standing benefits; overhaul the appeals system to overcome long delays in hearing and determinations and restore the role of the Repatriation Review Tribunal to hear and make determinations on new evidence;
reduce the waiting period for current applicants for Defence Service Home Loans; widen the eligibility provisions of such loans so as to ensure justice for ex-servicewomen and maintain the level of loans in line with increasing costs of home ownership;
provide for at least half yearly indexation of all repatriation benefit payments;
exclude war pensions, including the general and special rate, and family allowances, from consideration as income for the payment of Service pensions or corresponding Social Service pensions; to be implemented within three years with substantial relief being granted in the first year;
grant to Allied ex-service personnel who fought with Allied forces during the Second World War entitlement to pension and treatment rights on the same basis as British ex-service personnel and extend fringe benefits (such as medical and hospital treatment and transport and telephone concessions) to both British and Allied ex-service personnel;
extend to Allied ex-merchant mariners the same entitlements as those received by Australian ex-merchant mariners;
recognise the special needs of Vietnam veterans and their families and extend counselling services for them;
introduce legislation relating to overseas service so as to provide equality of repatriation entitlements for veterans of all wars, designated campaigns, or peacekeeping duties, at least equivalent to those now enjoyed by World War Two veterans;
provide relief and treatment, where appropriate, for wives of TPI veterans; and
institute an independent judicial enquiry into the effects of herbicides and pesticides on Vietnam veterans.
Mr Thomson-We decided well before that.
Dr BLEWETT-The Minister now tells me that he had decided long before that. Let us just deal with that argument. I remind him and the House that 12 months ago the Opposition moved with reference to the Repatriation Acts Amendment Bill 1981, that is, the Bill introduced last year which was comparable to this Bill, that the Bill be redrafted, inter alia, to:
Provide repatriation benefits to those who served in peacekeeping forces before 1 November 1981.
This was rejected by the Government in 1981. Only 12 months ago it refused to incorporate it. We are very glad to see today in this Bill that the Opposition's policy has now been adopted. Again we moved last year that the Bill be redrafted to:

Repatriation Legis/ation Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1965

Provide eligibility for service pensions for all Allied exmerchant mariners as is provided for Allied ex-servicemen.
Again a year ago our amendment was rejected by the Government. Again the Government in this Bill has adopted Labor policy. Of course, the Government is saying that it made its decision some months ago. Let us go back only a few months to March 1982. Again in March 1982, at the instance of my colleague the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding), the Opposition moved that the Repatriation Acts be amended to extend the broad eligibility criterion for disability pension included in the World War II provisions, that is, the 'arisen out of or attributable to' provision, to veterans from World War I and the Malaysian and Vietnam campaigns. In March this year-that was not many months ago-the Opposition's amendment was again rejected by the Government. Now, six months later, we find that this part of Labor's policy is included in this year's Bill although, as I shall point out, I am not very clear as to whether the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson), given what was said in the second reading speech, understands the provision. I must say that while imitation is the subtlest form of flattery I am amazed at the unblushing way in which the Government is now touting as reforms those principles that it rejected summarily 12 months or even six months ago.
Let us now look at each of these Labor reforms which the Government has decided to embody in this Bill. First of all I refer to the peacekeeping forces provision. I think there will be universal acceptance of the Government's decision to extend eligibility for repatriation benefits to members of peacekeeping forces who served as representatives of Australia prior to 2 November 1981. I can certainly say on behalf of the Labor Party and I think on behalf of most thinking Australians that once the Government decided to extend such eligibility to members of the Sinai peacekeeping force the logic of extending it to members of prior peacekeeping forces was recognised. It is true that as usual the Government took some time to recognise this logic but we now welcome its belated acknowledgment. There is, of course, in the Minister's second reading speech no confession of this lack of logic. Indeed, it is interesting to see that there is no explanation whatsoever as to why what is now possible was.not possible six months or a year ago.
Again in the Minister's second reading speech there is no praise for the quiet herosim of those long forgotten and now suddenly remembered Australians who served Australia in peacekeeping

and monitoring operations over the last generation. No sympathetic understanding is reflected anywhere in the speeches of either the Minister in this House or the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) in the other place of the unreported hazards of peacekeeping operations.
There is no apology for the years in which those who served in peacekeeping forces have been kept in limbo and no listing of the places in which they served out of sight and, to all intents and purposes, out of mind. The Opposition wishes to remedy that defect and let this Parliament record Australia's debt to those who served in peacekeeping and monitoring operations. I wish to present a list of those operations. I am not sure that it is complete but it is the best that we have been able to put together and as the Government has not offered this Parliament or the nation any such list at all, at least it is a first attempt.
First of all there was Australia's participation in the United Nations Truce Supervisory Organisation in the Middle East from 1956 to the present day. There was our participation in the United Nations Emergency Force II in the Suez Canal area from 1973 to 1980, our participation in the
Kashmir peacekeeping force 1949, I think again to the present day, and Australia's participation in 1963-64 in the observer mission that was sent to the Yemen as a result of the United Nations intervention there. In Cyprus, Australia has participated through its police forces in the United Nations Civilian Police Force from 1964 to the present day. In Zimbabwe, as I saw at first hand, the Australians played a particularly useful role in the peacekeeping operation during the election in 1980. From 1947 to 1951 Australia participated in the peacekeeping groups in Greece following the civil war. Similarly there was Australian participation in the peacekeeping force in Indonesia from 1947 to 1951 following the war against the Dutch. There is, of course, the present Sinai force. I believe we sent a small contingent to the peacekeeping operations in the Lebanon in 1958 and, as I am informed at the moment, we also sent a small contingent to the United Nations forces during the time of troubles in the Congo between 1960 and 1964. I think this is a record of which this country can be very proud. We welcome very much the establishment in this legislation of the eligibility of the participants in those forces.
The second issue to which I wish to draw attention is that of the amendments relating to the 'arisen out of' provisions which, of course, the Labor Party supports as it advocated them in this House six months ago. It is implied in the second reading speech that only Vietnam veterans are not presently covered by the more generous 'arisen

1966 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill

out of' or 'attributable to' provisions for disability
pension eligibility but at the moment it is only World War II veterans who are so covered. All other veterans-veterans of World War I and of the wars in Korea, Malaysia and Vietnam-are covered only by the more limiting occurrence provision. The Labor Party supports this extension because we believe not only that all veterans should be treated fairly but also that they should be treated compassionately, that men who have served their country in war should be given the benefit of the doubt.
The Minister tended to disparage the need for this amendment, alleging that very few instances were involved which would not be covered by the already existing occurrence provision. My reply to that is as follows: Firstly, even if there are very few instances, each of those instances are important and count and the discrimination should be remedied. Secondly, if the Minister examines the appeal cases in particular he will discover that sufficient tragic cases have occurred to justify providing the broader category, that is, the 'arisen out of' provision, to all veterans and not just to World War II veterans. Thirdly, interestingly enough, the Government has never produced any argument as to why the 'arisen out of' or 'attributable to' provision was limited to World War II veterans. When this was raised with the Minister in this House in March he seemed to have no answer. Perhaps because he had no answer then he decided something should be done about it now. Therefore, the Australian Labor Party welcomes very much the correction of this anomaly.
Furthermore, it is good at last to have an acknowledgment from the Government, particularly from a Liberal government whose predecessors have involved us in practically every war we have ever fought-though usually we have had to get them out of them-that, as the Minister said, the Government considers that the same principles of eligibility should apply to all veterans for all conflicts. We accept that. We believe that that is a desirable statement. We hope that the rider that was added by the Minister was added in good faith. I quote the rider: 'unless there is good reason to introduce specific principles and provisions for particular conflicts or for particular groups of veterans'. We trust that that rider will be interpreted only in a compassionate direction and not in a limiting direction.
This legislation also resolves at long last the long battle of merchant mariners who served in theatres of war during World War II. They can now be covered by this legislation. It has been a pretty tough struggle for those merchant mariners but it has been finally brought to a successful, if

somewhat belated, conclusion. It is now, of course, nearly 40 years since that conflict ceased. I cannot for the life of me understand why when the Government last year finally decided to cover Australian merchant mariners it did not then cover Commonwealth and allied merchant mariners. It had previously established that principle for covering Commonwealth and allied soldiers. When it caught up belatedly last year by providing cover for Australian merchant mariners it seemed to me to be an utterly simple task to cover Commonwealth mariners. Indeed, when one looks at the amendments in this Bill one sees that it was afl utterly simple task to include Commonwealth and allied merchant mariners. Certainly the lame answers provided by the Minister last year were just so much poppycock. I am reminded here of the words of John Curtin, recalled by a merchant mariner's wife during the debate on this matter. Curtin said:
They wear no uniform but just a badge with the letters M.N. When you see it raise your hat to the wearer.
At least those who wore that badge, whatever language it was in, will now have a tangible reminder of the gratitude of all of us.
Finally, I refer to two items about which the Labor Party is critical as not having been included in repatriation legislation-two matters which again we have raised frequently in this House. We believe ultimately-although I suppose there is no ultimately now for this Government-that the neglect of the wives of the totally and permanently incapacitated ex-servicemen will be corrected. I admit that this issue was not raised by the Returned Services League in its pre-Budget submissions though that I do not think is an excuse for not considering it. Certainly the issue was not dealt with in what, in many ways, is a generous Bill. Yet I think there is a need for recognising the role played by TPI wives and the great demands often placed upon them to the detriment of their own health and well-being in caring for their husbands. I think there is a need for some precise and specific responses from the Government to alleviate this position and to provide support for these wives. I know that the various organisations concerned have prepared proposals and I think we need to look at the responses to those requestsresponses which certainly are not yet apparent in the Government's repatriation legislation.
The other feature of the second reading amendment which the Opposition will move relates of course to the judicial inquiry into the effect of herbicides and pesticides on veterans who served in Vietnam. We make no apology for raising this issue again in the Parliament. The need is now overwhelming to cut through the procrastinations

Repatriation Legis/ation Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1967

and delays on this matter that have characterised government responses to Vietnam veterans. What is required is not further, prolonged research but a proper and prompt judicial assessment of the available evidence. A motion to this effect was actually passed in the Senate last year only to be cynically ignored by this Government. The demand for a judicial inquiry is the now all but unanimous policy of the Conference of ExService Organisations, although perhaps the RSL could be a little more resolute on this matter. It seems to me extraordinary that a government which can have inquiries into everything under the sun-into dubious meat carcasses, possible electoral rigging and the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union-refuses to open the Pandora's box which contains this legacy of the Vietnam war. If the Government can respond to its many troubles with all sorts of judicial inquiries why cannot it respond in the same way to the demands of those who served in Vietnam? Therefore, on behalf of the Opposition, I move the following amendment:
That all words after That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not declining to give the Bill a second reading. the House is of the opinion that the Bill should provide for--
(I) increased benefits for the wives of totally and permanently incapacitated veterans, and
(2) an immediate independent judicial inquiry into the effect on Vietnam veterans of their exposure to herbicides and pesticides.'
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)-ls the amendment seconded?
Mr Holding-Mr Deputy Speaker, I second the amendment.
Mr WHITE (McPherson) (5.53)-1 am very pleased that the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), generally speaking, supports the thrust of the Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill, which deserves the support of honourable members on both sides of this House. I would not disagree-I do not think many honourable members would-that many of the provisions in this Bill are perhaps somewhat overdue. Nevertheless, they are before us and they are most welcome. A whole range of measures in this Bill are to be applauded. I so applaud the Government and congratulate the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) for bringing them forward.
We have a repatriation system in this country of which I believe we should be very proud. That does not mean that it does not have some anomalies, nor does it mean that as time moves on and circumstances change we should let it fall into disrepair. The fact is that the responsibilities of

the Department of Veterans' Affairs are increasing and not decreasing as more and more veterans come within its area of responsibility and as veterans get older and need more care. I particularly applaud the main provisions of the Bill. The honourable member for Bonython has covered most of them and I do not intend to deal with them in any detail. I welcome the provision of repatriation benefits to members of peacekeeping forces prior to November 1981. As the honourable member for Bonython has said, Australia has been engaged in considerable peacekeeping forces over the years. I expect that this provision might cover up to 4,000 members of peacekeeping forces although I hope that not all of them are in such dire need that they will come forward to make claims.
We welcome the extension of the pension eligibility to merchant mariners of Commonwealth and allied countries. The House will remember that last year this provision was extended to Australian merchant mariners. There have been arguments over the years-I think it is a great pity that it has taken nearly 40 years to resolve this issuethat the merchant mariners did not bear the same brunt as people in the Services. To an extent some of those arguments are valid. It is true that the merchant mariners were not in uniform as such; they were not servicemen. Generally speaking, they were free to come and go as they were paid off. In some cases their pay was better. Nevertheless, many of them suffered great hardships and the dangers that all servicemen suffered in war theatres. Of course, some suffered a great deal more. Some were taken as prisoners of war. The great majority of these merchant mariners did their jobs as was expected of them. I am very pleased to see-I believe it is high time-the recognition that we are paying not only to Australian merchant mariners but also to those who served in Commonwealth and allied navies. Many Australians served in Commonwealth and allied merchant marine services.
The disability pension, 100 per cent of which will now be discounted for Service pension purposes, is a step forward. It is a disability pension, paid for the disability that someone has suffered. I do not think that a person who is in receipt of that pension should be taxed twice on it, so to speak. For some time tuberculosis pensioners have fought a hard and very astute fight to get their pensions restored. I fully support that measure. Most of them, prior to 1978, suffered great disability with that disease, with long cures, low insurance and hardship in getting employment. In some cases they were virtually outcasts from the society in which they lived. It is different now,

1968 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill

thank God. The treatment is quicker and more successful. But I think the Government was remiss in freezing that pension as it did in 1978.
The level of extra allowable income has moved up in line with social security movements; that is, from $20 to $30 for a single man and the same rate for married people. Again, that was overdue, not only for veterans but I believe also for people in receipt of social security benefits. The fact is, of course, that these days most benefits are indexed. It has been most unfair to freeze the level of extra allowable income. The 'arisen out of or 'attributable to' provisions will now be evened out across the board. That is most welcome. There should now be no difference in the treatment of veterans of different wars. I hope that proves to be the case.
I think some criticism is justified of the time it has taken the Government to move on the matter of Vietnam veterans. Of course, unfortunately, that matter has not yet been brought to a successful conclusion. Nevertheless, I think it is fair to say that the Australian Government is in the forefront in the treatment of Vietnam veterans now. Of all the countries I know that have undertaken projects to assist veterans of the Vietnam war Australia must be in the lead in its treatment of those people. The counselling centres that have been set up have been most successful. Studies are still going on. It could be argued that they should have been completed a long time ago. Perhaps that is right, but the fact is that they were not. They have now been undertaken. The report of the Senate inquiry should be presented by the end of November.
Mr Holding-You would support a judicial inquiry, wouldn't you?
Mr WHITE-I would not. One departmental study-the one into birth defects among children-should be completed by the end of this year. The study into the mortality of veterans should be completed next year. I think it would be very sensible to wait until we see the results of those studies instead of rushing into another judicial inquiry which, in my view, would be only cosmetic.
Mr Holding-You are a great disappointment.
Mr WHITE-lam glad I am a disappointment. I would hate to be one of the honourable member's friends. To be called a great disappointment by him is something of an accolade. I urge the Government to look at the long delays which are still inherent in the system. Some of them have been caused by representations received from the Returned Services League of Australia and others requesting that the system be amended. But the

fact is that the system we have, good as it is, is not coping with the people who are trying to use it. The delays are, I believe, unacceptable. I strongly urge the Government and the Minister to look at the total repatriation system of claims and benefits with a view to rewriting and revising it completely because what was good enough 40 years ago perhaps is not good enough now. It is hardly the fault of the veterans that they are caught up in a system which is imposing great delays on the processing of their claims and allowances. In conclusion, I again congratulate the Government and the Minister for bringing forward these measures. I urge the Department of Veterans' Affairs, the Minister and the Government to look at a total rewriting of the repatriation system, which has a lot of anomalies. I am sure it can be improved, particularly to allow the claims and appeals to be processed more quickly than at present.

Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (8.0)-The honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) has clearly put the view of the Opposition and indicated that the substantive amendments contained in the Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill are supported by the Opposition. Indeed, many of the present proposals were actually canvassed by way of an amendment moved by the Opposition in March earlier this year. That amendment was, of course, on that occasion defeated by the Government by the use of its superior numbers in this House. We welcome the change of heart. Perhaps it was not adequately explained at the time why the Government was taking the view that it then took. Nevertheless, I do not believe that we should be churlish about it. As far as we are concerned, it is better late than never.
That does not mean as far as I am concerned that this House should not address itself very seriously to the amendment moved by the honourable member for Bonython. I want to deal with that aspect of the amendment which refers to the need for a judicial inquiry into the effects of herbicidal spraying and the use of defoliants in Vietnam and their impact upon Vietnam veterans and their families. When I said by way of interjection to the honourable member for McPherson (Mr White) that I was disappointed with his contribution to this debate, I was not making a personal reflection. What I was seeking to do was to point out to the honourable member who himself had a distinguished record in Vietnam and who holds what would be regarded on any view of the matter as a very safe seat for the Government that I

Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1969

would have hoped that, as a result of his involvement, he would have been concerned to examine the plight and the problems of Vietnam veterans and to look at the mass of evidence which has been brought to public attention by the Vietnam veterans' organisation. My hope was that he would have been concerned to use his own knowledge and background and be influenced by his own natural sentiment. The fact that he holds a position within the Government parties caused me to hope also that he would use his voice on this occasion to remind the Government that this very important group of ex-servicemen has become a group of warriors forgotten by this Government.
The same patriotic appeals that we heard from various members of the Government when young Australians were conscripted or were part of the regular armed Services to go to Vietnam, the same concern and the same patriotic fervour have not been evident when these men who, on their own behalf and on behalf of their families, have asked for a judicial inquiry. They have not asked for any automatic compensation. On not one occasion have they ever demanded compensation as a right. On the basis of their experience, on the basis of suicide rates that are grossly disproportionate within their ranks and on the basis of continued illness, onsets of carcinoma and other diseases which are clearly related to chemical spraying, the Vietnam veterans have asked this Parliament and this Government time and time again for a judicial examination of their problems so that all the evidence can be considered. I have not heard to date from any Minister or any other Government member an argument that suggests that that is somehow an unreasonable request.
Why should the only people in this community who get the benefit of judicial inquiries or royal commissions be people who have broken the law? Why should the only people who are the subject of this kind of interrogation, this judicial process, which is essentially a process designed to examine the evidence and pursue it wherever it may lead, be the tax evaders or members of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union of Australia? Here we have one group of Australian citizens who have been saying to this Government for many years: 'We believe that, as a result of our service and our perception of our service to this country, something should be done'. It was not their political decision that placed them in Vietnam; it was the decision of Ministers sitting opposite. But, having endeavoured to discharge, to the best of their ability, their duties as members of the armed Services, they came back to Australia. They now find themselves suffering a range of diseases. In many cases they are concerned that their

exposure to those defoliants has not only affected them but also caused serious defects in their children and has produced an incredible range of pressures and tensions in their domestic situations.
The Vietnam veterans have said to this Government on the basis of scientific and medical evidence available to them: 'We believe we are entitled to have a royal commission which will test the evidence that we will bring. We will bring our scientific advisers. We will bring our medical advisers. They can give their evidence under oath and be subject to cross-examination'. Is that an unreasonable request? They have added: 'Let the Government, the Department of Defence and the Department of Veterans' Affairs which have spent thousands and thousands of taxpayers' dollars resisting this argument bring their experts to be subject to the same adversary process, the same process of cross-examination and then let the royal commission decide where the truth lies'. In Australia where we have a Prime Minister who recently felt the need to talk about fairness and equity, has there ever been a fairer question posed to any government? Is it not a matter of equity that this group without a single paid organiser and with a membership the greater proportion of whom suffers a range of illnesses, with its lack of resources, has virtually had to fight this Government for every concession that has been won? It is not good enough for the honourable member for McPherson to stand up and to say: 'Oh, this Government has treated Vietnam veterans better than any government in the world'.
I refer the House to a recent article in the Melbourne Herald by Bruce Wilson in Washington. He describes the attitude of the Reagan Administration and the Veterans' Administration in the United States of America. What has been their approach? It is the same as this Government's. Mr Wilson states:
. . . it has now become quite clear that the VA's-
That is, Veterans' Administration-
real aim is to stall. stall and then stall again.
He points out that a group of American Congressmen is now so concerned about the stalling capacities of the Veterans' Administration in the United States that it is now combining within the resources of the Congress to take the administration of those problems away from the Veterans' Administration. Is the situation any different here? The only difference, I suppose, is that in the United States there are some Congressmen across party lines who, as a matter of conscience, are not prepared to tolerate an attitude which means stall, stall and stall again. The same terms could

1970 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill

be aptly applied to the attitude of the present Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) and the Department of Veterans' Affairs. He talked about the achievement of counselling services as though they were some beneficial handout to veterans by this Government. How did counselling services come about? The Minister for Defence Support (Mr Viner), who is sitting at the table and who is, I believe, a man of some conscience on this matter, knows as well as I do that counselling services did not come about as a result of any initiative within the Government or the Department of Veterans' Affairs. They came about because one more veteran hung himself and there was a pile of correspondence from an anguished widow to the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) pointing out that this was likely to happen again. In that situation, as a result of the pressure from Vietnam veterans and their organisation, counselling services were created.
On the last occasion this matter was debated the Minister who is sitting at the table cited in this House figures that were wrong. He knows now that they were wrong. I do not believe he did so deliberately. I have enough good grace and enough regard for the Minister to say that he was fed the same misinformation from the Department which marks that Department's continued approach to this matter. How can we talk about equity and justice? The Minister or any member of the Government could not deny that, as a result of the existence of the Vietnam Veterans Association, of its seeking scientific evidence from America and of the capacity of its scientific adviser whose view was accepted by the Repatriation Review Tribunal, Colin Simpson's cancer was found to be caused by his exposure to toxic chemicals while in Vietnam. Therefore the Tribunal found in favour of his family.
What would have happened if the Vietnam Veterans Association had been prepared to accept the glib assertions of Ministers on behalf of this Government? If it had not been prepared, with its meagre resources and its honorary membership, to chase to America for evidence which in this case gave justice to a family that would have preferred the life of the father, this would not have happened. We cannot bring him back. For how long will the future of so many of these families have to rest upon the capacity of this organisation which, time and time again in its representations to the Minister for Veterans'Affairs, is subjected to continual and wilful misrepresentation of its cause? We are told that there is a Senate inquiry. How did we get a Senate inquiry? Every member of the Liberal Party voted against having any inquiry at all.

Mr Scott-They opposed it.
Mr HOLDING-They opposed it. But now that it has been forced upon them they embrace it because they have the numbers on it. I will wait to see the results of that inquiry. Who is kidding whom? I do not believe that all honourable members on the other side of the House are men who are blind to injustice where it occurs and I do not believe that all the truth exists on this side of the House. But if there are men of conscience on that side of the House it is time they entered this debate and it is time they examined the evidence of the Vietnam Veterans Association.
I sat and listened to evidence given to that inquiry by a Captain Manning. I know nothing about the man. For all I know he is a man of great integrity. But he was financed and paid by the Department of Veterans' Affairs. He was paid for a very lengthy examination to do what? It was to examine flight paths to try to prove that not too many Australian servicemen in Vietnam were exposed to chemical defoliants. I do not object to his doing that. I do not object to the Department of Veterans' Affairs putting together that sort of team to make that sort of investigation. But if it was good enough for the paid officers of the Department of Veterans' Affairs to spend their time on this matter and to go public disputing the arguments of the Vietnam Veterans Association, and if the Minister is so confident that he is prepared to spend public money on this matter, why should not that evidence be given to an independent judicial tribunal where it can be cross examined, weighed and tested and where the adversary process can measure inaccuracy? It is the same old story-stall, stall, stall.
While officers of the Department of Veterans' Affairs are saying that they are men of good will and that they want to see that something is done about these men Senator Messner, with a broad smile on his face, has his less senior officers, such as Captain Manning, doing otherwise. Only today in the news services a Dr Spragg, who is a psychiatrist and who is a spokesman paid for by the Department-I do not know the extent of his research-was reported as saying, in speaking at a conference in departmental time about his use of departmental money, that he discounts any possibility of agent orange or any chemical being the

Repatriation Legis/ation Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1971

cause of any veterans' problems, that the problems are due to post-traumatic stress disorders. I do not object to his saying that but I object to public money being used to put a one-sided argument in public when this Government and this Minister are not prepared to take that evidence and put it where it ought to be-before an impartial, judicial inquiry, call it a royal commission if you will. If the evidence is that good it will stand up. If it will not stand up it is time we sought justice and truth on behalf of these men.
That is all that they have ever asked for. I think it is reasonable for a married woman who has had two perfectly normal children and whose next child after her husband came back from Vietnam was born with a gross deformity still to want to have another child. But she has that doubt. Is there no compassion in this Government that it is not prepared to try to resolve that doubt or to pursue the evidence and let the truth fall where it may? That is all these men have ever asked for. That is all their families and wives have ever asked for. The Government has suppressed evidence. It refuses to answer the simplest questions.
