Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (8.21 )-1 support the amendment, put forward so ably by the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett) and seconded by the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding), with regard to the Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill 1982. I do this with a heavy heart because over the last months I have heard on a number of occasions attempts by the Opposition to get the Government to recognise and to move to the benefit of Vietnam veterans or forgotten soldiers. I should like briefly to go over the Vietnam tragedy of 14 years as it was seen and told by some creditable persons in recent months. On 9 September this year a Brisbane man shot himself dead. On the following day the Courier-Mail reported:

There are those who believe that bullet was fired not by his own hand, but by the Vietcong, 6000 miles away in South Vietnam 14 years ago.
His father, for example, 'The Vietnam war had a marked effect on my son,' he said yesterday.
'He saw some terrible things. He told me the Vietnamese woman who did his laundry was beheaded by the Vietcong and her head was nailed to the door.
'He'd been a very quiet, serious kid; he loved cars, he didn't drink or smoke.
'The only girl he ever had was his wife, Ira, and they were married just before he went to Vietnam.
'He just wasn't prepared for what he saw there-none of them was.'
A doctor has reported that when Graham saw the movie Apocalypse Now it re-created the war for him in all its threedimensional sound-wracked reality.
'How,' Graham asked his wife, Ira, as he glanced at other filmgoers as the film blasted across the screen, 'can those people sit here and eat ice cream?'
What Graham Rolley-
The young man who took his life-
saw in Vietnam to change his life was military warfare on a huge scale while people in other parts of the world were starving, blood trails where the shells he had fired during the night had landed.
They were the things he told his father about, and how they worried him.
As the years rolled on, Graham, now 36, became familiar with the wards and psychiatrists of Greenslopes Repatriation Hospital.
And now he is dead.
'It was Vietnam that caused it all,' his sister, Mrs Susan Bennett, said:
'He was obsessed by it. He was always talking about it, and going to see films like Apocalypse Now and the Deerhunter over and over again.
"He would say it never solved anything, that it was just a waste of human life.
But he never said he shouldn't have gone. That's the way he was-if there was a job to be done, he just did it.'
Graham was to have appeared in court again yesterday for sentence.
Instead his barrister, Mr Des Draydon, told Judge Gibney:
'Perhaps now something can be done to destroy the public belief that Vietnam returned servicemen are whingers who are trying to make a quick buck out of pensions.'
I realise that this matter sounds sensational. It is all the more sensational to me because it is a reality to which many people want to be kept blinded. On an AM program the same day Mr Des Draydon, who was representing this young man, was questioned by John Austin, who asked:
How can you be so sure that the emotional disturbance that was the experience of Graham Rolley is really typical of Vietnam veterans?
Des said:
Not all veterans do. Many go through it initially. Some are going through it years later. I can only rely on the scientific

1972 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Repatriation Legis/ation Amendment Bill

evidence available. that evidence is mounting. Don't just ascribe it to general madness. This isn't madness. These are people reliving the horrors of a war.
John Austin asked, in effect, whether it would not be better just to forget about the Vietnam war; it is all over now.
Draydon said:
One can't forget about it. One can't say, 'Let's just forget about it, it never happened', and hide one's head in the sand. What society, who sent them there-society sent them there, society has to say, 'Boys, although we may say now we should never have been there, you fellows did the right thing. We sent you there and we welcome you as people who did the right thing'.
Draydon called for more public awareness of the problem and for more sympathy for it. The Courier-Mail took up the tragic record a number of days later on 16 September when Bob Hannah, the Queensland welfare officer for the Vietnam Veterans Association, was reported as beginning a register of dead Vietnam veterans, marking the reasons for death. Suicide and murder were among the violent deaths. The article states:
On almost every page, among the conventional blue ink entries of cancer, leukaemia and heart attacks, there is at least one in red. Sometimes there are two or three, on one page there are four.
Already it suggests that more veterans have died since the Vietnam conflict ended than the 473 killed there but as yet no one knows just how many. Welfare organisations have not bothered to compile follow-up statistics.
Bob and other association workers have had to go to cemetries and find the names and serial numbers of dead veterans and then carry out their own investigations to find what happened to them.
'No one else wants to know,' he said. 'The Government, the RSL, medical people, welfare workers, no-one is interested.'
Nearly all the ex-Diggers were in their late 20s or early 30s when they died and as Bob pointed out, they were the fittest men physically and psychologically Australia ever sent to war.
'Something happened to them there and it's time the Federal Government made some serious attempt to find out . . .'
That is Bob Hannah, the welfare officer for the Vietnam Veterans Association, who was being reported. On 17 September the Vietnam Veterans Association stated:
The reasons for demanding a Royal Commission were strong and clear in 1980. They are stronger and clearer now. Veterans must learn, from a non-political and impeccable source, whether or not those toxic chemicals have adversely affected them.
The Association pointed out that it and 39 other national ex-service organisations are calling for the royal commission that we are now calling for. The following day, in a letter to the editor of the Courier-Mail, the Minister for Veterans Affairs (Senator Messner) defended the Government's lack of action. In doing so he brought up the second aspect which we are fighting for in our amendment. The first one is an immediate,

independent judicial inquiry into the effect on Vietnam veterans of their exposure to herbicides and pesticides. The information I put forward, I think, is a strong basis upon which to call for this judicial inquiry, particularly in view of the fact that 39 associations, including the Vietnam Veterans Association, are calling for that inquiry 14 years after the veterans were seduced into giving themselves, at the peak of their physical fitness, for this country. What is so different about this matter is that they are cast aside when other people are feted on ANZAC Day and on other occasions when we get together to revere what Australians have done in war. The second aspect of the amendment seeks to increase benefits for the wives of totally and permanently incapacitated veterans. In a letter, the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) wrote:
The government has acted to make available free medical treatment in urgent cases for Vietnam veterans and their wives, even if they have no repatriation entitlement.
But there is a problem there. There is a loophole. The treatment is just for urgent cases and most of these people are chronically ill. The illnesses of veterans are going on and on and they are reflecting on their wives so that they are in fear of having a family and in fear of how their children will be affected. They are not being properly compensated. In the Courier Mail of 16 September an article by Ken Blanch stated:
A Veterans Affairs Department officer who addressed a meeting of Vietnam veterans a couple of weeks ago put their problems down to post-service stress and said it was no different from that suffered by participants in Australia's other wars.
He angered the listeners by telling them: 'Sometimes puppy dogs feel sick, too. They go off by themselves and have a sleep and then they feel better'.
I make it clear, as I did in March of this year, to honourable members on the other side of the House what sorts of problems veterans who were exposed to agent orange have been suffering from. We are not talking of trifling medical disorders. The conditions include carcinomas of several kinds, nausea, diarrhoea, weight loss, lethargy, depression, anxiety, fits of rage, insomnia, headaches and skin rashes. The cumulative effect is a high incidence of suicide. Their families also are really suffering. They do not receive compensation. It is now well known that their children may have a number of physical deformities. When we add to this already extensive list of physical problems the emotional pressures placed on a family trying to decide whether to keep having children after one or more miscarriages or the birth of a deformed child we are getting to the root of why families break up or why people commit suicide or turn to crime.

Repatriation Legislation Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1973

This Government has paid lip service to Vietnam veterans but its expressed concern about the weak, the young and the infirm has not been evident from its treatment of Vietnam veterans and, I emphasise, their spouses and families. Those women are bearing the brunt of the exposure of their husbands to herbicides and defoliants. Despite extensive evidence which has been available for some years about the very real human problem of these veterans and their families, it took until about four months ago for the Minister for Veterans Affairs to announce that there would be free emergency treatment for wives and children at repatriation hospitals. The problem is that the facilities are earmarked for emergency treatment only. I am concerned about the number of veterans and families who are chronically affected by different diseases and do not have access to these facilities.
If this House can hold judicial inquiries into meat scandals or any number of issues which have come before the chamber month after month, why can it not hold a judicial inquiry into what is wrong with Vietnam veterans and the rotten deal they are getting in Australia? If honourable memers are so proud of Australia~-they have been saying that during the last week when the Commonwealth Games were being held-and if they are so concerned when they line up with their medals, march and hold their heads up high on ANZAC Day, why are they not calling out aloud and why is not someone in this House, which is rapidly becoming deficient and defunct, doing something about this very real problem? Vietnam veterans and their families should be compensated. Something should have been done. If this House rejects the Opposition's amendment and continues to reject the pleas of the returned soldiers it is a sick institution.

1976 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Diesel Fuel Taxes Amendment Bill

I just put on record that we are disgusted at the way in which this Parliament is being run.
The Opposition does not oppose these Bills. Their purpose is to introduce a system of rebates on duty paid on diesel fuel used off-road in agriculture, mining, forestry and fishing. Fuel used in residential premises, hospitals and aged persons homes and nursing homes will also be eligible for the rebate. These Bills replace the automatic exemption which previously applied to all off-road use of distillate. The rationale for exempting off-road use was that excise was essentiallya road tax. However, the apparent rationale for removing off-road exemption and then introducing a system of rebates for most off-road users is in fact to apply a similar tax on rail transport to that which currently applies to road transport. The excise is to be applied at the rate of 6.155c per litre, the same as for petrol, because the Budget also raised the excise by one cent.
We do not oppose the Bills because they are Budget Bills, but it is impossible to be other than highly critical of what the Government is doing and how it is doing it. The measure will particularly disadvantage primary industry, the mining industry and the tourist industry. The Bills represent another pathetic example of the confusion, the bumbling and the sheer incompetence of the Fraser Government. This Government has proven itself to be not only a disastrous economic manager but also an inept and inefficient administrator. The Bills are designed to implement a measure which has been poorly thought out. They have been amended; they have been withdrawn. Debate on them was called off in the last session of Parliament and now we are finally debating them nearly two months after the measure was announced on Budget night way back in August. National Country Party members and Ministers and Liberal Party members representing rural seats have been subject to so much embarrassment and confusion that they have not known which scapegoat to blame in their own Government. The honourable member for Paterson (Mr O'Keefe) even blamed the Bureau of Customs rather than the Government of which he is a member. Admittedly there has been a lot of work on the back benches of the Government trying to get the administration, the eligibility, of these measures cleared up. But it is absolutely hopeless. The people who hope to get the rebate do not know where they are and the bills are mounting up. I understand that some forms were printed after August, and there was another new form in September. I do not know how the Government can print forms when the measure still has not been debated in the Parliament. Is that again

another example of treating the Parliament with absolute contempt?
Large fuel users in primary industry, such as fishermen, have had to extend their overdraft limits. The people I have been talking with over the last few weeks fear that they may be eventually faced with requests for forms of documentation that they cannot provide to gain the rebate. Several fishermen have shown me the most basic of dockets and say that if they are given rebates on the basis of those eventually-I do not know when it will be; it is two or three months since the measure was first announced-as they should be, it is highly likely that abuses and corruption will occur. I think we need to take this very seriously. I was shown dockets which are entirely handwritten. I am sure the fishermen should be eligible for the rebate, but they point out that, if they are to be paid on that basis eventually, we will have a lot of corruption.
The people supposedly eligible have had to expend thousands of dollars due to the incompetence of the Government, which decided to change from a certification system to a rebate system fundamentally to get more revenue, to attack the State railway systems and to disadvantage several industries. I could name the wheat and sugar industries-most of the crop industries-and industries such as mining, forestry and tourism. All this was done on the ostensible rationale that there were abuses in the certification system. There were abuses, but they could have been tidied up by recertification, sounder procedures and higher penalties for corruption. What makes the Government think that there will not be abuses and rorts with the rebate system? The Government simply cannot have it both ways. The Budget estimate was that $176m would be gained on the basis of 5.155c a litre. The figure is now 6.l55c a litre. It will produce about $2 10m in a full year or $192m this year. Surely that is not all due to abuses. So the Government cannot say that it is just tidying up abuses. It is simply a revenue measure. The Government has to make up its mind which it is going to be; and I say that this is simply to get revenue.
The relevant Minister is trying to have it both ways. He put out a release saying that it will be easy to get the rebate-details have to be given only once and from then it will all flow smoothly. It looks to me like a paper shuffler's paradise. How will the army of bureaucrats who will have to be employed discern between the fuel used by a truck off-road and then on-road? I cite the example of a wheat truck in a paddock carting wheat to the silos. Fishermen have been cynically amused by the naive inquiries that they have had

Diesel Fuel Taxes Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1977

from officials asking how much fuel they use. Of course, fishermen are particularly angry because the price of distillate has risen by over 600 per cent since 1964, and this is for an industry which receives nil assistance in terms of the Industries Assistance Commission's measure of effective
protection.
On 9 September I put a question on notice to the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon). He admitted in his answer that the changeover to the rebate system was not properly thought out. He said that any change in rail freight for primary producers will be a matter for State rail authorities. I am not sure where that leaves producers in South Australia where the Commonwealth railways transports the wheat. Are they to be excluded from the freight increases? The Minister says that he does not know what effect increased rail freight will have on road transport and the deplorable state of many country roads through cutbacks in funding. The Minister in his answer said that he did not know how much extra income would be earned from the grain industry through this increased freight charge. I understand that the National Farmers Federation has estimated a
20 per cent increase in rail freights while there are other estimates as high as $20m alone for the wheat industry. There will be an additional cost of at least $30m for primary industry; that is, if all the forms flow and everyone gets the rebate. But a lot is still subject to doubt. What about the cane trains and the sugar trains? Do we know exactly how they go for any exemption?
If the Government is seriously concerned about abuses in the certificate system, why has it not seriously considered the suggestion that fuel be coloured so that on-farm distillate is clearly distinguishable from off-farm distillate. The number of inspectors needed to keep tabs on this would be a useful comparison to make with the extra administrative cost for the rebate system in general. I note that the original Bill contained a minimum claim limit of $100. Many small farmers would have been disadvantaged by that limit. At 6.155c a litre a farmer would have had to buy 1,600 litres of diesel to be eligible to make a claim. The Government has been forced to change the minimum claim limit to 1,000 litres or within three months of a previous claim. But I assure the House that many people in primary industry still do not know where they are with this measure.
Under the system of rebates all purchases of diesel fuel will be made on the duty paid price. To obtain the rebate the user must lodge a claim with the Collector of Customs. Claims for rebates will be limited to a minimum amount and users must keep records of fuel purchases and usage for a

period of not less than two years. Just how long users will have to wait before a rebate claim is
processed is not made clear. It seems to me that this is a rather complicated way to impose an excise on diesel used by the railways. Is there also the implication of widespread misuse of the off-road exemption by primary producers and others, because I note that the Bill contains investigative powers essential for the effective administration of the new rebate system? If we accept that $30m or more will be the additional farm cost, it can be seen that while this is a small addition to the total level of farm costs, it is not an insignificant amount to producers facing the prospect of a 40 per cent decline in farm incomes this year, the combined result of a rapid acceleration of farm costs and the drought. Of course, part of the acceleration in farm costs is due to the Budget. Freight is only one aspect of farm costs affected by the new diesel excise arrangements.
Although rebates are to be paid on diesel used off-road, producers will initially have to meet the 6.155c per litre. As a result, any change in the excise arrangements will have a major direct impact on farm costs through on-farm fuel usage. Rural industries, including fishing, are big fuel users, and the biggest fuel users are diesel users. For example, in the wheat industry fuel equals 13 per cent of total cash costs. In the dairy industry fuel equals 6.5 per cent of total cash costs. In the sugar and tobacco industries fuel equals 10.5 per cent of total cash costs.
In the fishing industry fuel equals 30 per cent to 40 per cent of total cash costs. The figures I have given are taken from Bureau of Agricultural Economics surveys for the years 1979 and 1980. Tuna boats and prawn boats use about 200 litres of fuel an hour and in the average year they cruise for 3,000 hours, so this is very big money indeed. I just do not know for the life of me, if the idea of this measure is to provide for off-road use, why there cannot be a total exemption for the fishing industry. Surely even this Government can understand that fishing boats do not ride on roads. The fishing industry will be the most severely affected rural industry because of the large component of total cash costs of this industry that fuel represents. As we all know, parts of the fishing industry are already experiencing severe economic difficulties.
I would have liked to go through the mechanism of this legislation but I will leave that to other speakers. I hope that there will be some from the Government side. Let us just take some of the implications for the forestry industry and the mining industry. The legislation contains a very limited definition of forestry, including only those

1978 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Diesel Fuel Taxes Amendment Bill

beneficiation operations carried out in the forest or plantation. On this basis forest operations will incur very significant additional costs because the mills are not directly located within the forest or plantation. Wood harvested from the forest is required to be transported some distance to pulp mills where beneficiation of the wood takes place. In the case of the mining industry, the definition of mining includes, firstly, the dressing or beneficiation, at the mining site or elsewhere, of minerals et cetera. This would be analagous to the process of pulp making.
Secondly, the definition includes the transporting of the minerals or ore from the mining site to the place where they are dressed or beneficiated. This would also be analagous to the transportation of wood from the forest to the pulp mill. I do not have time to go into all that. The forestry industry says that it wants to be treated equally with the mining industry. It does not quite know where it stands. It wants clarification.
If we look at the eligibility criteria for the exemptions that have been set out for the mining industry it is very easy to see that these measures will add significantly to rail costs for coal producers in both New South Wales and Queensland. The Australian Mining Industry Council has estimated that overall they will add $50m a year to costs. The mining industry itself is still concerned that the legislation is unclear, particularly with respect to the position regarding iron ore miners who process, or crush, the ore. This is still uncertain. In Western Australia one iron ore producer may be in a different position from another iron ore producer. It depends on whether the producer uses a private railway or its own railway or whether it actually crushes the ore or beneficiates it. I have been unable to find out exactly what the situation will be. Fundamentally we have been faced with the fact that the Government is amending these Bills and that the back bench has been worried about eligibility and administration. I hope that the relevant Minister will clear up some of these things in his contribution tonight.
I will not speak for much longer. I wish simply to say that these are rotten Bills. They are poorly thought through. The Government has put up a straw man to say that there are abuses. The legislation is all about getting more revenue for the Government. It is all about putting additional costs on to the primary industries of this country, whether they be agricultural or mining, and the tourist industry. I hope that the Government can come forward with some explanation. All that it has done in the last two months or so and all that it will do until people get some of the money back through rebates is to place an added cost burden

on some of our primary producers and other people in this country who are trying to make a living even under this rotten Government.
Mr MacKENZIE (Calare) (9.4)-ln speaking on these three pieces of legislation relating to
changed arrangements regarding diesel fuel excise I wish to make some comments about various sectors of industry, those that are directly affected by the rebate and those that are not. One of the reasons why they have been delayed is that a number of back benchers have been concerned that the administrative arrangements that were originally proposed should be streamlined and modified. The original proposal for the administering of the rebate was that it could be claimed only as it accumulated to the level of $100 in value. There was some suggestion that it may be able to be claimed only at the end of the financial year. The responsible Minister has changed that so that the rebate can be claimed once 1000 litres-in effect, $61.55 worth of rebate-~have been purchased or once every three months, whichever is the sooner event. That has made a tremendous difference. I understand also that the rebate will be able to be claimed on the basis of presentation of consignment notes or evidence of delivery of the fuel. Evidence of payment will not be required. So an

Diesel Fuel Taxes Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1979

exempt purchaser will be able to make an immediate claim for the rebate once the fuel has been delivered to him, on presentation of documentary evidence that it has been delivered to him.
I wish to mention some other groups in the community which, because of the change in the rebate from an off-road, on-road basis to a different basis, are naturally fairly unhappy about the additional costs that they will bear. I have before me a note which reminds me that my colleague the honourable member for Paterson (Mr O'Keefe) pointed out to me some days ago that the distributor was to carry some burden. Generally he has to pay the oil companies the full price of the fuel within seven days whereas the farmer may pay when he gets the rebate. The other groups that are exempt-the people who are involved in charter shipping, or cruise operatorswill now be paying the levy. They may well question why they should have to pay a levy which applies for the Bicentennial Road Development Program, but I point out that those operators will benefit from that program in that the people they take on their boats will in many cases arrive at the port of embarkation by road. Much of the money collected under the Bicentennial Road Development Program will be spent on highways that are very close to and serve those areas of Queensland, such as the Great Barrier Reef, at which so many of these charter boat operators are based.
Public transport will have to pay the additional fuel excise. That means that rail and ferry operators, which were previously exempt, will now have to pay this excise. I cannot see that that will necessarily lead to disastrous consequences. In fact, one group that should be very pleased about that decision is the independent road transport operators, who will now have a much fairer chance to compete with State rail authorities. Some commercial operations which have been using diesel for heating purposes, such as dry cleaners, bakers, brickworks and others, will also be affected. In my electorate, an area which experiences cold winters, quite a number of commercial premises, clubs and such types of organisations use diesel for heating. Indeed liquid fuels should be conserved. I think those sorts of operations should have been looking carefully at using some substitute form of energy rather than scarce liquid fuels.
I believe local government has somewhat of a difficulty. I would like to put its case to the Minister because often local government has to pay substantially more for construction operations. In many cases that applies to small local government authorities which have a small ratepayer base. For

example, I can think of a number in my area where the additional bill for diesel will be, say, between $10,000 and $20,000. On a ratepayer basis it becomes quite significant because in many cases the smaller local government areas that have a big involvement in road construction and maintenance have a small ratepayer base.
It may be something like $2 or $3 per ratepayer whereas in larger provincial councils or metropolitan councils that do not have that construction activity the additional excise is barely noticeable. Of course, this measure has also reduced somewhat the benefit that the councils were to get from the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program. My colleague the honourable member for Maranoa (Mr Ian Cameron), who will be making some comments later on, I am sure will mention the situation where people are reliant on diesel for power generation for their business activities. This applies to some motels and other tourist operations in isolated Australia which are not connected to bulk power supplies. They are reliant on diesel. This will affect their operations.
The honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) asked why it was necessary to introduce these changes. I refer him to efficiency audit report No. 4 of the Auditor-General which examined the collection of excise duties and deferred Customs duties by the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs. In that report the Auditor-General pointed out that the opportunities for abuse of the system were widespread, that they occurred at the user, distributer and even the company level. Whilst it was very difficult to estimate the degree of abuse and the loss to revenue from that it was nevertheless felt that a change in the system was essential to try to overcome this abuse. I believe that there will be a significant number of additional public servants engaged to process and administer these applications. A figure of 120 has been used. Of course, the costs of those additional 120 bureaucrats will have to be offset against the abuse. Personally I would be interested to hear some more definitive figures of the costs of administering the scheme and the estimations of the abuse.
I wish to make sure that my colleague has time to speak in this debate. Before closing I would just like to raise one further issue. Perhaps the Minister could refer to this matter in his closing comments. The system of making claims for the rebate involves the applicant first making a claim and then he or she will be asked to provide certain basic pieces of information as to the use of diesel and the like. Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (9.15)-1 enter this debate on one narrow interest-that is, my interest in the tourist industry.

I guess that two factors are involved here. Firstly, this Government, which purports to be a government of private enterprise and a great efficient economic manager of this country, reveals itself as its period of office extendsprobably in a limited fashion-as being a government of ad hockery. So many decisions are made that can affect the industries that the Government purports to represent so desperately without reference to those industries that it is no wonder that the Australian Labor Party suddenly finds itself not being cossetted by industry but certainly being on a much more acceptable wave length with industrial leaders around this country. The great hopes that private enterprise had for the Fraser Government are suddenly disappearing. The decision on diesel excise is a fairly good example. I do not think the Government really realises the import of tourism to the Australian economy. It is probably the only chance we have in Australia of a growth industry which employs people and which is so labour intensive. Yet it strikes me as quite odd that this Government that purports to be a government of free enterprise has not yet come to grips with the great impact that the tourist industry has on our economy.
I will just relate a few simple facts to the Government for its own elucidation. The facts are these: The tourist industry provides 2.7 per cent of our gross domestic product; the mining industry provides 2.8 per cent; and the car manufacturing industry provides 2.6 per cent. I am sure that some of the honourable members sitting on the other side of the House do not realise just how important the tourist industry is to our economy in the broader sense. When I saw this move by the Government to introduce a new petrol excise Bill,

particularly on diesel fuel which, in terms of the Government announcement, was to extend the amount of money that would be available to improve public roads, I was disappointed if not surprised that while the exemption which has always applied to agriculture and to mining was retained
that which traditionally applied to tourism was removed. The tourist industry suddenly found itself with an imposition of a 6.16c per litre increase in the cost of its diesel fuel. I apply my criticism in particular to the islands off the Queensland coast. 1 am glad that the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr McVeigh), who is seated at the table, is a Queensland representative, albeit from the National Country Party up on the Downs. This industry should be exempt from the payment of the duty in the first place. The reasons are quite clear.

The rebate is to be paid at determined intervals. The fishing industry is appalled at the legislation. I am well aware that the previous plan was open to abuse and was indeed abused. It did not set up an expensive inefficient, time wasting bureaucracy to delay payments and add costs to the industries concerned. Britain adopted a sensible and very simple solution; it coloured bright green the diesel fuel that was sold duty free to those who held certificates. It proved to be a very successful deterrent against abuse in that police and Customs had the power to stop and check vehicles and sample the fuel. The penalties imposed against those who transgressed were heavy. I suggest to the Government that it colour the fuel green, cut out the bureaucracy, cut out the abuse and save the industry the interminable forms and heavy interest on extended overdrafts while people await their rebates. As you would probably realise, this Council's earthmoving plant is almost wholly involved in the construction and maintenance of roads throughout this District. The District happens to be the third largest Council area of the State and has some 1,000 kilometres of unsealed roads under its control.

Government~
That is, this Government~
has recognised this concept in continuing to grant exemptions to agricultural, mining and other industries which activities do not directly involve the use of the road system.
That is the opinion of the District Council of Elliston on the Eyre Peninsula in South Australia, and I think it is symptomatic of the general view. In a similar letter the District Council of Murat Bay, the most westerly council in the State, had this to say:
With the expenditure by local government on roadworks rising in a totally disproportionate manner to the roadworks expenditure of the State and Federal Governments, the current situation is obviously totally inequitable to local government.
Road construction costs, particularly in rural areas are rising at a far more rapid rate than inflation, the imposition of this additional burden~~
That is this additional tax~
will ensure that Commonwealth Grants Commission funding paid to local government through the State Governments will not be an 'untied grant' in the same manner as previously. Perhaps local government's fears that the 'untied' nature of these grants will soon become a thing of the past, are already being realised.
As I said, that was the opinion of the District Council of Murat Bay, the most westerly council and one of the largest in the State. The general view of councils was expressed by the Local Government Association of South Australia in a letter which it wrote to me about this matter. I do not want to delay the House much longer on these issues. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard this letter from the Local Government Association of South Australia.
Leave granted.
The letter read as follows~
Local Government Association of South Australia
2 Hutt Street, Adelaide, S.A. 5000
ML: MF 930/82
1st September, 1982
Mr L. G. Wallis, M.P., 99 Commercial Road, Port Augusta, S.A. 5700
Dear Mr Wallis,
You will be aware that the recent Federal Budget contained provision for a new levy to be applied to all fuel, the specific intent of which is to channel more funds directly into road construction. It has been the contention of this Association for some considerable time that the nation's road system requires urgent attention and, as such, we were supportive of this move and acknowledged it as a fitting bi-centenary project.
Our enthusiasm is significantly diminished, however, by the statement by the Treasurer that as from the evening of the Budget, all diesel fuel would bear the full rate of excise duty irrespective of its end use. Local Government until then

Diesel Fuel Taxes Amendment Bill

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1987

received an exemption of 5.155 cents per litre for diesel used by 'off-road' vehicles. Councils will not receive an exemption from now on. The extra cent per litre applied this year and the further cent to be applied next year, will accentuate our problem.

The exemption previously granted was of direct assistance to Council road construction and maintenance activities. Withdrawal of the exemption cannot be seen as anything but a clear reduction in the Commonwealth's commitment to the road system and consequently, significantly undermines the value of road funds derived from the new levy. In South Australia, we hold grave fears that Councils may be no better off in the final analysis than they were before the budget. Accordingly, scepticism over the contribution of the Bicentenary project to the upgrading of our road system is widespread. From our perspective, this is extremely disappointing.

We are currently attempting to compile figures on the cost of the exemption being taken from Local Government. In small Councils, such as the District Councils of Barossa and Owen, the cost will between $2,500 to $3,000 annually. In larger areas, such as the District Council of Coonalpyn Downs, the cost will be in the order of $7,000 per annum. In the Metropolitan area, the cost varies but will be most significant in the fringe Council areas. It seems reasonable, therefore, to assume that across the 127 Council areas in this State, the loss will be at least in the order of $400,000 to $500,000.

This Association respectfully requests your support in our efforts to return Local Government exemption from diesel fuel tax for off-road vehicles. Such an event would not only ensure that needed resources are channelled into roads, but would help restore trust in the fairness of the Commonwealth Treasury.

Yours sincerely,
J. M. HULLlCK,
Secretary-General

Of course, the imposition of this additional diesel fuel tax will take some of the shine off those grants, and a lot of people in those councils feel that the Government has pulled a shifty one in that it involves the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program, et cetera. As I said earlier, it looks as though the councils, like everybody else in Australia, are to get a birthday present for the bicentenary but will have to pay for it themselves.

The main purpose of the amendments circulated is to amend the Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Bill 1982 to simplify the process of claiming rebates of duty paid on diesel fuel and to extend the eligibility for rebate to sawmilling operations carried on outside a forest. In the Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Bill provision is made for a rebate of duty on diesel fuel to be paid to certain categories of persons when they use the fuel for 'off-road' purposes. That Bill forms part of a package of measures designed to terminate the previous diesel fuel certificate system which enabled users of diesel fuel to purchase such fuel for 'off-road' purposes duty free, on presentation of a certificate. Clause 5 of the Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Bill enables certain categories of users to obtain a rebate of the duty paid on diesel fuel used for specific 'off-road' purposes. These categories comprise users in the agricultural, mining, forestry and fishing industries, and in domestic premises, hospitals, nursing institutions and aged persons' homes.
I turn now to the amendments which have been circulated. These amendments will remove the requirement in the present Bill that a person is required to purchase and use the fuel before an application may be lodged, and will place in its stead a provision under which a person may claim a rebate after purchase for the specified uses. It is proposed that payments of the rebate will be made on the basis of the estimated proportion of fuel purchases that will be used for specified purposes. The present Bill provides that domestic users may claim rebates at any time but imposes a minimum limit of $100 per claim on all other users. The amendments change this latter provision to allow other than domestic users to claim after three months of a previous claim or after 1000 litres of fuel have been purchased. One thousand litres is equivalent to a rebate of $62 at the current rate of duty. This change will benefit small users of diesel fuel, particularly those users engaged in primary production.
The amendments also extend the eligibility for rebate to milling operations carried on at a sawmill located outside a forest or plantation, including the transport of timber, other than on a public road, to those sawmills. The present Bill restricts rebate eligibility to forestry milling and to the processing of timber in the forest or plantation. This amendment is proposed so as not to disadvantage mills located outside the forest in comparison with competitive establishments located

Diesel Fuel Legislation

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1989

in the forest. Because of the revised criteria whereby rebates will now be paid on purchases of diesel fuel for use rather than on amounts of fuel used in the defined categories, the amendments include provision for the recovery of rebates paid to a person in circumstances where the person initially purchases the fuel for use in the defined categories but subsequently uses that fuel in some other manner.
Finally, Mr Chairman, the amendments alter the provision in the Bill relating to residential premises to make it clear that diesel fuel used at those premises in providing domestic lighting will be eligible for the rebate. For this purpose the word 'lighting' has been inserted into proposed section 164 (I) (B) (II) of the Customs Act and proposed section 78A (I) (B) (II) of the Excise Act.
1 mention that it is proposed to amend the Customs and excise regulations to set out the requirements relating to applications for the rebate and to the procedures for claiming rebates. It is the view of the Government that these changes will lead to a far more simplified claiming process for the rebate and enable more frequent payments of claims than would have been possible under the arrangements originally proposed by the Bill.

PRIVILEGE Mr SPEAKER-Earlier today, the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) raised as a matter of privilege an article published in the Australian newspaper today. The portion of the article to which the Leader of the Opposition particularly drew my attention read as follows:

1990 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Adjournment

Government sources said the public servant's name had been deleted from a sheaf of documents tabled yesterday in the House of Representatives by the Leader of the Opposition, Mr Hayden.
The honourable gentleman drew attention to a number of other matters. However, I have now had an opportunity to examine the matter further. There can be no breach of privilege unless there is obstruction of or impediment to the performance of the duties of the member or a tendency to do so. Nothing said by the Leader of the Opposition would lead me to the conclusion that he has been so obstructed or that the article has a tendency to do so. There may have been a misrepresentation of a degree to be decided on a factual basis but there is no breach of privilege.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-Mr Speaker, with your indulgence I wish to make a few comments. First, I accept completely your ruling. I have enough confidence in and respect for you to do that unreservedly. The second thing I have to say is in the light of the evidence that I produced today, namely, that the explanation to Mr Hill of the Australian made it clear that there could be no justification in fact for any claim of a deletion in the sense that documents, as I outlined, showed beyond any doubt that Mr Moore had ceased to be a director of the relevant companies at 31 May 1977 and that any suggestion to the contrary, explicit for instance in documentation subsequently provided for official purposes, would have been erroneously based. I think that was established beyond any doubt. One can reach only one conclusion, therefore, and that is that Mr Hill, or whoever was responsible for that claim in the newspaper article, proceeded to write it in spite of the clear evidence produced for him. He failed to refer to the clear evidence on this matter that had been referred to him. One is left with the quite justifiable suspicion that there was some politically prejudiced motivation in this matter. Allowing for Mr Hill's previous employment I think that is not an unreasonable conclusion to reach.
I conclude by saying that this is not the first time. I have had a problem with Mr Hill. On an earlier occasion, in Tasmania, he reported matters quite erroneously and in a way which, if they were intentionally reported erroneously, could have been motivated only by a wish to disadvantage the Labor Party and me in the presentation of a particular issue. I regret that and I trust that the management of the Australian newspaper shares my concern about this matter. I hope that it takes steps to make sure that it does not happen again.

Trade Union Activity in the Philippines-Australian Steel Industry and

Mr HOWE (Batman) (10.9)-1 wish to raise tonight the crackdown on trade union activity by the Marcos Government in the Philippines. This morning's newspapers tell us that as part of the campaign by the Philippines Government against political opposition its agents had shot and killed one of two Catholic priests upon whom the Government had placed a bounty because of their alleged political activities. Another report said that the Australian priest from Perth, Father Gore, is currently being unjustly harassed as a subversive by the Philippines security forces. On 13 August the Government arrested without trial Felixberto Olalia, a distinguished trade unionist and an official of the KMU-a coalition of progressive unions with an estimated membership of over one million workers. Mr Olalia, 79 years of age, was described by the Far Eastern Economic Review on 27 August as one of the few credible trade unionists in the country. Since his arrest a total of 38 unionists have been arrested, including another KMU leader, Crispin Beltram; At least three union offices have been raided and ransacked.
I raise the matter of the arrest of Philippine trade unionists not only because it is an important human rights issue but also because Australia is increasingly being implicated in supporting the Marcos regime through the economic ties and through the militarisation of Australia's aid programs to the Philippines. Australian and United States Government support is being used to shore up the Marcos dictatorship. This Australian Government has been a strong advocate of closer military co-operation with the Marcos regime as a way of strengthening the region against the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics and its ally, Vietnam.
Increasingly, since the lifting of martial law in January last year, unions in the Philippines have been demanding improvements to the appalling pay and conditions under which Philippine workers have been suffering. Wages range from one-tenth to one-quarter of what workers in Australia receive. Official estimates suggest that an average family of six needs 55 pesos or $A7 to survive. Hundreds of thousands of Philippine

Adjournment

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1991

workers are receiving equal to or below the daily basic wage presently set at 15 pesos or $A2. These appalling conditions explain the dramatic increase in union activity in the Philippines. There were over 250 strikes in 1981 as many workers strived to regain rights and wages lost since 1972 when martial law was declared. The Government's response has been to strengthen labour laws through measures such as giving business more freedom to fire workers, declaring a list of so-called vital industries in which strikes are outlawed and empowering the President to certify virtually any strike as affecting the national interest. In the past 12 months five workers have been killed and many more injured on picket lines. Union organisers have disappeared and hundreds of workers have been arrested in their attempts to improve their working conditions.
From Australia's point of view we are no longer passive onlookers in South East Asia. It is increasingly clear that Australian business is perceiving the Philippines as an attractive area for investment which is able to reap profits through the exploitation of cheap labour and raw materials in the area. The Philippines is also becoming an important source of trade worth about $300m. We are becoming one of the Philippines, largest suppliers of raw materials and capital equipment. As the Australian Government allows our local manufacturing sector to crumble, Australian companies are closing factories and exporting their industries to Asian countries where political repression of workers keep wages low and profits high.
Australian companies with operations in the Philippines include the Dunlop organisation, Safcol Pty Ltd, Australian Consolidated Industries Ltd and the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd. For example, Dunlop Olympic Ltd now employs some 4,500 people in overseas factories. It has four factories in the Philippines and two in Malaysia. Dunlop's investment strategy undoubtedly seeks to benefit from the anti-union position of the Marcos regime and appalling wages and conditions. This is typified in the export processing zones, such as in Bataan 150 kilometre north-west of Manila. Foreign-owned companies employ some 20,000 workers and two Australian companies, Dunlop and Australia Star Billiards, operate in the zone. The Dunlop workers had a long and tough struggle just to form a union. It is not surprising then that Australia's foreign policy is locked in to buttressing the political oppression of opposition forces and trade union activity in the Philippines. Our aid program, rather than assisting the poor to improve their lives, is providing

military logistical support to the Marcos Government. Australia has been expanding its tole in developing the counter-insurgency capabilities of the neo-colonial Marcos regime. An increasing number of personnel from the Philippines armed forces have been trained in advanced counterinsurgency warfare in Australian military schools. We have been selling the Philippines planes such as the Nomad aircraft. We have sold it the Dartthe automatic range target system. The military collaboration between Australia and the Philippines Government has been supported by bilatera! aid programs, such as the Northern Samar integrated rural development project. A report in March this year by the Catholic Commission of Justice and Peace suggested the aid was useless in terms of improving people's standard of living but assisted the Government's military aims.

Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (lO.15)-It was interesting to hear the speech of the honourable member for Batman (Mr Howe) because no doubt it involves a situation of concern. I wish to talk about some of the effects that the actions of various trade unionists in Australia have on the people of Australia. Some time ago in this House I asked the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) about his meetings with the then Secretary of the Federated Iron workers Association of Australia in regard to the future of the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd. I was aware that the iron workers were seeking help from the Government because they, as workers, are currently considerably concerned about their future and, of course, the future of the steel industry. Of course, the problem with the Australian steel industry is that it is now the most expensive producer of steel, bar one, in all the world. The entire blame for this situation does not lie with the work force. Nevertheless there is some evidence that it has created a problem, as the productivity drop per man hour between 1975 and 1982 has been 6.8 per cent. This has been at a time when productivity per man hour in the steel industry throughout the world has been growing substantially.
The problem that BHP, as our prime supplier of steel, has represented to other industries was clearly exemplified when the Santos company called a tender for 38 kilometres of steel pipe. The price for Japanese pipe, probably manufactured from Australian iron ore and coal and delivered back to Australia duty paid, was half the price of that of local manufacture. If we extrapolate the sorts of costs throughout the entire energy system that would be applied to the price of gas, we can

1992 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Adjournment

see that many other workers and many other businesses also would have had increased costs had Australian steel pipe been used. Somebody has to ask themselves who bears the eventual burden. It is interesting to me to note that for the first time a company such as BHP is feeling the brunt of the Government's saying that we will have a halt. I am very pleased to hear that for the sake of all the people down the line who would otherwise have been pressured out of their jobs.
It is interesting in this area of one lot of workers affecting another lot of workers to consider the situation that applied in New South Wales when 400 workers decided that the jobs of millions of New South Welshmen were really not worth worrying about if there was some personal gain for themselves. A similar situation occurred recently in New South Wales in the case of electricity workers. Nobody really cared. One sometimes wonders where the enemy is. In this case I am suggesting the enemy is within. Various unions in Australia have for so long been convinced that the sole enemy was their employer that they have been unable to identify the fact that maybe some of their colleagues, their brothers, are equally the problem. It is this matter which I wish to refer to tonight. There are those workers who get the industrial advantage completely unrelated to their own abilities or productivity. It has been the greed of these sectors of the work force that has substantially reduced the opportunities for others to gain employment.

Mr TUCKEYI am not saying that that is not a problem. It is usually reinvested in business. In fact even the shareholder gets little. I will look at one point. declared that this feather-bedded industry imposes a burden on BHP of $25m per annum compared to international shipping costs. It is reason enough in my view for an urgency motion at the Australian Council of Trade Unions. If the ACTU cannot resolve this type of injustice one should question its existence.

Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (10.25)- Honourable members may recall that I have previously addressed the House concerning a Bill introduced into the Victorian Parliament to amend the Council of Law Reporting in Victoria Act. That Bill has now been passed, having recently been rushed through both Houses on the same evening. This new Act claims to make it unlawful for anybody except the Council of Law Reporting in Victoria, a body which is not elected or responsible to any Minister or to the public, 'to

commence the publication or to publish the whole or any part of any reports of judicial decisions of any court in Victoria', unless the reporting is done by or in association with a newspaper, a news magazine or a news broadcast.
The powers which this new Act purports to give the Council are extraordinarily wide. On the face of it, one now needs permission even to quote from a judgment unless one is a newspaper proprietor. Law students will require permission before they sit for examinations, and lawyers quoting Victorian judgments will need permission before they can send out their advice, create their own private data bases or even write letters on word processors. Further, if the word 'report' is not read to mean a formal case report, then freedom of speech in Victoria is itself being restricted because everyone requires permission to summarise or to discuss in detail a Victorian court decision unless, of course, one happens to own a newspaper. One can only wait with bated breath to see what treasures will be contained in Mr Cain's version of a freedom of information Bill. This legislation does not concern only Victorian courts adjudicating domestic Victorian issues. It purports to cover Federal courts' sittings in Victoria, State courts exercising Federal jurisdiction and State courts considering legislation uniformly enacted by the States and the Commonwealth, such as the new Companies Act. Furthermore, the Council has shown that it will use its powers to restrict publication of judicial decisions and it has restricted the CCH from publishing cases heard in Victorian courts on tax and company law.
This legislation is outrageous and I hope that the Commonwealth Attorney-General (Senator Durack) will move at once to open up access to all laws-cases, statutes and regulations-and to put these laws in the public domain, where they belong. The Acting Attorney-General (Mr N. A. Brown), in answering a question on 23 September this year, said:
We believe there should be the absolute and most extensive freedom in the dissemination of legal matters which are of importance to the profession by way of statutes and law reports.
One could say to the public as well. There need be no delay about deregulating judgments from Federal courts and tribunals and Commonwealth Acts and regulations. What could be easier than to announce that copyright restrictions on these legal materials are lifted for both hard copy and electronic media? Similarly, I hope the Government will announce that it will legislate to open access to the laws in the States and especially in Victoria. That the Commonwealth has constitutional powers to do this is hardly arguable in the

1994 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Adjournment

light of section 51 of the Constitution and its various subsections, as well as chapter Ill's powers to make laws with respect to the judicature.
The whole saga of computerised legal information retrieval systems in Australia provides a most salutary lesson. The deliberations of the Attorneys-General and their advisory bodies have not been published and are still secret. There has been little public debate, if any, because there is so little information on Government policies. Victoria, by being, as it were, the first cab off the rank, has been able to steal a march on all the other States and the Commonwealth. By arguing that a government-controlled monopoly is necessary, it was able to give a Victorian monopoly to a joint venture between a Victorian company and a large United States computer company and is able to ensure that the computer company's new $18m computer centre is located in Victoria. By arguing that government-controlled monopolies are necessary and that for convenience there ought to be a single national operator, the Victorian Government is attempting to ensure that such a national monopoly is under its control.
But what is also frightening is the magnitude of the waste caused by the governments. The money spent in establishing government policy, in setting up advisory committees, in paying consultants, in sending politicians, committeemen and their consultants around the world, in drafting invitations for proposals, in the answering of these proposals by companies, and in the evaluation of these proposals and the subsequent negotiations would probably have been enough to establish a computerised legal information retrieval system. If this wasted money were spent on establishing one rather than on working out how the computerised legal information retrieval system is to be controlled, we would probably already have a computerised legal information retrieval system and it would be largely paid for. The shame of it is that it could have been achieved so easily-simply by the deregulation of copyright-at so much less cost to everyone involved and much more quickly.

for the airline to undertake these trial ft.ights before the first regular scheduled service is brought into operation.

It is not long since the first sod was turned and now this project is near completion.

Kingsford-Smith and Tullamarine International Air Terminals
(Question No. 4731)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(1) What was the total cost of building (a) Kingsford Smith International Air Terminal and (b) Tullamarine International Air Terminal.
(2) What was the cost of additional runways and taxiways provided for these terminals.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

What funds have been allocated to the Aboriginal Development Commission for the purpose of (a) acquiring traditional lands, (b) establishing viable economic ventures and (c) providing low-cost housing finance in each year since its establishment.
Mr Wilson-The an!iwer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed that the Aboriginal Development Commission allocated the following amounts for the purpose indicated:

1980-81 1981-82 1982-83

Actual

Actual Estimated

Expenditure Expenditure Expenditure

(I) (a) $38,121,448 including the cost of access roads, carparks, aprons and taxiways; (b) $30,722,105 including the cost of both the domestic and international terminals as well as access roads, carparks and aprons.
(2) (i) No additional runways were provided for the international terminal building at Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport. The cost of additionsl taxiways is included in the cost of the terminal building given in the answer to question I (a) and is not seperable. (ii) The international terminal building at Melbourne (Tullamarine) Airport was built as part of the whole new airport project. Runways and taxiways built for the international terminal cannot therefore be identified separately.

Independent Audit: Attorney-General's Department

(Question No. 4779)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In those instances where grants are made pursuant to legislative provisions, it is required by the legislation that an audited financial statement of receipts and payments of the

1998 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

organisation be provided. Where a grant in excess of $5,000 is made to an organisation in those instances where there is no legislative requirement for an audited financial statement, an independently audited statement is required as a condition of the making of the grant. Where a grant of $5,000 or less is made to an organisation in those instances where there is no legislative requirement for an audited financial statement, a certificate by a responsible officer of the organisation showing that the amount provided was expended in accordance with the terms of the grant is required as a condition of the making of the grant.
Independent Audit: Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs
(Question No. 4794)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The requirements for audit of grants to non-government organisations are contained in Finance Directions issued pursuant to the Audit Act. Departmental instructions covering such grants specify procedures to ensure the proper expenditure of funds.

United States Military Satellites Supported by NASA Station at Orrora1 Valley, Australian Capital Territory
