Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No.

Port Clinton, Queensland: Feasibility Study for Use as Coal Port
(Question No. 4859)
Dr EYeringham asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Have Hancock and Wright proposed or prepared feasibility studies of the development of Port Clinton, Queensland, as a coal port based on its excision from the Shoalwater Bay training reserve.
(2) Has the Queensland Government sought entry to the reserve for officials of the State Department of Harbours and Marine; if so, (a) was this arranged through normal channels, (b) were attempts at entry stopped by service personnel and (c) what legal remedies will be sought and who will be charged if such attempts were illegal.
(3) What studies of land use and management in the reserve have been undertaken by Australian authorities.

2012 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

(4) Was a study made of the potential development of tourist marinas and other facilities in Port Clinton; if so, (a) who made the study and (b) does the study indicate illegal access was gained to the reserve.
(5) Has the Government advised the Queensland Government that Cape Manifold has deeper water more suitable for a coal port than Port Clinton.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am not directly aware of any proposals or studies by Messrs Hancock and Wright. Newspaper reports suggest that a feasibility study for the development of Port Clinton has been issued by them.
(2) (a), (b) and (c). Through appropriate channels, the Queensland Government sought entry to the Shoalwater Bay Training Area for a State government technical team to conduct an on-site inspection and feasibility study, to establish the suitability of the area for development of a deep water port. A marine survey of Port Clinton was conducted during August 1982. There have been no known attempts at illegal entry to the Training Area.
(3) The Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation Division of Land and Water Resources has conducted a wide range of environmental studies of the Training Area for my Department, which are specifically related to land use and management. A number of these studies have wider application than required by the Department of Defence and have been published by the CSIRO. They cover such matters as physical and biological resource inventories, terrain classification and fire dynamics studies. In addition, the CSIRO has prepared, at my Department's request, unpublished studies on matters specifically related to Defence training activities, such as the impact on the Area of those activities and associated works proposals, vehicle trafficability, forest resources, and soil stability.
(4) I am not aware of any such study, or of illegal entry associated with any such study.
(5) No.
Commonwealth Ombudsman: Complaints Received
(Question No. 4862)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) How many complaints were received by the Commonwealth Ombudsman in the ACT, and each of the State Offices for each year since the office was set up.
(2) How many of those complaints were within jurisdiction.
(3) Of those within jurisdiction, how many were found to be sustained.
(4) Of those sustained, how many were rectified.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (4) Detailed statistics on complaints received and resolved by the Commonwealth Ombudsman for each year of his operation are contained in his annual reports. These statIstics are broken down to show, among other matters, the State of residence of each complainant, the relevant department or authority, findings on jurisdiction and the manner of handling, and thus substantially provide the information sought.

Industry Assistance Measures: Export Promotion
(Question No. 4867)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that, under the industry assistance measures announced by the Prime Minister on 19 July 1982, anybody who travels abroad for purposes of 'export promotion' can receive a cash payment of $50 a day up to a maximum of 10 days.
(2) Is it also a fact that he or she can already get 70 per cent of the fares incurred in overseas travel refunded in cash from the Export Development Grants Board.
(3) Can a travel allowance be paid for up to 3 years without the payee having to export anything.
(4) Was a travel allowance rejected by the Industries Assistance Commission because it found that such a move was likely to increase the scope for deliberate abuse.
(5) Did the Government reject this opinion; if so, for what reason.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. Claimants must first establish with the Export Development Grants Board (EDGB) that they are eligible for a grant; secondly they must satisfy the Board that a visit was undertaken primarily and principally for the development of export markets. A promotional visits allowance of $50 a day up to a maximum of 10 days is then payable as a component of the Export Market Develoment Grant which is subject to taxation.
(2) Subject to satisfying the criteria indicated in (I) above, 70 per cent of overseas airfares may be paid as a component of an Export Market Development Grant.
(3) As the development of export markets is a lengthy procedure requiring persistence over a considerable period of time and often generating low initial returns, new claimants may receive grants for three years regardless of the amount of their export earnings during this time. After receiving grants for three grant years a claimant may only continue to receive grants if his export earnings exceed $25,000 per year for goods and services or $10,000 per year if derived from industrial property rights and know how. Tourist industry claimants are exempt from these provisions but must incur at least $5,000 eligible expenditure (other than fares) before they qualify.
(4) and (5) The Government took into account the lAC's views on the possible abuse of the scheme before deciding to provide these benefits.
The Government believes that the procedures developed to administer the payments will prevent abuse. The allowance has been set at a level consistent with the shared cost principle of the scheme, that is it is not intended that the allowance cover all a claimant's living expenses whilst overseas.
United States Military Satellite Supported by Tracking Station at Carnarvon, Western Australia
(Question No. 4882)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
Further to the answer to question No. 4236 (Hansard, 6 and 7 May 1982, page 2500), what was the (a) formal designation, (b) date of launch and (c) orbital parameter of each

Answers to Questions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2013

United States military satellite supported by the NASA tracking station at Carnarvon, Western Australia, between 1969 and the closure of the station.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Details relating to unclassified US military satellites supported from the NASA tracking station formerly at Carnarvon were provided in the answer to question No. 4235 (Hansard 6 and 7 May 1982, page 2500). Security considerations preclude the provision of details of classified military satellites that were supported by the Carnarvon station.
Strawberries: Disease Risk in Imports from California
(Question No. 4886)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Are some Californian strawberries coming into Australia heavily soiled; if so, is the soil checked by quarantine authorities for the possible introduction of animal and plant diseases.
(2) Are many of the strawberries coming into Australia with the calyx attached to the fruit; if so, can the Mediterranean fruit fly be introduced on the calyx of the strawberries.
(3) Is it a fact that strawberries cannot be fumigated or sprayed due to their perishable nature.
(4) Does it take a month of laboratory testing to determine whether strawberries are carrying disease.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. Soil is a prohibited import. All goods are inspected on arrival in Australia and if any soil is found as a contaminant it must be removed or the import is rejected. All soil removed from imported goods is dealt with to remove any quarantine risk.
(2) Strawberries are imported with the calyx attached, as it is considered an integral part of the fruit. There is a prohibition of plant debris, which includes foliage, but not the calyx. There is no possibility that the Mediterranean fruit fly would be introduced on the calyx.
(3) The perishable nature of strawberries makes treatment difficult, but not impossible. Treatment will be ordered if insect infestation is found at inspection.
(4) No. Fruit, including strawberries, is not tested for disease on arrival. Permits are not given for the import of fruit which presents a disease risk to Australian industries. Rotten fruit found at inspection would be destroyed.
Sinai Peacekeeping Force
(Question No. 4891)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Is he able to say whether a private company called 'E' Systems from the United States of America, has the contract to supply certain commodities and services to the multinational, peace-keeping force in Sinai.
(2) Who invited tenders for the contract.
(3) Which companies (a) were invited to tender and (b) were among the tenderers.
(4) What were the national origins of (a) those invited to tender, (b) those who responded and (c) what were the quotes given.

(S) Who (a) reviewed the tenders and (b) granted the contract.
(6) Was the Australian Government consulted at any stage.
(7) What reasons were given for awarding 'E' Systems the contract.
(8) What were the terms of the contract.
(9) Was a separate contract for supplying the Australian contingent ever considered; if so (a) why was this alternative rejected and (b) by whom.
(10) What items has the (a) Australian Army and (b) individual troops purchased or hired from the company.
(II) What were the individual prices of the items or services.
(12) What is the total value of purchases or hirings by (a) the Australian Army and (b) Australian troops.
(13) Has the company supplied aviation support for Australian troops; if so (a) what form did this support take, (b) what was the cost and (c) who paid for the support.
(14) Is the Australian Army capable of supplying any of the services or commodities offered for sale or hire.
(IS) Did the Australian Army offer any of these same commodities or services for sale or hire and were any of them purchased or hired.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. The Multinational Force and Observers (MFO), the body responsible to the Governments of Egypt and Israel for supervising the implementation of the terms of the Peace Treaty, has arranged for 'E' Systems to suppl;' certain commodities and services to the peace-keeping force in the Sinai.
(2) to (S) The information required to answer parts two to five of the honourable member's question is not available to the Australian Government. Under the terms of the A:ustralian MFO Agreement governing Australia's participation in the MFO, the MFO is required to provide, at no cost to the Government of Australia, such commodities and services as food, lodging and base support for the Australian Contingent MFO (ACMFO). Contractual arrangements for the provision of commodities and services is a matter for the MFO.
(6) No.
(7) and (8) See answer to (2) to (S).
(9) No.
(10) (a) Neither the Australian Army nor the ACMFO has purcl;lased or hired items from the company. (b) Individual members of the ACMFO have the opportunity of purchasing an increasingly varied range of items, mainly of a personal or souvenir type, from the company run canteen service. No record of any purchase is available to the Australian Government.
(II) and (12) See answer to part (10). (13) No.
(14) Yes. See also my answers to parts (2) to (S), (7) and (8).
(IS) No.
Brucellosis: Cases among Persons Employed in Abattoirs
(Question No. 4892)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Is brucellosis a notifiable disease.

2014 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

(2) If so, is he able to say how many cases of brucellosis have occurred among persons employed in abattoirs in Australia in the calendar years 1973 to 1982 inclusive.
(3) Is he able to say how many Federal employees, for example, meat inspectors, have applied for compensation after contracting the disease.
(4) Is he able to say whether the Federal Government pays compensation to its employees when they contract the disease; ifso, how many people have received compensation.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Brucellosis is a notifiable disease.
(2) The Department is unable to provide details of the number of cases occurring among all persons employed in abattoirs as requested. There have however been 132 cases involving the Department's meat inspection staff in the calendar years 1973 to 1982 inclusive.
(3) In the calendar years 1973 to 1982 inclusive, 129 meat inspection staff have applied for compensation after contracting the disease. Three persons did not claim compensation.
(4) Where claims are submitted and liability accepted, the Federal Government pays compensation to its employees when they contract the disease. During the period 1973 to 1982 inclusive 128 employees have received compensation. One further claim is presently being considered.
Aircraft Passenger Seats: Safety Requirements
(Question No. 4893)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Can he say whether, after investigation into the crash of an Air Florida aircraft in Washington D.C. on 13 January 1982, an official of the United States National Transportation Safety Board expressed concern about the strength of passenger seats on the aircraft.
(2) Has his Department investigated the standard and strength of passenger seats on aircraft using Australian airspace.
(3) Are similar seats and anchorage parts used in Australian aircraft as in the Air Florida aircraft which crashed in Wa,hinglOn.
(4) What are the safety requirements for passenger seats in aircraft.
(5) How often is the strength of passenger seats checked, how are they checked and by whom.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) My Department is aware of reference in a television news program to alleged expressions of concern by the National Transportation Safety Board about the strength of passenger seats in general and in the Air Florida aircraft in particular. Some preliminary information has recently been received from the NTSB and is being studied but the final report and recommendations of the Board is still awaited.
(2) My Department has no jurisdiction on the standards applicable to passenger seats of airline aircraft using Australian airspace, except when such aircraft are on the Australian register. However, since there are few manufacturers of large transport aircraft and all such aircraft are built to the same internationally accepted standards, it is accurate to say that all airline aircraft using Australian airspace are fitted

with seats which comply with the United States Federal Aviation Regulation Part 25 standards or equivalent.
(3) Yes.
(4) Internationally accepted airworthiness requirements for passenger seats in airline aircraft cover the strength of seats, of safety belts, of attachments to the aircraft structure and flammability aspects. Passenger seats are designed to provide their occupants with maximum protection in a survivable accident such as a crash landing not involving deceleration forces greater than 9 times the force of gravity. Detail standards can be found in United States Federal Aviation Regulations FAR 25-785.
(5) The strength of each type of passenger seat and its attachments is checked in structural tests before the seat is allowed to be fitted to aircraft. The condition of the seat, belt, and attachments is checked for damage and insecurity regularly while the aircraft is in airline service. Specific checks are laid down in the aircraft Inspection Schedules which are approved by my Department. They usually take place once every 65 to 100 hours of operations depending on the aircraft type, and are performed by the airlines' maintenance organisation.

Cancer Patients: Treatment with Use of Laser Technology

(Question No. 4895)

Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 19 August 1982:

(I) Has his Department made a grant for research into the

use of laser technology in the treatment of cancer at the

Queen Elizabeth Hospital in Adelaide.

.

(2) When the project becomes marketable, could it be worth millions of dollars to the export market.

(3) Have these grants been at a level of about one third of the expenditure of the research project.

(4) Has one of the grants to a PhD. graduate been given only in alternate years; if so, Why.

(5) Has the project's progress been impeded by the nature of the on/offgrant to that graduate.

(6) When the project is completed (a) will Australian cancer patients have more chance of surviving cancer therapy and (b) will patients benefit from new operating procedures
which aid rapid recovery from operations, thus reducing the costs of hospitalisation.

Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) The National Health and Medical Research Council
(N.H. and M.R.C.) is supporting a project at the Queen Elizabeth Hospital concerning research into the treatment of cancer by phototherapy, in which a laser coupled to an optical fibre is used as one light source. The project is being undertaken by Drs Jacka, Blake and Forbes, with funding of $13,605 in 1982 and a commitment for 1983, and is entitled
'Physical principles of dosimetry for cancer phototherapy with haematoporphyrin derivative (HPD).'

The project forms part of an interdisciplinary team approach to the development of an alternative non-intrusive
treatment for malignant tumours. The N.H. and M.R.C. has also supported another aspect of this research, which involves
the separation and identification of components of HPD. This work is being undertaken at Adelaide University by Drs Ward and Forbes. Details of funding are:

1980-$19,538 for project entitled 'Chemistry of photoradiation therapy of malignant tumours'

Answers to Questions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2015

1982-$24,152 for 'Chemistry of cancer phototherapy of HPD'.
(2) The development of phototherapy is at a relatively early stage, and the fundamental mechanism by which it may kill tumour cells is not fully understood and its therapeutic spectrum is unknown. It is therefore premature to estimate the commercial potential of the research.
(3) The grants were provided at the level recommended by the N.H. and M.R.C. after an extensive peer review examination. It is understood, however, that the research is supported by significant additional funds from private sources.
(4) The grant to Drs Ward and Forbes, which sought support for a Ph. D. graduate, was provided in 1980 and 1982 but not suported in 1981. The application as presented to the N.H. and M.R.C. for support in that year did not achieve the level of scientific merit required at the time.
(5) In awarding project grants, the N.H. and M.R.C. specifies a particular term of support. No guarantee is given of further funding of the project beyond that period. Applications for funding in future years are considered as new applications and assessed on the basis of scientific merit in open competition with all other applications. These rules are understood by applicants to the N.H. and M.R.C. However, I understand that notwithstanding the lack of N.H. and M.R.C. funds the project continued in 1981 with support from private sources.
(6) The nature of research is such that it is not possible to guarantee results prior to the completion of the work. The project certainly aims at providing a non-invasive alternative clinical treatment for cancer which, if proven, could provide an alternative to existing treatment procedures and may perhaps reduce hospital costs.

Mammary Prosthesis: Benefit Entitlement
(Question No. 4921)
Mr Maher asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
Does Medibank Private refuse to pay benefit entitlements on external mammary prosthesis more than once in a 36 month period; if so, why is a benefit entitlement not payable more often if a medical practitioner certifies an existing prosthesis to be causing ulceration, et cetera, due to a patient's loss of weight.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The approved rules of Medibank Private provide that ancillary benefits are not payable for the replacement of any prosthesis or appliance, including an external mammary prosthesis, if the previous supply for which benefits were paid occurred within the preceding 36 months. Medibank Private has advised that this applies irrespective of the reason for the replacement, except in the case of artificial eyes for children under 17 years of age.
The health insurance legislation is primarily designed to protect persons in the provision of basic medical and hospital benefits. There is no specified provision in the legislation relating to ancillary benefits. Health insurance funds are free to determine for themselves the nature and extent of the services for which ancillary benefits are payable.
Medibank Private has advised that this particular rule, and other limiting rules in the ancillary table, are necessary to ensure the table's viability and competitiveness with other funds' tables.
Citizenship: Adopted Alien Infant
(Question No. 4922)
Mr Maher asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
Must an application be made for Australian citizenship on behalf of an alien infant legally adopted by Australian citizens pursuant to an order of the Supreme Court of a State; if so, why.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Yes. The reason is that the Australian Citizenship Act 1948 makes no provision for the automatic acquisition of Australian citizenship by children on adoption. One of the proposed amendments to the Act, announced in the Parliament on 6 May 1982, provides for the automatic acquisition of Australian citizenship by a child, resident in and adopted in Australian by an Australian citizen, upon granting of an adoption order by a court.
Potato Growers: Effect of New Zealand Imports
(Question No. 4931)
Mr Mildren asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 24 July 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the continuing concern of potato growers in the central highlands region of Victoria that their economic livelihood is threatened by the current proposals for closer economic relations with New Zealand.

2016 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

(2) Has his attention also been drawn to the fact that the potato industry contrubutes some $16m annually to the economy of the central highlands region and provides direct employment for 1,300 people within that region.
(3) Can he say whether the New Zealand potato industry enjoys certain economic advantages over its Australian counterparts.
(4) Will he give an assurance to the central highland region potato growers that the Australian potato industry will not be disadvantaged as a result of any final trade agreement with New Zealand.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) The economics of potato growing in New Zealand, and in Australia, is influenced by many factors. For example, climatic and other resource endowment characteristics, production costs, inflation rates, currency exchange rates, transport and other marketing costs and Government assistance measures such as tariffs, quantitative import restrictions and export subsidies all have a bearing upon the relative competitiveness of the Australian and New Zealand potato industries. On balance it would seem that, in recent years the Australian industry has not been in a position of disadvantage relative to the New Zealand industry since the balance of trade across the Tasman in potato products has been in Australia's favour.
(4) When negotiating the draft CER proposals, which were released early in June as a basis for consultation with industry, considerable emphasis was given to developing procedures which would ensure that undue disruption or hardship for particular industries would be avoided. The interests of the Australian potato industry were kept very much in mind during the negotiations and led to the development of a proposal for the accelerated phasing out of New Zealand's potato export subsidies matched by a phasing out of Australian and New Zealand potato tariffs (over the same period and in the same proportional steps).
In acknowledgement of the concerns expressed by the Australian industry, including Victorian growers, the Bureau of Agricultural Ecomonics (BAE) was asked to undertake a detailed examination of the likely arrangements. The BAE concluded that the relative competitive position of the Australian and New Zealand potato industries will be virtually unaffected by the proposed liberalisation measures:
In addition to the specific arrangements for potatoes other aspects of the draft CER package include the establishment of special liaison arrangements bet ween the Government and the horticultural processing-growing industry to monitor imports and to facilitate prompt action should a legitimate complaint arise under Australia's anti-dumping legislation. More generally, the proposed CER agreement provides special measures to safeguard industries from severe material injury or demonstrable threat thereof during the transition to free trade.
Answering of Questions on Notice: Minister for Trade and Resources
(Question No. 4933)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
( I) What are the details of the Minister's arrangements for the answering of questions on notice, specifying what officers

(a) on the Minister's personal staff and (b) within the Minister's Department, are responsible for (i) examining the question asked and (ii) preparing the answers.
(2) How many questions on notice from senators and members has the Minister been asked from 25 November 1980 to date.
(3) What is the average delay incurred between the time of questions being placed on notice and the provision of the answer.
(4) For each question referred to in part (2), how many have taken (a) 1-3 months, (b) 3-6 months, (c) 6-9 months, (d) 9-12 months and (e) over 12 months, to be answered.
(5) How many of these questions are currently still on notice, and what is the reason for delay in the case of each question which has been on the Notice Paper of either House for 12 months or more.
Mr Anthony~- The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Minister's staff and the Parliamentary Questions Officer in the Department are responsible for scanning the Notice Papers for questions directed to the Minister.
These questions are then forwarded to the appropriate Divisional Head in the Department for preparation of a draft reply.
The draft reply with the appropriate executive approvals is submitted to the Minister for his approval.
Once approved the reply is returned to the Department's Parliamentary Questions Officer who arranges for the appropriate number of copies to be forwarded to the Table Office via the Minister's Senior Private Secretary.
(2) 169 questions were placed on the Notice Papers from 25 November 1980 to date by senators and members.
(3) 73 days.
(4) (a) 72; (b) 27; (c) 16; (d) nil; (e) nil, 37 questions have taken less than one month to answer.
(5) 17, none of which has been on the Notice Paper for 12 months or more.

Diesel Fuel Excise Exemption
(Question No. 5040)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 9 September 1982:
(I) Did the Government change the diesel fuel excise exemption for primary producers to a rebate in the 1982-83 Budget; if so, (a) how will primary producers obtain this rebate and (b) will this rebate be payable to the rail authorities.
(2) If the rebate is not payable to rail authorities, how much will this diesel fuel excise on rail freight increase freight costs to primary producers per tonne of grain transported by rail.
(3) Is he able to say how much grain transport will be transferred to road transport by this excise.
(4) How much extra income will the Government earn from the grain industry through the diesel fuel excise on rail freight (a) in the current drought year and (b) in a normal year.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. (a) Primary producers eligible for the rebate should address their claims to the Department of Industry and Commerce which administers the scheme. The Department has issued an information bulletin which is available from customs officers, oil companies, their agencies and distributors. (b) No.
(2) Any changes in rail freights for the transport of grain resulting from the diesel fuel excise will be a matter for State rail authorities. No information is currently available on this matter.
(3) The substitution effects, if any, of the excise between road and rail transport of grains are not known.
(4) No information is currently available on which to base a reliable estimate of the likely Government revenue from this source.

Animal Diseases in Middle East Ports
(Question No. 5090)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 15 September 1982:
(I) Was it stated in the report of the Australian Sheep Meat Study Mission to the Middle East, dated May 1982, that the mission was concerned to learn of outbreaks of highly contagious animal diseases in the Middle East region.

Answers to Questions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2021

(2) Has his attention been drawn to outbreaks of highly contagious animal diseases in some Middle Eastern ports to which Australia ships live sheep.
(3) Is he able to say (a) how many outbreaks of these diseases have occurred and (b) what action is taken by the authorities in the countries concerned to combat these diseases.
(4) What action is being taken to ensure that exotic diseases are not brought into Australia via vessels trading with the Middle East.
(5) Does the Government intend to allow continued trading in livestock with countries in which there are regular outbreaks of highly contagious animal diseases; if so, what are the risks to Australia's livestock industries.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Report of the Australian Sheep Meat Study Mission to the Middle East in which the representatives of the Australasian Meat Industry Employees' Union voiced concern has been noted. The problem of exotic disease outbreaks was reported to me late in 1980 in the report of an Australian veterinary officer who visited the Middle East, and on occasions since then when an outbreak of Contagious Bovine Pleuropneumonia was alleged to have occurred in Australian cattle. Disease status in the region is assessed on the basis of the Animal Health Yearbook and the regular reports of the Office International des Epizooties. However critical veterinary reports on specific outbreaks of exotic animal dieases at the livestock collection points at Middle East ports are not available routinely.
(3) (a) Outbreaks of specific diseases are reported by member countries to the Office International des Epizooties. Not all countries are members of the OlE, and the efficiency of reporting varies from country to country. The animal Health Yearbook produced by FAO/WHO/OIE gives an annual summary of the world situation on animal disease; (b) No. There are internationally agreed general principles for action to contain an exotic disease but detailed information on any outbreaks near ports is not available.
(I) and (2)-

(4) Returning vessels are cleansed and then inspected by quarantine personnel before berthing. In some cases the vessels are disinfected. Policies are determined by the Department of Health. My Department has requested that quarantine arrangements for livestock vessels be reviewed at the Chief Quarantine Officers (Animals) Conference, which will be convened by the Department of Health in November 1982. I am concerned that, while quarantine requirements are generally adequate, problems can arise if the vessel is unexpectedly delayed in port while partly or fully loaded.
(5) Yes. Quarantine risks can be eliminated by appropriate, adequate treatment of the vessel before arrival in Australia. My Department is examining ways in which to improve the flow of technical information on animal disease and welfare from regions such as the Middle East.

Air Training Corps: Issue of New Uniform
(Question No. 5095)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 September 1982:
(I) Have the 12 and 15 Flights, Western Australia Squadron of the Air Training Corps, been issued with the new allseason uniform that has been on issue in the eastern States for some time; if not, why not and when will the uniforms be issued.
(2) Have some cadets who joined these Flights in February 1982 still not been issued with uniforms; if so, why.
(3) When will arrangements be made to admit female cadets into the Air Training Corps as has already happened with the Army and Navy cadet system.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Nos 12 and 15 Flights Western Australian Squadron Air Training Corps, have not been issued with the new allseasons uniform. The Royal Australian Air Force is in the process of issuing the newall-seasons uniform to members of the Air Training Corps on a state-by-state basis with Western Australia and North Queensland being issued last because of their temperate climates. The newall-seasons uniform will be made available to Western Australian Squadron Air Training Corps by the end of 1982.
(2) There are some cadets who joined these Flights in February 1982 who still have not been issued with a uniform. The reason is that these particular cadets' sizes are not available in the old uniforms. They will be issued with the new uniform at the end of 1982.
(3) On 6 May 1982 the Minister for Defence announced that female cadets would be admitted to the Australian Cadet Forces Scheme. The Air Force on 12 May 1982 authorised female enrolments in the Air Training Corps and many female cadets are now enrolled in the Air Training Corps.
Anti-missile Decoy on Australian Ships
(Question No. 5112)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 21 September 1982:
(I) What progress has been made with Project Winnin.
(2) Has it reached a point where (a) deployment of the anti-missile decoy on Australian warships can be considered and (b) a time for it being brought into service can be estimated.
(3) What investigation has been undertaken of potential markets for the decoy.
(4) Have approaches been made to potential purchasers in the light of experience in the Falkland Islands.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) An experimental WINNIN vehicle has undergone a number of successful trials to demonstrate its feasibility.

(2) The RAN fully support the WINNIN development, and envisage the installation of WINNIN on RAN ships. Inservice time cannot be established until the development program has been defined.
(3) Several countries have expressed interest in the WINNIN concept, and a Defence/Defence Support team will shortly leave for overseas to investigate these interests further, including collaborative possibilities.
(4) There is little doubt that the Falkland Islands conflict has enhanced the interest mentioned in (3) above.
Government Aircraft Factory, Melbourne: Tender for Supply of Autoclaves
(Question No. 5116)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence Support, upon notice, on 21 September 1982:
(I) Was the instrument and control equipment offered in the tender for the supply of autoclaves for the Government Aircraft Factories, Melbourne, by Thermal Equipment of Los Angeles different to that offered by other tenderers; ifso, how.
(2) Has the tender been accepted.
(3) Was the equipment of the Leeds and Northrop type mentioned in the specifications for the tenders, if not and the tender has been accepted, why.
(4) Was his Department prepared to accept types of equipment other than those specified, if so, were all tenderers made aware of this and were they all made aware at the same time.
(5) Was the price differential between the Australian based tenders and Thermal Equipment's tender the result of different instrument and control requirements being placed on the Australian suppliers to the advantage of the overseas suppliers.
(6) Were the technical requirements for Australian based suppliers higher, and consequently more expensive, than those required of overseas suppliers.
(7) Has any opportunity been afforded the Australian suppliers to tender on the same specifications as that of the overseas suppliers.
(8) Is he able to say what would be the estimated loss of employment in Australia as a result of a Government decision to accept an overseas tender.
Mr Viner-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The instrument and control equipment required and specified in the tender for autoclaves for the Government Aircraft Factories covered three systems:
A temperature and pressure controller for a manual mode of operation and the required equipment was 'Leeds and Northrop'.
Data Logging equipment for a manual mode of operation and the required equipment was 'Leeds and Northrop'.
Master Central control system equipment for an automatic mode of operation and the tender specified 'Leeds and Northrop' or similar equipment.
Thermal Equipment Corporation of Los Angeles in their tender response offered the same 'Leeds and Northrop' equipment for manual mode of operation controllers and data logging equipment as offered by other tenderers. The company also offered 'Leeds and Northrop' equipment and two other similar equipment options for the automatic mode of operation.
One of the options for the automatic mode of operation was selected as it was less expensive than the 'Leeds and Northrop'

Answers to Questions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2023

equipment and fully met the tender requirement. No other tenderer offered this equipment.
(2) Yes.
(3) 'Leeds and Northrop' equipment was mentioned in the specification for the tender.
(4) 'Leeds and Northrop' control equipment was specified in the tender as mandatory for the manual mode of operation and my Department was not prepared to accept other equipment. Tenderers were given the option of specifying either a 'Leeds and Northrop' controller or similar equipment for the automatic mode of operation, and all were made aware of this at the same time.
(5) No. Tender requirements for the instrumentation and control elements of the specification were the same for Australian and overseas based suppliers.
(6) No. Technical requirements for the autoclaves were established by the tender specification, and responses from all suppliers, whether of local or overseas origin were treated equally.
(7) Yes. The Australian and overseas suppliers quoted against the same specification of requirements.
(8) While the net effect on employment in Australia as the result of awarding this contract to an overseas supplier as compared with awarding it to an Australian tenderer is not known, it should be noted that Thermal Equipment Corporation have joined with Wessberg Martin Pty Ltd of Sydney to co-produce the equipment, and that the Australian content will be approximately 34 per cent of the total value of the contract.
Commonwealth Cars: Use by Ministers
(Question No. 5131)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 22 September 1982:
In what specific circumstances are Commonwealth cars made available for Ministers to visit inmates of prisons.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
It has been a long established practice, approved by Government, that Ministers have unrestricted use of a Commonwealth car and driver. Therefore, a Commonwealth car is made available on request for use at a Minister's discretion.

Taxation Office: Provision of Information to Government Department
(Question No. 3916)
Mr Free asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 17 February 1982:
Has information from individual taxpayers' returns been supplied by the Taxation Office to the Department of Education for the purpose of checking adjusted family incomes to determine entitlements under the Secondary Allowance Scheme and the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme; if so:
(a) what procedures exist for the receipt and approval of such req uests;
(b) when did the Taxation Office begin to supply such information; and
(c) under what circumstances is information on individual taxpayers supplied by the Taxation Office to other Government departments.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Under section 16 (4) (i) of the Income Tax Assessment Act the Commissioner of Taxation and his officers are authorised to supply information in specified circumstances to the Secretary, Department of Education or the Commonwealth Scholarships Board for the purpose of the administration of any law of the Commonwealth relating to financial assistance to students:
(a) Any information supplied is in response to a specific request made by an authorised officer of the Department of Education to a Deputy Commissioner of Taxation and is confined to specific matters related to data supplied by a person in an application for a secondary allowance or for tertiary education assistance. The Taxation Office ensures that information is only supplied within the terms of the income tax law and that the authorised persons receiving the information are aware of their responsibilities under the secrecy provisions of the Income Tax Assessment Act. Under no circumstances is any person given access to a taxation return.
(b) The Taxation Office has supplied information to the Department of Education in relation to the Secondary Allowance Scheme and Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme since 1973-74. Before that, however, information relating to certain scholarship holders had been supplied for many years, firstly to the Universities Commission and since 1960 to the Commonwealth Scholarship Board.
(e) In providing for the proper administration of certain Commonwealth funded programs Parliament has thought it appropriate to authorise the Commissioner of Taxation to release certain information relating to a

2024 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

person's financial affairs to a small number of Commonwealth organisations and departments, the authority being contained in sec. 16 (4) of the Income Tax Assessment Act.
Sales Tax: Commonwealth Games
(Question No. 3929)
Mr Humphreys asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 17 February 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that the Commonwealth Games Flag is a symbol of a group of nations, not just an event, and is therefore similar in type to the United Nations Flag.
(2) Is it also a fact that there is no other flag which symbolises the Commonwealth of Nations.
(3) Given that flags of all nations, states and the United Nations are sales tax exempt, why is the Commonwealth Games not exempt of the 17.5 per cent general sales tax.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Prior to the 1982-83 Budget the only flags that qualified for exemption from sales tax were those made of cloth, paper or plastic sheeting that were the emblems of nations, states or territories or of the United Nations.
However, the Government has decided to underline its support for the Commonwealth Games by exempting the official flag of the XII Commonwealth Games from sales tax. This is to be achieved by amending the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act to provide for exemption of the flag with effect from I January 1982. The Bill was introduced into the House on 17 August 1982.
Australian Ballet Foundation
(Question No. 4079)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 25 February 1982:
(I) Is it a fact that the answer to question No. 3657 (Hansard, 16 February 1982, page 187) treated the question requesting information about the Australian Ballet, its board and corps de ballet as if it had asked about the Australian Ballet Foundation and stated that it would not be appropriate for him to give the details requested.
(2) Is it also a fact that (a) the Commonwealth Government has paid to the Ballet Foundation, through the Australia Council, sums totalling $7 026 000 in the period 1977-82 inclusive, (b) the Foundation is in fact a holding company for the Australian Ballet and (c) the principals of fiscal accountability require that members of Parliament should be able to ascertain how public funds are spent.
(3) Has his attention been drawn to questions about the Australian Ballet asked and answered in Senate Estimates Committee meetings; if so, what is the justification for failing to provide answers to questions on notice which were answered when asked orally at a Senate Estimates Committee meeting.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) (a) I am advised by the Australia Council that in the period 1977-78 to 1981-82 an amount of $8 190 000 was paid to the Ballet Foundation through the Australia Council.

(b) In October 1970 the Australian Ballet Foundation was incorporated as a Company under the Victorian Companies Act limited by guarantee.
(c) Yes.
(3) Yes. I am informed that a number of questions dealing with the Australian Ballet Foundation were asked at the 1981 Senate Estimates Committee B. These related to a breakdown of performances by capital city, given by the full Company and those by a selection of dancers, for both 1981 and 1982. That question was placed on notice. Further questions related to the percentage of the Ballet's 1980 income from corporate sponsorship and private sector donations. Those questions were also placed on notice. Subsequently, as I am advised, answers to the questions were provided to Senate Estimates Committee B. The answers to these questions do not provide the details sought by the honourable member.
The names of the current members of the Board of the Australian Ballet Foundation were provided to the Senate Estimates Committee on 14 September 1982 by the Administrator, Australian Ballet Foundation. This was part of the information previously sought by the honourable member, and is recorded on page 508 of the Senate Hansard of 14 September 1982.
Money Supply
(Question No. 4165)
Dr Blewett asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on II March 1982:
(I) Has the Government's monetary policy since 1976 included limiting the rate of growth in the money supply.
(2) Does the Government's monetary policy include direction to the trading banks to limit their new lending.
(3) In formulating its monetary policy, does the Government consider the effect of that policy on commercial decision makers.
(4) Do controls on trading bank lending limit the extent to which trading banks compete with each other for lending opportunities; if so does this enable trading banks to increase their interest charges on loans and alter direction of their lending towards more profitable sectors.
(5) Does the Government's monetary policy include controlling the money supply which includes liabilities of the trading banks; if so, (a) do controls on trading bank liabilities reduce the amount trading banks can advance to borrowers and (b) does this have a similar effect to direct controls on trading bank lending.
(6) Has the monopoly power of trading banks increased since 1976; if so, is this a result of the Government's monetary policy strengthening the trading bank 'cartel'.
(7) Are the trading banks required to report their profits to the Reserve Bank; if so, are these published.
(8) What is the annual rate of growth in trading bank profits in each year since 1976.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) A central tenet of this Government's economic policies has always been the need to reduce inflation and inflationary expectations. A prerequisite to reducing the rate of inflation is to reduce the rate of growth of the money aggregates. In each Budget since 1976-77 the Government has announced a projected range for the money supply (M3) during the financial year.
(2) In the past, the Reserve Bank had on occasion given the major trading banks lending guidelines designed to help restrain the growth in bank lending to a degree consistent with

Answers to Questions

13 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2025

wider monetary policy objectives. The Reserve Bank announced on 29 June that it had withdrawn its earlier request to the trading banks to keep growth in their advances to not more than 12 per cent per annum.
(3) The Government is concerned with the effects of its policies on all groups within the community. Given that the Government's monetary policy is primarily aimed at the reduction of inflation and inflationary expectations the community overall stands to benefit from the Government's monetary policy stance.
(4) One of the undesirable aspects of the lending guidelines mentioned above was that they did, to a certain extent, tend to freeze each individual bank's share of total lending and thereby reduce competition between the banks. This is one of the reasons the Reserve Bank decided not to continue with the lending guidelines in 1982-83.
(5) As mentioned in the answer to part (I) the Government's monetary policy aims at slowing the growth of the monetary aggregates generally. The growth of these aggregates is influenced by, inter alia, the rate of growth of trading bank deposits. The Government does not have any direct controls on trading bank liabilities similar to the lending guidelines.
(6) I am not sure what the honourable member means by the 'monopoly power of the trading banks'. Neither am I sure how the Government's monetary policies, which have if anything borne more heavily on the banks than on other financial institutions, could strengthen the trading banks as a group.
(7) Section 52 of the Banking Act 1959 requires, inter alia, that a trading bank subject to the Act shall prepare and deliver to the Australian Statistician and the Reserve Bank a statement of profit and loss, in accordance with Form B in the Schedule to the Act, in respect of each year ending on a date prescribed in respect of the bank. From this and other information statutorily provided by the trading banks, the Australian Statistician is required under sub-section 58 (I) of the

Banking Act to prepare and publish in the Gazelle a statement showing, in respect of each trading bank, its liabilities, assets and profit and loss. The last such statement was published in Gazelle No. G 31 of 3 August 1982. The banks themselves publish profit statements in accordance with Companies Act and Stock Exchange listing requirements.
(8) Comparative analysis of bank profits is materially affected by the treatment of extraordinary items, intra-group dividends, determination of expenses and provisions, changes in corporate structure and ownership of subsidiaries, changes in accounting standards and other relevant factors.
Within the period covered by the question there have, in particular, been changes in disclosures practices by banks which have had significant effects on stated profits. This development followed my Press statement of 17 September 1978 of the Government's intention to amend the reporting forms prescribed under the Banking Act to bring the basis of reporting by banks closer to that of other companies. In particular, the balances of inner reserves, except for bad and doubtful debts, were in future to form part of shareholders' funds. These changes in disclosure had the effect of raising reported profits. Although these accounting adjustments were introduced at different times by the banks over their 1978 and 1979 reporting years, some impression of their significance can be obtained by comparing the reported profits for 1978 in the banks' 1978 annual reports with the profits reported for that year in the banks' 1979 annual reports.
Against that background it is apparent that a variety of calculations concerning profit growth could be made on the basis of different assumptions. The figures below show major trading bank profits as reported in the Commonwealth of Australia Gazelle. In assessing the figures I refer the honourable member to the respective Gazelles and to the banks' annual reports and accounts and relevant statements therein.

Taxation
(Question No. 4665)
Mrs Darling asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Is the Federal Government package of taxes progressive as opposed to the impositions of State and local government income tax.

l\Ir Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) Local governments have never imposed an income tax in Australia and State Governments have not imposed such a tax since the introduction of uniform taxation in 1942. Accordingly, it is not possible to compare Commonwealth taxes with State and local government income tax.

2026 REPRESENTATIVES 13 October 1982

Answers to Questions

Holsworthy Army Base, New South Wales: Noise Levels of Gunfire
(Question No. 4756)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) How many complaints have been received in each of the last 5 years regarding the noise level of gunfire at the artillery range at Holsworthy, N.S.W.
(2) How are these complaints treated.
(3) How many claims for compensation in respect of detonations at Holsworthy have been received.
(4) How many have been successful.
(5) What are the criteria used in assessing these claims.
(6) Are detonations occurring at the Holsworthy range capable of causing (a) nuisance distress to people and pets and (b) damage to foundations, cement rendering and bathroom and kitchen tiles of domestic residences.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 220 telephone complaints have been received over the past 5 years and although no firm figures are available, it is estimated that no more than 12 written complaints are received annually.
The following numbers of telephone calls were received:
(a) 1978-46;
(b) 1979-49;
(c) 1980-54; (d) 1981-13;
(e) 1982-58 (to 27 August 1982).
(2) Complaints are treated in two ways:
(a) Telephone Callers. Details of telephone complaints are recorded by Duty Officers. Callers are informed of the factors which contribute to the intensity of the noise.
(b) Written Complaints. Each is acknowledged. Advice is provided on the necessity for training activities and the actions taken by Army to minimise disturbance to residents consistent with meeting training objectives.
(3) Eight claims for compensation have been received in respect of detonations at Holsworthy.
(4) Four claims have been successful.
(5) Each claim for compensation for alleged damages is investigated by an officer who is a Civil Engineer, attached to HQ 2 Military District. Completed investigations are referred to the Deputy Crown Solicitor for determination.
(6) The effects of detonations emanating from the Holsworthy range are, as previously stated, being investigated by the National Acoustics Laboratory. An investigation conducted in 1979 by the Bureau of Mineral Resources suggested that:
'the low readings of ground and air vibrations measured at locations within the nearby civil communities indicated that any annoyance suffered as a result of weapons firing was, essentially, acoustical'.
Also, technical assessments indicate that it is highly improbable that range detonations could cause damage to foundations especially at the distance the civilian dwellings are from impact areas. Damage to cement rendering and bathroom and kitchen tiles of domestic residences are more likely due to the expansion and contraction characteristics of the soil in the area which has a very high clay content.

Holsworthy Army Base, New South Wales: Noise Levels of Gunfire
(Question No. 4760)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) What were the main features of the National Acoustics Laboratory report on the noise levels of firing at Holsworthy Army Base, N.S.W.
(2) What action (a) has been taken and (b) is contemplated following receipt of the report.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The National Acoustics Laboratory report on noise levels of firing at Holsworthy Army Base, N.S.W., has not yet been received.
(2) No action has (a) been taken and (b) is contemplated until the report is received.
Holsworthy Artillery Range, New South Wales: Firing of Explosive Charges
(Question No. 4761)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
Are there any restrictions placed on the firing of explosive charges at the Holsworthy Artillery Range, N.S.W.; if so, what restrictions are placed on their (a) frequency and (b) duration.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows.
Yes. Firing of explosive charges at the Holsworthy Range, N.S.W., is restricted to the following periods:
(a) Monday to Saturday, 0700 hours to 2100 hours. (b) Sundays and Public Holidays, 0900 hours to 1900
hours. Within these time frames the duration of firing is not restricted.
Holsworthy Artillery Range, New South Wales: Bush Fires
(Question No. 4762)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Is there any evidence to show that artillery fire at the Holsworthy Artillery Range, NSW, has caused bush fires (a) on the range and (b) in the surrounding countryside.
(2) If so, (a) when did each incident occur and (b) was there any damage to (i) Army or (ii) domestic property.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) There is evidence to indicate that artillery fire at the Holsworthy Artillery Range, NSW, may have caused bush fires on the range, and
(b) There is no evidence to suggest that artillery fire has caused bush fires in the surrounding countryside.
(2) (a) Fires started on the range, not necessarily in the impact area, on the following days when artillery were firing:
1978-16 August; 19 October; 30 November; 5, II, 13, 14, 15, 16, 19 December.
1979-22 February; 17 August; 27 September; 15 October.

1982-11 March; 4,13 May; 24, 30 June; 8 July.
(b) Only one fire has spread off the range. This fire occurred spontaneously and linked with other fires burning in the area. Artillery were not being exercised on this day. There are no reports of damage to army or domestic property.
Independent Audit: Department of Administrative Services
(Question No. 4787)
Mr Leo McLeay as-ked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:

What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
A condition of grants to international conferences held in Australia is that a statement of income and expenditure for the conference, audited by a qualified public accountant, should be submitted to the Department of Administrative Services as soon as possible after the conference.
Organisations in receipt of grants-in-aid are required to submit an audited financial statement in respect of the year in which a grant was made to the Department of Administrative Services.
